The Self-Evident Need for a Determinant
Razi presents and explains the first view that the need for a determinant is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need for a determinant (murajjih) is considered a self-evident premise in kalam theology.

Core Idea
The mind naturally demands a cause for every contingent event.

Imam al-Razi begins by establishing a foundational principle: that the contingent requires a determinant is not a conclusion reached through reasoning but an axiom ingrained in human intellect. This reflects the universal human instinct to seek causes for events. In this passage, he explains why this principle is self-evident and provides a concrete illustration.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the concise, logical flow from the general principle to the concrete example.
2
Understand the definition of 'self-evident premise'
Identify that Razi describes this premise as 'primary' (awwaliyya) and 'self-evident' (badihiyya), meaning it requires no proof.
3
Grasp the logical reasoning
Follow the argument: when existence and non-existence are equally possible, the mind judges that one cannot preponderate without a cause.
This is the core logical move.
4
Reflect on the universal human instinct
Consider the example: every rational person, when noticing a new event, instinctively asks for its cause. This shows the principle is innate.
5
Connect to the broader kalam framework
Understand that this self-evident premise underlies all arguments for the existence of God and the contingency of the world.
It is a cornerstone of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : أنها مقدمة أولية بدهية وذلك لأنه لما كانت نسبة الوجود ونسبة العدم إلى تلك الماهية على السوية قضى صريح العقل بأنه يمتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر لا لأجل انضياف أمر من الأمور إلى الطرف الراجح، ولذلك فإن كل عاقل إذا أحس بحدوث شيء في وقت معين طلب لحدوثه علة وسبباً، وذلك يدل على أن افتقار رجحان الجائز إلى المرجح أمر مقرر في بدائه العقول.
One of them: that it is a primary, self-evident premise, because when the relation of existence and non-existence to that essence is equal, the clear intellect judges that it is impossible for one of them to preponderate over the other without the addition of something to the preponderant side. Therefore, every rational person, when he perceives the occurrence of something at a specific time, seeks for its occurrence a cause and a reason. That indicates that the dependence of the preponderance of the contingent upon a determinant is a matter established in the primary intuitions of minds.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the first view regarding the principle that the contingent requires a determinant (al-murajjih). The author states that this premise is primary (awwaliyya) and self-evident (badihiyya). The reasoning is: when existence and non-existence are equally related to an essence, reason necessarily judges that one cannot preponderate without an additional factor tipping the scale. This is illustrated by the universal human tendency: when someone perceives an event occurring at a particular time, the mind instinctively asks for its cause. Thus, the dependence of preponderance on a determinant is an axiom ingrained in the primitive intellects.

Key Terms
مقدمة أولية
بدهية
راجح
مرجح
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle that the contingent requires a determinant is not self-evident but derived from philosophical reasoning or empirical observation.
Correction: Razi argues it is self-evident because the mind instinctively seeks a cause for any contingent occurrence, as shown by the universal human tendency to ask 'why' when something happens.

Checkpoint
According to the first view presented by Razi, why is the need for a determinant (murajjih) considered self-evident?
A) Because it is proven by logical demonstration.
B) Because the mind naturally seeks a cause for any contingent occurrence.
C) Because it is derived from scripture.
D) Because the philosophers agreed on it.

Key Takeaway
The need for a determinant is a self-evident axiom because the human mind instinctively demands a cause for any contingent event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a crisis, do you instinctively search for a secondary cause (a person, a circumstance) or do you immediately turn to the ultimate Cause, Allah? What does your reaction reveal about the state of your heart?
?
If the need for a determinant is stamped onto your very intellect, why do you sometimes live as if events happen by chance, ignoring the hand of the One who tips the scale? How can you train your mind to see Him in every occurrence?
?
Consider one blessing you received today—did you attribute it to your own effort or to the Determiner? What would change if you consciously traced every good back to its true Source, and how would that affect your gratitude?

Critique of the Contradiction Argument for a Determinant
Razi presents and critiques the contradiction-based argument for a determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi considers the contradiction-based argument for a determinant insufficient to prove the premise is demonstrative.

Core Idea
Razi exposes a flaw: the contradiction only holds if the essence's equality precludes uncaused preponderance.

Imam Razi now examines the second view, which claims that the premise 'every possible needs a determinant' is demonstrative. The strongest formulation uses the contradiction between equality and preponderance. Razi finds this argument weak and explains why. In this lesson, we'll follow his logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument from contradiction
Read the passage to see how proponents argue that equality and preponderance cannot coexist, so a determinant must tip the scale.
Notice the key terms: استواء (equality) and رجحان (preponderance).
2
Identify Razi's objection
Razi says the contradiction only follows if the essence necessitates equality in a way that precludes any preponderance. But if preponderance occurs without any cause, no contradiction arises.
The phrase 'لا لعلة البتة' (without any cause at all) is crucial.
3
Reflect on the significance
This shows that the premise is not demonstrative; a possible could in principle tip without a determinant, but that is absurd in practice. The argument fails to be decisive.
Razi does not reject the need for a determinant; he only weakens this particular proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : أن هذه المقدمة برهانية، وأحسن ما قيل في هذا المقام أن الاستواء والرجحان متناقضان متنافيان ولما ثبت أن ماهية الممكن مقتضية للاستواء، فلو حصل الرجحان أيضًا لاجتمع النقيضان، وهو محال. وعندي أن هذه الحجة ضعيفة لأن التناقض إنما يلزم لو كانت الماهية مقتضية للاستواء أو مقتضية للرجحان أيضاً، أما من يقول الماهية مقتضية للاستواء وأما الرجحان فإنه حصل لا لعلة البتة لا لذاته ولا لغيره لم يلزم التناقض على هذا القول.
The second view: that this premise is demonstrative. The best that has been said in this context is that equality and preponderance are contradictory and mutually exclusive. Since it has been established that the essence of the possible necessitates equality, then if preponderance also occurs, the two contradictories would combine, which is impossible. In my view, this argument is weak, because the contradiction would only follow if the essence necessitates equality or also necessitates preponderance. As for one who says that the essence necessitates equality, but as for preponderance, it occurs without any cause at all—not from itself nor from another—contradiction does not follow according to this view.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second view, that the premise is demonstrative (burhāniyya). The best formulation given is based on the contradiction between equality (istiwā') and preponderance (rujḥān). If the essence of the possible necessitates equality, then preponderance would bring together two contradictories, which is impossible. However, Imam Razi immediately critiques this argument as weak. He explains that the contradiction would only occur if the essence necessitates equality in a way that excludes preponderance, or if it also necessitates preponderance. But if one maintains that the essence only necessitates equality, while preponderance happens without any cause at all—not from itself nor from another—then there is no contradiction. Thus, the argument fails because it does not preclude the possibility of random preponderance.

Key Terms
برهانية
استواء
رجحان
تناقض
مقتضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi rejects the need for a determinant altogether.
Correction: Razi only rejects this particular proof as weak; he does not deny the need for a determinant. He still holds the premise as probable, not demonstrative.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the second view's argument (based on contradiction) weak?
A) Because the essence is not necessitating equality.
B) Because the contradiction only follows if the essence necessitates both equality and preponderance, but one could say preponderance occurs without a cause.
C) Because equality and preponderance are not contradictory.
D) Because the premise is not demonstrative at all.

Key Takeaway
Razi rejects the contradiction argument as weak because it does not rule out the possibility of uncaused preponderance, so the premise remains probable, not demonstrative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you faced two equal options and felt stuck. Did you truly believe Allah could tip the scale without any worldly cause, or did you demand a tangible reason? Razi shows that uncaused preponderance is logically possible. Will you now more confidently trust His direct guidance, even when no cause is visible?
?
Do you ever insist on logical necessity from Allah's decrees—demanding to see a reason for your trials? Razi's critique humbles our rational expectations: some truths are not demonstrative but accepted through revelation. Can you submit to His will even when no cause is apparent, trusting that His preponderance is sufficient?
?
The proponents' argument felt airtight until Razi exposed its weakness. Are there beliefs in your life you hold as 'certain' that might only be probable? Will you have the courage to examine them with the same intellectual honesty, and find peace in probabilities grounded in faith rather than logical demonstration?

The Stronger Argument: Contingent Requires an Efficient Cause
Razi states his stronger argument for the need of an efficient cause for every contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every contingent thing requires an efficient cause, based on the need for a preponderating factor that cannot arise from its own existence.

Core Idea
Existence requires a pre-existing preponderance; that preponderance must come from an efficient cause.

Imam Razi presents what he calls the 'stronger argument' for the existence of an efficient cause. He begins with a precise definition of the possible (al-mumkin) and shows why it cannot bring itself into existence without an external factor that tips the balance. This argument is foundational for proving the need for a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Define the possible
Read Razi's definition: the possible is that whose existence and non-existence are equal. Understand that such a thing cannot come into existence without a preponderating factor.
Equality means no inherent inclination toward existence.
2
Identify the need for preponderance
Notice that existence requires preponderance of existence over non-existence. This preponderance must be prior to existence itself.
3
Avoid circularity
Razi argues that the preponderance cannot be an attribute of the thing's own existence (otherwise circularity). Therefore it must be from something else – the efficient cause.
Circularity would mean the preponderance is both prior and posterior to existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقوى أن يقال : الممكن هو الذي يكون العدم والوجود بالنسبة إليه على التساوي والشيء الذي يكون كذلك امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا أن يصير وجوده راجحًا على عدمه، وذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة لشيء آخر سابق على وجوده، فيمتنع أن يكون محل ذلك الرجحان هو وجوده؛ لأن ذلك الرجحان لو كان صفة لوجوده لكان متأخرًا عن وجوده لكنا قد بينا أنه متقدم على وجوده، فيحصل الدور، وهو محال، فإذن ذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة لشيء آخر يلزم من وجوده، وذلك هو المؤثر، فثبت أن كل ممكن فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر. ويمكن تقرير هذه المقدمة بناء على قول الفلاسفة من وجه آخر : وذلك أنهم قالوا : الزمان يمتنع أن يكون له مبدأ ومنتهى لأنا لو فرضنا للزمان أولا لكان عدمه سابقاً على وجوده بالزمان والسبق بالزمان لا يحصل إلا مع وجود الزمان فيلزم أن يقال : الزمان كان موجوداً قبل أن كان موجوداً، وذلك محال. وهكذا لو فرضنا للزمان منتهى لكان عدمه حاصلا بعد وجوده، بعدية بالزمان والبعدية بالزمان لا تحصل إلا مع وجود الزمان فيلزم أن يقال: الزمان موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً، وذلك محال، فثبت أن عدم الزمان محال. وإذا ثبت هذا قالوا : الزمان إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته، والأول باطل ؛ لأنه مركب من آنات متوالية منقضية وكل ما كان كذلك لم يكن واجبًا لذاته، فهو إذن ممكن لذاته والممكن إما أن يقع لغيره أو لذاته، أو لا لغيره ولا لذاته، فإن كان لغيره ثبت افتقاره إلى المؤثر، وإن كان لذاته وجب أن يكون منقضياً، بل يكون باقيًا مستمرا، وهو محال، وإن كان لا لذاته ولا لغيره كان حدوثه اتفاقياً، وكل ما كان اتفاقيًا لم يمتنع في العقل أن لا يوجد، ولكنا قد بينا أن العدم على الزمان محال، ولما بطل القسمان ثبت القسم الأول وهو أنه ممكن لذاته إلا أنه واجب الوجود لسببه ومؤثره، فلأجل وجوبه لوجوب سببه يمتنع العدم عليه. وعند هذا يظهر أن دلالة وجود الزمان على وجود واجب الوجود، أظهر من دلالة جميع الممكنات ولعله هو المراد من قوله : لا تسبوا الدهر فإن الله الدهر، هذا ما يمكن تقريره بناء على أصول القوم.
The stronger [argument] is to say: the possible is that with respect to which non-existence and existence are equal. A thing that is so is prevented from entering into existence unless its existence becomes preponderant over its non-existence. That preponderance must be an attribute of something else prior to its existence. It is impossible for the locus of that preponderance to be its existence, because if that preponderance were an attribute of its existence, it would be posterior to its existence, while we have shown that it is prior to its existence—thus circularity occurs, which is impossible. Therefore, that preponderance must be an attribute of something else from whose existence it follows, and that is the efficient cause. Thus, it is established that every possible is in need of an efficient cause. This premise can also be formulated based on the philosophers' view in another way: they said that time cannot have a beginning or an end, because if we assume a beginning for time, its non-existence would be prior to its existence in time, but temporal priority only occurs with the existence of time, so it would follow that time existed before it existed—impossible. Similarly, if we assume an end for time, its non-existence would occur after its existence, temporal posteriority only occurs with the existence of time, so it would follow that time exists after it did not exist—impossible. Thus, it is established that the non-existence of time is impossible. When this is established, they say: time is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. The first is false because it is composed of successive transient moments, and everything that is such is not necessary in itself. Therefore, it is possible in itself. The possible is either [such that its existence] occurs from another, or from itself, or neither from another nor from itself. If it is from another, its dependence on an efficient cause is established; if it is from itself, it would have to be transient—rather, it would be permanent and continual—impossible; if it is neither from itself nor from another, its occurrence would be accidental, and everything accidental is not impossible in the intellect to not exist, but we have shown that non-existence of time is impossible. Since the two sections are invalid, the first section is established: that it is possible in itself but necessary in existence due to its cause and efficient cause. Because of its necessity due to the necessity of its cause, non-existence is impossible for it. At this point, it becomes clear that the evidence of the existence of time for the existence of the Necessary Existent is more apparent than the evidence of all contingent things. Perhaps this is what is meant by His saying: 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time.' This is what can be formulated based on their principles.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, a stronger argument for the need of an efficient cause (mu'aththir) for any contingent; second, a reformulation based on the philosophers' discussion of time. In the first part, Razi defines the possible as that which is equally related to existence and non-existence. Such a thing cannot come into existence unless its existence outweighs non-existence. This preponderance must be an attribute of something else preceding its existence; it cannot be an attribute of its own existence, as that would cause circularity. Hence, the preponderance must belong to another thing from whose existence the preponderance follows—that is the efficient cause. Thus, every possible depends on an efficient cause. The second part uses the example of time. The philosophers argue that time cannot have a beginning or end, else contradictions arise (e.g., time existing before itself). Therefore, the non-existence of time is impossible. Since time is composite and transient, it cannot be necessary in itself; it must be possible. As a possible, it must either be caused by another, or be self-caused, or be accidental. Self-causation is impossible (would imply permanence, but time is transient), and accidental occurrence would allow the possibility of non-existence, which has been shown impossible. Therefore, time is caused by another—the Necessary Existent. Razi notes that this argument shows time is a stronger indication of God than other contingent things, and he references a hadith: 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time.'

Key Terms
ممكن
راجح
مرجح
دور
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the preponderance (rujhan) that brings a contingent into existence not be an attribute of its own existence?
A) Because its existence is not yet present.
B) Because that would cause circularity: the preponderance is prior to existence but would be posterior if it were an attribute of existence.
C) Because the contingent has no attributes.
D) Because preponderance is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
Every possible thing requires an efficient cause because the preponderance needed for its existence cannot come from itself without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that your existence cannot come from yourself without absurdity. In what area of your life are you most tempted to believe you are self-sufficient, and how does this argument expose that as an illusion?
?
If every possible thing depends on an efficient cause, then your very existence is a gift from God. Does your daily posture reflect someone who is contingent and dependent, or someone who acts as if they are the source of their own success? What would it look like to live today conscious of that dependence?
?
The argument shows that preponderance must come from something prior. When you feel spiritually stagnant, do you seek the external Cause to preponderate faith over doubt in your heart, or do you rely on your own efforts? What is one concrete step to invite that preponderance today?

The Example of Time: A Striking Proof for the Necessary Existent
Razi reformulates the argument using the philosophers' example of time to illustrate the need for an efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' argument that time has no beginning or end leads to the conclusion that time is contingent and must be caused by the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Time cannot begin or end, so it must be caused by an eternal Necessary Existent.

Razi now reinforces the previous argument using a thought-provoking example: time. He explores the philosophers' view that time is without beginning or end, and shows that this very fact forces us to recognize time as contingent and dependent on an eternal cause. This argument is considered even more evident than other proofs from contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the paradox of time's beginning and end
Read the argument that time cannot have a first moment (or last) because that would imply time existed before itself—a contradiction.
The reasoning uses the concept of temporal priority and posteriority.
2
Determine whether time is necessary or possible
Since time is composed of transient moments, it cannot be necessary in itself; it must be possible.
3
Examine the three possibilities for the possible
A possible thing can be caused by another, self-caused, or occur accidentally. Self-causation is impossible (time would then be permanent, but it is transient). Accidental occurrence would allow non-existence, but we proved non-existence of time is impossible. Thus time must be caused by another.
4
Conclude that time points to the Necessary Existent
Time is possible in itself but necessary through its cause, which must be the Necessary Existent. This shows that time is an even stronger evidence for God than other contingent things.
Razi references the hadith: 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقوى أن يقال : الممكن هو الذي يكون العدم والوجود بالنسبة إليه على التساوي والشيء الذي يكون كذلك امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا أن يصير وجوده راجحًا على عدمه، وذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة لشيء آخر سابق على وجوده، فيمتنع أن يكون محل ذلك الرجحان هو وجوده؛ لأن ذلك الرجحان لو كان صفة لوجوده لكان متأخرًا عن وجوده لكنا قد بينا أنه متقدم على وجوده، فيحصل الدور، وهو محال، فإذن ذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة لشيء آخر يلزم من وجوده، وذلك هو المؤثر، فثبت أن كل ممكن فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر. ويمكن تقرير هذه المقدمة بناء على قول الفلاسفة من وجه آخر : وذلك أنهم قالوا : الزمان يمتنع أن يكون له مبدأ ومنتهى لأنا لو فرضنا للزمان أولا لكان عدمه سابقاً على وجوده بالزمان والسبق بالزمان لا يحصل إلا مع وجود الزمان فيلزم أن يقال : الزمان كان موجوداً قبل أن كان موجوداً، وذلك محال. وهكذا لو فرضنا للزمان منتهى لكان عدمه حاصلا بعد وجوده، بعدية بالزمان والبعدية بالزمان لا تحصل إلا مع وجود الزمان فيلزم أن يقال: الزمان موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً، وذلك محال، فثبت أن عدم الزمان محال. وإذا ثبت هذا قالوا : الزمان إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته، والأول باطل ؛ لأنه مركب من آنات متوالية منقضية وكل ما كان كذلك لم يكن واجبًا لذاته، فهو إذن ممكن لذاته والممكن إما أن يقع لغيره أو لذاته، أو لا لغيره ولا لذاته، فإن كان لغيره ثبت افتقاره إلى المؤثر، وإن كان لذاته وجب أن يكون منقضياً، بل يكون باقيًا مستمرا، وهو محال، وإن كان لا لذاته ولا لغيره كان حدوثه اتفاقياً، وكل ما كان اتفاقيًا لم يمتنع في العقل أن لا يوجد، ولكنا قد بينا أن العدم على الزمان محال، ولما بطل القسمان ثبت القسم الأول وهو أنه ممكن لذاته إلا أنه واجب الوجود لسببه ومؤثره، فلأجل وجوبه لوجوب سببه يمتنع العدم عليه. وعند هذا يظهر أن دلالة وجود الزمان على وجود واجب الوجود، أظهر من دلالة جميع الممكنات ولعله هو المراد من قوله : لا تسبوا الدهر فإن الله الدهر، هذا ما يمكن تقريره بناء على أصول القوم.
The stronger [argument] is to say: the possible is that with respect to which non-existence and existence are equal. A thing that is so is prevented from entering into existence unless its existence becomes preponderant over its non-existence. That preponderance must be an attribute of something else prior to its existence. It is impossible for the locus of that preponderance to be its existence, because if that preponderance were an attribute of its existence, it would be posterior to its existence, while we have shown that it is prior to its existence—thus circularity occurs, which is impossible. Therefore, that preponderance must be an attribute of something else from whose existence it follows, and that is the efficient cause. Thus, it is established that every possible is in need of an efficient cause. This premise can also be formulated based on the philosophers' view in another way: they said that time cannot have a beginning or an end, because if we assume a beginning for time, its non-existence would be prior to its existence in time, but temporal priority only occurs with the existence of time, so it would follow that time existed before it existed—impossible. Similarly, if we assume an end for time, its non-existence would occur after its existence, temporal posteriority only occurs with the existence of time, so it would follow that time exists after it did not exist—impossible. Thus, it is established that the non-existence of time is impossible. When this is established, they say: time is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. The first is false because it is composed of successive transient moments, and everything that is such is not necessary in itself. Therefore, it is possible in itself. The possible is either [such that its existence] occurs from another, or from itself, or neither from another nor from itself. If it is from another, its dependence on an efficient cause is established; if it is from itself, it would have to be transient—rather, it would be permanent and continual—impossible; if it is neither from itself nor from another, its occurrence would be accidental, and everything accidental is not impossible in the intellect to not exist, but we have shown that non-existence of time is impossible. Since the two sections are invalid, the first section is established: that it is possible in itself but necessary in existence due to its cause and efficient cause. Because of its necessity due to the necessity of its cause, non-existence is impossible for it. At this point, it becomes clear that the evidence of the existence of time for the existence of the Necessary Existent is more apparent than the evidence of all contingent things. Perhaps this is what is meant by His saying: 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time.' This is what can be formulated based on their principles.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, a stronger argument for the need of an efficient cause (mu'aththir) for any contingent; second, a reformulation based on the philosophers' discussion of time. In the first part, Razi defines the possible as that which is equally related to existence and non-existence. Such a thing cannot come into existence unless its existence outweighs non-existence. This preponderance must be an attribute of something else preceding its existence; it cannot be an attribute of its own existence, as that would cause circularity. Hence, the preponderance must belong to another thing from whose existence the preponderance follows—that is the efficient cause. Thus, every possible depends on an efficient cause. The second part uses the example of time. The philosophers argue that time cannot have a beginning or end, else contradictions arise (e.g., time existing before itself). Therefore, the non-existence of time is impossible. Since time is composite and transient, it cannot be necessary in itself; it must be possible. As a possible, it must either be caused by another, or be self-caused, or be accidental. Self-causation is impossible (would imply permanence, but time is transient), and accidental occurrence would allow the possibility of non-existence, which has been shown impossible. Therefore, time is caused by another—the Necessary Existent. Razi notes that this argument shows time is a stronger indication of God than other contingent things, and he references a hadith: 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time.'

Key Terms
زمان
واجب الوجود
ممكن
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's time argument, which of the following possibilities for time (as a possible being) is actually true?
A) Time is self-caused (exists from itself).
B) Time is accidental (occurs without cause).
C) Time is caused by another (an efficient cause).
D) Time is necessary in itself.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that time has no beginning or end forces us to conclude that time is contingent and caused by the Necessary Existent, making time a powerful evidence for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it look like to surrender your entire timeline—past, present, and future—to Allah today?
?
The argument proves that time cannot have a beginning or end. This means the Creator of time is outside of it, knowing your entire life at once. Does that thought fill you with awe or anxiety? How can you turn the passing of each moment into an act of worship, knowing that each second is a gift from the Eternal?
?
Razi says time provides clearer evidence for God than any other created thing. Yet you may have never considered the clock on your wall or the sunset as a proof. What does your neglect of this constant sign say about the state of your heart? Will you pause right now and let the very fact that time exists remind you of the One who sustains it?

The First Objection: Timing of Dependence
Razi presents the first objection against the need for an efficient cause, based on the timing of dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and reasoning of the first objection against the principle that every contingent needs an efficient cause.

Core Idea
The objection traps the causal principle in an impossible dilemma: dependence during existence or non-existence.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the foundational kalam principle that every contingent needs an efficient cause. The objector forces a dilemma: the dependence must occur either when the possible exists or when it does not. Both options are argued to be logically impossible. Follow Razi's tight reasoning and see why the objector claims the entire causal framework collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the clear dilemma structure and the two horns of the argument.
The objector uses a classic 'division and refutation' (taqsīm wa ibṭāl) method.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objector claims dependence must be either during existence (ḥāl al-wujūd) or during non-existence (ḥāl al-‘adam). Find where these are stated.
The Arabic phrases are 'حال وجوده' and 'حال عدمه'.
3
Analyze the argument against each horn
Read the reasons why each horn is impossible. For existence: it would mean 'bringing into existence something already existent' (ījād al-mawjūd). For non-existence: the efficient cause cannot act on pure nothingness, and original non-existence cannot be attributed to a cause of existence.
Pay attention to the logical flow: each horn is refuted with a concise argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since both horns are impossible, the objector concludes that the statement of efficient causation (al-ta’thīr) is impossible. This is the challenge Razi will later answer.
This is an objection, not Razi's own position—he will refute it in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : القول بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر عليه أسئلة : السؤال الأول : افتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر إما أن يكون حال وجوده أو حال عدمه، والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول بالافتقار أما الحصر فظاهر، وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الافتقار حال الوجود فلأنه يلزم إيجاد الموجود، وهو محال، وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الافتقار حال العدم، وذلك لأن المؤثر ما يكون له أثر، والعدم نفي محض فالقول بإثبات التأثير حال كون الأثر عدما محضا محال. وأيضًا فلأنه ما دام يكون باقيًا على العدم كان باقيا على عدمه الأصلي، والعدم الأصلي يمتنع استناده إلى مؤثر الوجود والإيجاد، فإذن ما دام يكون باقيا على العدم، امتنع استناده إلى المؤثر، وإذا ثبت فساد القسمين ثبت أن القول بالتأثير محال.
If it is said: The statement that the possible depends on an efficient cause is subject to questions: The first question: The dependence of the possible on the efficient cause is either during its existence or during its non-existence. Both divisions are invalid, so the statement of dependence becomes invalid. As for the exclusivity [of the division], it is apparent. As for it being impossible for the dependence to be during existence, because that would necessitate the bringing into existence of something already existent, which is impossible. As for it being impossible for the dependence to be during non-existence, because the efficient cause is that which has an effect, and non-existence is pure negation, so the claim of bringing about an effect while the effect is pure non-existence is impossible. Also, as long as it remains in non-existence, it remains in its original non-existence, and original non-existence cannot be attributed to an efficient cause of existence and bringing into existence. Therefore, as long as it remains in non-existence, its attribution to the efficient cause is impossible. When the corruption of the two divisions is established, it is established that the statement of efficient causation is impossible.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the principle that the contingent needs an efficient cause. The objection is structured as a dilemma: the dependence must occur either during the possible's existence or during its non-existence. Both options are argued to be impossible. During existence, dependence would mean 'bringing into existence' something already existent—absurd. During non-existence, the efficient cause cannot act, since its effect would be pure non-existence, which is nothing. Moreover, original non-existence cannot be traced back to an efficient cause. Since both sides fail, the objector concludes that the concept of efficient causation (al-ta'thir) is invalid. Note that Razi will likely answer this objection, but the response is not included in this excerpt.

Key Terms
افتقار
حال الوجود
حال العدم
إيجاد الموجود
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because non-existence is pure negation and cannot receive an effect.
B) Because the possible does not exist at all.
C) Because the efficient cause would then be prior to the possible.
D) Because non-existence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first objection against efficient causation argues that dependence cannot occur during existence or non-existence, thus the concept of efficient causation is impossible.

Second Objection: What Does the Efficient Cause Act Upon?
Razi presents the second objection and begins its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against efficient causation and how Razi begins to refute it by analyzing the possible objects of causation.

Core Idea
The efficient cause cannot act on essence, existence, or their combination without absurdity.

Razi presents a sophisticated objection against the very idea of efficient causation. The opponent argues that if a cause produces an effect, it must act on one of three things: the essence, the existence, or the qualification of the essence by existence. All three are claimed to be impossible, so causation is impossible. Razi then begins to refute this by showing that the first option leads to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks this argument and its initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the three possibilities.
The objection lists three things the efficient cause could act upon: the essence, the existence, or the qualification of essence by existence. Notice how these exhaust all options.
This exclusivity is defended logically.
3
Understand the exclusivity argument.
If none were dependent on the cause, the effect would be independent entirely, contradicting its being an effect.
This shows the three options are the only possibilities.
4
See why acting on essence is impossible.
Using the example of blackness: if blackness were blackness by an agent, removing the agent would require blackness to lose its essence, which is impossible because blackness is essentially black.
The refutation of this division is begun here.
5
Note the incomplete refutation.
The text cuts off mid-sentence; the full refutation of the remaining divisions is not present in this chunk.
This passage is only the beginning of the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : لو فرضنا أن مؤثرًا يؤثر في أثر لكان إما أن يؤثر في ماهية ذلك الأثر أو في وجوده أو في انصاف ماهيته بوجوده والكل باطل، فكان القول بالتأثير باطلا ، أما بيان الحصر فلأنه لو لم تكن الماهية ولا الوجود ولا اتصاف الماهية بالوجود مستندًا إلى المؤثر لكان ذلك الذي يفرض أثرًا لذلك المؤثر غنيا عن ذلك المؤثر في ماهيته وفي وجوده وفي اتصاف ماهيته بوجوده، وحينئذ يكون ذلك الأثر غنيا عن ذلك المؤثر مطلقاً ، فلا يكون أثرًا له هذا خلف. وأما بيان فساد الأقسام فهو أن نقول : إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير المؤثر في ماهية الأثر ، وذلك لأنه لو كان كون السواد سوادًا بالفاعل مثلا لكان عند فرض عدم ذلك الفاعل يلزم خروج السواد عن كونه سواداً؛ لأن ما بالغير لا يبقى عند فرض عدم ذلك الغير، لكن خروج السواد عن كونه سوادًا محال، فوجب أن لا
The second question: If we suppose that an efficient cause brings about an effect, then it either brings about the essence of that effect, or its existence, or the qualification of its essence by its existence. All are invalid, so the statement of efficient causation is invalid. As for the explanation of the exclusivity: if the essence, the existence, and the qualification of the essence by existence were not dependent on the efficient cause, then that which is supposed to be an effect of that efficient cause would be independent of that efficient cause in its essence, its existence, and its qualification of essence by existence. Then that effect would be absolutely independent of that efficient cause, so it would not be its effect—this is a contradiction. As for the explanation of the corruption of the divisions, it is that we say: We hold that it is not permissible for the efficient cause to affect the essence of the effect, because if, for example, blackness were blackness by the agent, then upon the assumption of the non-existence of that agent, it would necessitate the departure of blackness from being blackness, since that which is by another does not remain upon the assumption of the non-existence of that other. But the departure of blackness from being blackness is impossible, so it must not...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against efficient causation and begins its refutation. The objector argues that if an efficient cause (mu'aththir) produces an effect, it must act upon one of three things: the essence of the effect, its existence, or the qualification of the essence by existence. The objector claims all three are invalid. First, the exclusivity is defended: if none of these were dependent on the efficient cause, the supposed effect would be independent of it entirely, contradicting its being an effect. Then, the refutation of each division begins. The text only covers the first division—that the efficient cause cannot affect the essence. The example given is blackness: if blackness were blackness by the agent, then if the agent ceased to exist, blackness would have to cease being blackness, which is impossible because blackness is what it is essentially. Therefore, the efficient cause cannot affect the essence. The text cuts off here, but the argument presumably continues on the next page.

First Objection: Dependence Cannot Be at Existence or Non-Existence
First objection: dependence cannot be at existence or non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the dependence of the contingent on a cause, and why the objector argues that dependence cannot occur during existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
Dependence cannot be during existence (redundant) or non-existence (no effect), so causation is impossible.

This lesson examines a critical objection raised against the claim that contingent beings depend on a cause. The objector argues that the supposed dependence must occur either at the time of the contingent's existence or its non-existence, but both possibilities lead to absurdity. As a result, the very notion of causation is challenged.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection is presented as the 'first question' (السؤال الأول).
2
Identify the two alternatives
Find the two cases: dependence at existence and dependence at non-existence. Understand that these are presented as the only possibilities.
The objector uses a division (حصر) to set up the argument.
3
Analyze why dependence at existence is impossible
Consider the argument: if dependence occurs at existence, it would mean 'bringing the existent into existence' (إيجاد الموجود), which is logically absurd—you cannot bring about what already exists.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze why dependence at non-existence is impossible
If dependence occurs at non-existence, the cause would have no effect because non-existence is pure negation. Additionally, remaining in original non-existence cannot be attributed to a cause of existence.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since both alternatives are impossible, the objector concludes that the statement of influence (التأثير) is impossible. Reflect on the challenge this poses to the doctrine of dependence.
This objection must be answered by the theologian to preserve the belief in causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : القول بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر عليه أسئلة : هو السؤال الأول : افتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر إما أن يكون حال وجوده أو حال عدمه، والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول بالافتقار أما الحصر فظاهر، وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الافتقار حال الوجود فلأنه يلزم إيجاد الموجود، وهو محال، وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الافتقار حال العدم، وذلك لأن المؤثر ما يكون له أثر، والعدم نفي محض فالقول بإثبات التأثير حال كون الأثر عدما محضا محال. وأيضًا فلأنه ما دام يكون باقيًا على العدم كان باقيا على عدمه الأصلي، والعدم الأصلي يمتنع استناده إلى مؤثر الوجود والإيجاد، فإذن ما دام يكون باقيا على العدم، امتنع استناده إلى المؤثر، وإذا ثبت فساد القسمين ثبت أن القول بالتأثير محال.
If it is said: The statement of the dependence of the contingent on the cause has questions. The first question: The dependence of the contingent on the cause must either be at the time of its existence or at the time of its non-existence. Both divisions are invalid, so the statement of dependence is invalid. As for the division, it is apparent. As for why it is impossible for the dependence to be at the time of existence, it is because it would entail bringing the existent into existence (إيجاد الموجود), which is impossible. As for why it is impossible for the dependence to be at the time of non-existence, that is because the cause is that which has an effect, and non-existence is pure negation, so the statement of affirming influence while the effect is pure non-existence is impossible. Also, because as long as it remains in non-existence, it remains in its original non-existence, and original non-existence cannot be attributed to a cause of existence and bringing-into-being. Therefore, as long as it remains in non-existence, its attribution to the cause is impossible. And when the invalidity of the two divisions is established, it is established that the statement of influence is impossible.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (السؤال الأول) against the claim that the contingent depends on a cause. The objector argues that the dependence (الافتقار) must occur either during the existence of the contingent or during its non-existence, and both are impossible. If dependence occurs during existence, then the cause would be 'bringing the existent into existence' (إيجاد الموجود), which is redundant and logically absurd (تحصيل الحاصل). If dependence occurs during non-existence, then the cause would be influencing nothing, because non-existence is pure negation (العدم نفي محض). Additionally, as long as the contingent remains in non-existence, it is in its original non-existence, which cannot be attributed to a cause that brings existence. Therefore, since both possibilities are invalid, the concept of causation (التأثير) itself is deemed impossible.

Key Terms
افتقار
مؤثر
وجود
عدم
إيجاد الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dependence only applies at existence.
Correction: The objection shows that dependence at existence leads to the absurdity of bringing about what already exists, so it is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the first objection argue that dependence cannot be at the time of existence?
A) Because it would entail causing what already exists.
B) Because non-existence is pure negation.
C) Because dependence is a relation.
D) Because existence is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first objection against causation argues that dependence is impossible both at existence and non-existence, so the concept of influence is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objector claims that non-existence is pure negation and cannot be influenced. How does that affect your understanding of things you hope for or fear that do not yet exist? Do you rely on causes to bring them about? What does this passage say about that reliance?
?
This objection seems to undermine all secondary causation. If you accept it, would you still trust in the means you use to achieve your goals? How would you reconcile your practical life with this theological argument?

The Impossibility of Causal Influence on Essence, Existence, or Attribution
Second objection: the cause cannot influence essence, existence, or attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible modes of causal influence and why each is deemed impossible according to this objection.

Core Idea
Causal influence on any aspect of an effect leads to absurdity or infinite regress.

Imam Razi presents a powerful objection against the concept of causation by analyzing the possible ways a cause could influence its effect. The objection argues that influence cannot be on the essence, existence, or the relation between them, because each option leads to contradiction or infinite regress. This passage is a classic example of philosophical reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible modes of influence
Read the passage carefully and note the three categories: essence (ماهية), existence (وجود), and the attribution of existence to essence (اتصاف الماهية بالوجود). Understand why these three exhaust the possibilities.
The division is justified by claiming that if none of these were caused, the effect would be independent, contradicting its being an effect.
2
Understand the refutation of influence on essence
Examine the argument that if the cause influences the essence, then removing the cause would destroy the essence itself. For example, blackness would cease to be blackness without its cause—impossible.
This shows the essence cannot be contingent on a cause for its very nature.
3
Analyze the refutation of influence on existence
The same reasoning applies: if existence is caused, it would cease to be existence when the cause is removed, which is impossible.
4
Examine the two arguments against influence on attribution
First, the attribution is not an extra real entity; if it were, it would have its own essence and existence, leading to infinite regress. Second, even if it were extra, the same three-way division would apply to it, leading to the same impossibilities.
The infinite regress argument (تسلسل) is a key tool.
5
Synthesize the overall conclusion
Since all modes of influence are impossible, the concept of causation (التأثير) is rejected. Reflect on how this objection challenges naive views of causality.
This objection is part of a broader Ash'ari critique of necessary causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : لو فرضنا أن مؤثرًا يؤثر في أثر لكان إما أن يؤثر في ماهية ذلك الأثر أو في وجوده أو في اتصاف ماهيته بوجوده والكل باطل، فكان القول بالتأثير باطلا ، أما بيان الحصر فلأنه لو لم تكن الماهية ولا الوجود ولا اتصاف الماهية بالوجود مستندًا إلى المؤثر لكان ذلك الذي يفرض أثرًا لذلك المؤثر غنيا عن ذلك المؤثر في ماهيته وفي وجوده وفي اتصاف ماهيته بوجوده، وحينئذ يكون ذلك الأثر غنيا عن ذلك المؤثر مطلقاً ، فلا يكون أثرًا له هذا خلف. وأما بيان فساد الأقسام فهو أن نقول : إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير المؤثر في ماهية الأثر ، وذلك لأنه لو كان كون السواد سوادًا بالفاعل مثلا لكان عند فرض عدم ذلك الفاعل يلزم خروج السواد عن كونه سواداً؛ لأن ما بالغير لا يبقى عند فرض عدم ذلك الغير، لكن خروج السواد عن كونه سوادًا محال، فوجب أن لا يكون كون السواد سوادا واقعا بالفعل، وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير المؤثر في الوجود لأن عند تقدير عدم المؤثر يلزم أن يخرج الوجود عن كونه وجوداً، وذلك أيضًا محال كما قررناه في الماهية. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير المؤثر في اتصاف الماهية بالوجود فذلك لوجهين : الأول : أن اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ليس أمرًا زائدا على الماهية، وعلى الوجود، إذ لو كان زائدا لكانت له ماهية وله وجود، وكان اتصاف تلك الماهية بذلك الوجود زائدا عليه، ولزم التسلسل. الثاني : أن بتقدير أن تكون الموصوفية أمرًا زائدا على الماهية وعلى الوجود حصلت لها ماهية ووجود فحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور، وهو أن المؤثر إما أن يؤثر في ماهيته أو في وجوده أو في موصوفية ماهيته بوجوده والكل محال، فثبت أن المؤثر لو أثر لكان إما أن يؤثر في الماهية أو في الوجود أو في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، والكل محال، فكان القول بالتأثير محالا.
The second question: If we assume that a cause produces an effect, then it must either influence the essence of that effect, or its existence, or the attribution of its essence with existence. All [divisions] are invalid, so the statement of influence is invalid. As for the explanation of the division: because if neither the essence, nor existence, nor the attribution of essence with existence is dependent on the cause, then that which is assumed to be an effect of that cause would be independent of that cause in its essence, in its existence, and in the attribution of its essence with its existence. Then that effect would be absolutely independent of that cause, so it would not be an effect of it; this is a contradiction. As for the explanation of the invalidity of the divisions: we say that it is not permissible for the influence of the cause to be in the essence of the effect, because if, for example, blackness being blackness were through the agent, then if we assume the non-existence of that agent, it would necessitate blackness departing from being blackness; since what is through another does not remain when that other is assumed non-existent, but blackness departing from being blackness is impossible, so it must not be that blackness being blackness occurs through the agent. As for why it is not permissible for the influence of the cause to be in existence, because when the non-existence of the cause is assumed, existence would have to depart from being existence, and that is also impossible, as we established for the essence. As for why it is not permissible for the influence of the cause to be in the attribution of the essence with existence, that is for two reasons: First, the attribution of the essence with existence is not an additional thing over and above the essence and existence, because if it were additional, it would have an essence and existence, and the attribution of that essence with that existence would be additional to it, leading to an infinite regress. Second, if it is assumed that the state of being described is something additional over and above the essence and existence, then it would have an essence and existence, and then the aforementioned division would return: that the cause either influences its essence, or its existence, or the attribution of its essence with its existence, and all are impossible. Thus it is established that if the cause were to produce an effect, it would either influence the essence, or existence, or the attribution of the essence with existence, and all are impossible, so the statement of influence is impossible.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (السؤال الثاني) against causation. The objector assumes that if a cause (مؤثر) has an effect (أثر), the influence must be on one of three things: the essence (ماهية) of the effect, its existence (وجود), or the attribution of existence to the essence (اتصاف الماهية بالوجود). First, the division itself is justified: if none of these three are caused by the agent, then the effect would be completely independent, contradicting its status as effect. Then, each division is refuted. Influence on the essence is impossible because if the essence were caused, then removing the cause would destroy the essence itself (e.g., blackness would cease to be blackness), which is impossible. Influence on existence is similarly impossible because it would make existence dependent and perishable. Influence on the attribution of existence to essence is refuted by two arguments: (1) this attribution is not a real additional entity; if it were, it would lead to infinite regress; (2) even if it were additional, the same division would apply, leading to the same impossibility. Therefore, all possible modes of influence fail, and the concept of influence (التأثير) is deemed impossible.

Key Terms
ماهية
وجود
اتصاف
مؤثر
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that influence on the essence is analogous to creation, but the objection shows it would make the essence contingent and mutable.
Correction: The argument shows that if the essence were caused, it would cease to be itself when the cause is removed, which is impossible for essential properties.

Checkpoint
Why does the second objection argue that influence on the essence is impossible?
A) Because the essence is eternal.
B) Because the essence would cease to be itself if the cause were removed.
C) Because influence on essence leads to infinite regress.
D) Because the essence is not real.

Key Takeaway
The three possible modes of causal influence are all impossible, therefore the concept of causation is logically flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely believe that only Allah has true causal influence, or do you unconsciously attribute power to secondary causes like your own efforts? What practical change would this belief demand in your daily reliance on means?
?
When you plan your future, do you feel that your actions are causally effective? How does this passage challenge the assumption that you are the true cause of your outcomes?
?
If all causal influence other than Allah is impossible, what does that mean for your sense of control? Are you willing to let go of the illusion of being an independent cause and place your trust entirely in Allah? What would that look like tomorrow?

Third Objection: Influence is Not Identical to Cause or Effect
Third objection: influence cannot be identical to agent or effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against causation, which argues that the act of influence cannot be identical to the essence of the cause or the effect, and the three proofs supporting this objection.

Core Idea
Influence is a distinct relational attribute, not identical to cause or effect.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a third objection against the theory of causation. The objector argues that the act of influence (التأثير) must be either identical to the cause's essence, the effect's essence, or a third thing. Since the first two are impossible, and the third leads to problems, causation is invalid. The three arguments demonstrate why influence cannot be identified with the essences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's structure
The passage begins by stating three possibilities for influence: identical to cause, identical to effect, or a third distinct thing. Then it declares the first two invalid and gives three arguments.
2
Examine the first argument (الوجه الأول)
The first argument relies on the conceivability of essences without knowledge of influence. We can conceive the cause and effect while doubting causation, so the influence is unknown and thus distinct.
Example: conceiving the Necessary Existent and the world while doubting the cause.
3
Examine the second argument (الوجه الثاني)
Influence is a relation (نسبة) between cause and effect. A relation is posterior to its relata, and what is posterior is distinct. Therefore, influence is distinct from both essences.
4
Examine the third argument (الوجه الثالث)
We describe the cause as 'influencer' (صفة). The described subject (الموصوف) is distinct from the attribute, so influence is distinct from the cause's essence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the first two possibilities are invalid, and the third is not discussed (or leads to regress), the objector concludes that influence is impossible.
The passage does not explicitly reject the third option, but implies it is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لو أثر شيء في شيء لكان تأثير المؤثر في الأثر إما أن يكون عين ذات المؤثر أو ذات الأثر أو يكون أمرًا ثالثًا مغايرا لهما والقسمان باطلان، فكان القول بالتأثير باطلا. بوجوه: الأول : أنه يمكننا أن نعقل ذات المؤثر وذات الأثر، مع الشك في كون ذلك المؤثر مؤثرًا في هذا الأثر مثلا : نعقل الموجود الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته، ونعقل هذا العالم، ثم نشك في أن المؤثر في وجود هذا العالم هو ذلك الواجب أم واجب آخر أو شيء من معلولات واجب الوجود؟ والمعلوم غير ما هو غير معلوم فهذه المؤثرية التي هي غير معلومة مغايرة لذات المؤثر، ولذات الأثر، الذي هو معلوم. الثاني : إن تأثير المؤثر في الأثر نسبة بين ذات الأثر وذات المؤثر، والنسبة بين شيئين متأخرة عن تينك الذاتين، والمتأخر عن الشيء مغاير لذلك الشيء. الثالث : وهو أنا نصف ذات المؤثر بكونه مؤثرًا في ذلك الأثر، والموصوف مغاير للصفة.
The third question: If something produces an effect in something, then the influence of the cause in the effect must either be identical to the essence of the cause, or to the essence of the effect, or be a third thing distinct from both. The two divisions are invalid, so the statement of influence is invalid. By [the following] arguments: First: We can conceive the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect while being doubtful about whether that cause is actually influencing this effect. For example, we conceive the being that is the Necessary Existent in itself, and we conceive this world, then we doubt whether the cause of the existence of this world is that Necessary, or another Necessary, or something from the effects of the Necessary. The known is other than the unknown; thus this 'influencer-ness' which is unknown is distinct from the essence of the cause and from the essence of the effect, which are known. Second: The influence of the cause in the effect is a relation (نسبة) between the essence of the effect and the essence of the cause, and the relation between two things is posterior to those two essences, and whatever is posterior to a thing is distinct from that thing. Third: We describe the essence of the cause as being an influencer in that effect, and the described is distinct from the attribute.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (السؤال الثالث) against causation. The objector argues that if a cause produces an effect, the act of influence (التأثير) must be either identical to the essence of the cause, identical to the essence of the effect, or a third distinct thing. The first two are impossible, as shown by three arguments (وجوه). The first argument: we can conceive the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect without knowing whether influence occurs. For instance, we conceive the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود) and the world, but we doubt which cause brought the world into existence. The 'influencer-ness' (المؤثرية) is unknown, whereas the essences are known, so the influence is distinct from both essences. The second argument: influence is a relation (نسبة) between the cause and effect, and a relation is posterior to its relata (المتأخر عن الشيء مغاير لذلك الشيء), so it is distinct. The third argument: we describe the cause as 'influencer' (صفة), and the described subject (الموصوف) is distinct from the attribute. Since influence cannot be identical to either cause or effect, and cannot be a third thing (because that would lead to infinite regress or other problems?), the objector concludes that influence is impossible.

Key Terms
تأثير
ذات
نسبة
مغايرة
مؤثرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Influence is simply a property of the agent, identical to its essence.
Correction: The objection shows that influence is a relational attribute distinct from the agent's essence, as we can conceive the agent without knowing its influence.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument (الوجه الأول) given to show that influence is not identical to the essence of the cause?
A) We can conceive the cause without its influence.
B) Influence is a relation posterior to the relata.
C) We can conceive the cause and effect without certainty about causation.
D) The described is distinct from the attribute.

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that influence cannot be identical to cause or effect, nor a third thing, thereby challenging the possibility of causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a success in your life to your own action, have you ever paused to consider that the 'influence' you claim might be a mere relational attribute, not identical to your essence? If your influence is not truly yours, then who is the real actor? How would your daily accountability change if you truly believed that your ability to cause is not identical to you?
?
The passage argues that we can conceive of the cause without conceiving its influence. Think of a time you felt proud of your 'influence' over someone. Can you separate your identity from that influence? If your influence were removed, would you still be the same person? This lesson strips away the illusion of control—are you willing to let go of the belief that your actions are your own, and instead see them as mere relational accidents?
?
The third objection says influence is a relation posterior to cause and effect. If all your 'effects' (actions, results) are merely relations, and you as the 'cause' exist prior, then what is left of your identity after the effect? Are you more than the sum of your influences? This lesson calls you to examine whether you live as a 'cause' or as a mere link in a chain. What would it take for you to live as though your essence is independent of your outcomes?

Second Way: Intuitive and Inferential Approaches
Razi presents two approaches to proving the need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two approaches (intuitive and inferential) that mutakallimun use to prove that every originated thing needs a cause.

Core Idea
Origination implies need — either by immediate intuition or by logical inference.

Imam Razi continues his argument for a cause of the world by examining how we know that originated things need an originator. Most theologians accept this premise, but they differ on whether it is self-evident or requires proof. In this passage, Razi presents both sides, using the famous example of a building to illustrate the intuitive approach and the Mu'tazilite theory of human action for the inferential one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two distinct groups mentioned.
The term 'muhdath' (originated) is key.
2
Identify the two approaches
Find where Razi introduces the first approach (intuitive) and the second (inferential). Note the evidence each uses.
The first uses the building example; the second uses human actions.
3
Understand the intuitive argument
The intuitive argument claims that knowledge of the need for a cause is immediate. The building example shows that we naturally recognize that a building needs a builder. Denying this is considered irrational.
4
Understand the inferential argument
The inferential argument, used by Mu'tazila like Abu Ali and Hashim, starts from human actions: we cause our own actions because they are originated. This establishes origination as the cause of need, then applies to the world.
This approach uses analogy and logical reasoning, not direct intuition.
5
Reflect on the difference
Consider why Razi presents both views without favoring one. What does this tell you about the state of kalam debate?
The common misunderstanding is that the inferential approach is weaker, but it still provides certainty through proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني في تقرير هذا البرهان أن نقول : الأجسام محدثة، وكل محدث فهو محتاج إلى المحدث من غير تعرض لكون ذلك المحدث جائزا أو واجبًا - وأكثر مشايخ المتكلمين كانوا معولين على هذه الطريقة - ثم لهؤلاء طريقان : الأول : الذين قالوا : العلم باجتماع المحدث إلى المؤثر علم ضروري قالوا : والذي يدل عليه أن كل من رأى بناء رفيعا وقصرًا مشيدًا اضطر إلى العلم بأن له بانيا صانعاً، حتى إن من جوز حدوث ذلك البناء لا عن فاعل كان محكوما عليه بالجنون، فعلمنا أن هذه المقدمة بديهية . الثاني: الذين قالوا بأن هذه المقدمة استدلالية - وهم أكثر شيوخ المعتزلة كأبي علي وهاشم - وطريقهم هو أنهم يثبتون كون العبد موجدا لأفعال نفسه، يثبتون أن أفعالنا إنما افتقرت إلينا لأنها حدثت بعد أن كانت معدومة، وعند هذا يظهر أن الحدوث علة للافتقار إلى المحدث، فالعالم لما كان محدثًا افتقر إلى الفاعل.
The second way of presenting this proof is to say: Bodies are originated (muhdatha), and every originated thing is in need of an originator (muhdith) without addressing whether that originator is contingent or necessary. Most of the senior mutakallimun relied on this method. Then for these [advocates] there are two ways: The first: those who said that knowledge of the need of the originated for the effecting agent (mu'aththir) is necessary (daruri) knowledge. They said: evidence for it is that whoever sees a lofty building and a well-constructed palace is compelled to know that it has a builder and maker, so much so that one who allows that building to come about without an agent is judged insane. Thus we know that this premise is self-evident (badihiyya). The second: those who said that this premise is inferential (istidlaliyya) — and they are most of the senior Mu'tazila like Abu Ali and Hashim. Their method is that they establish that the servant is the creator (mujid) of his own actions; they establish that our actions need us only because they occurred after being non-existent. From this it appears that origination (huduth) is the cause ('illa) of the need for an originator. Thus, since the world is originated, it is in need of an agent (fa'il).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the second way of arguing for the existence of a cause for the world. Razi explains that most mutakallimun rely on the premise that every originated thing needs a cause. Within this, there are two approaches: (1) The intuitive approach: knowledge that an originated thing needs an agent is self-evident, as illustrated by the example of a building — anyone seeing a well-constructed building immediately knows it has a builder, and denying that is considered insanity. (2) The inferential approach: this premise is not self-evident but is proven by analogy with human actions. The Mu'tazila (especially Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son Hashim) argue that humans originate their own actions, and since those actions occur after non-existence, they need a cause (the human). This establishes that origination (huduth) is the cause of need. By extension, the world, being originated, must need an agent. Razi presents these approaches without yet critiquing them.

Key Terms
محدث
مؤثر
بديهية
استدلالية
حدوث
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inferential approach makes the need for a cause less certain or merely probable.
Correction: Both approaches aim at certainty; the inferential approach provides logical proof, not mere probability. Razi presents them as two valid methods, not as degrees of certainty.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi give for the intuitive approach to proving the need for a cause?
A) A tree growing from a seed
B) A lofty building and a well-constructed palace
C) A child being born
D) The movement of the sun

Key Takeaway
The need of an originated thing for an originator can be known either through immediate intuition or through logical inference from the nature of origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the universe, do you feel an immediate compulsion that it must have a Creator, or do you rely on logical arguments? Which approach resonates more with your heart, and why? How would you answer someone who says the universe could have come about without a cause, just as some might say a building could appear without a builder? What does your response reveal about your own certainty in Allah?
?
Have you ever considered your own actions as evidence of the need for a Creator? If your own existence and actions are originated after non-existence, what does that demand from you in terms of gratitude and purpose? How does this affect your daily choices?
?
If the knowledge that everything needs an originator is self-evident, why do so many people deny God? What does that tell you about the state of the human heart? How can you guard against such denial in yourself?

Three Proofs of the Equality of Essences
Razi gives three proofs for the first premise and deduces the second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three arguments that all essences are equal in their concept and why this necessitates distinction through attributes.

Core Idea
Essences are equal; distinction comes from attributes.

Imam Razi begins this passage by establishing a foundational principle: all essences (dhawat) share a common concept of being an essence. He offers three crisp proofs to show this. Then, building on this equality, he argues that the distinctiveness of any essence—especially that of Allah—must arise from additional attributes, not from the essence itself. This lesson walks through each of those proofs and the crucial inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that Razi first states the conclusion, then gives proofs, then returns to the second premise.
2
Understand the first premise: equality of essences
Identify the three proofs Razi gives for why all essences are equal in their concept of being an essence. How does each proof support the same conclusion?
The proofs are: 1) division into necessary/possible, 2) constancy of essence-concept, 3) exhaustive division of known into essence/attribute.
3
Grasp the second premise: distinction through attributes
See how Razi moves from equality to distinction: if essences are equal, anything that distinguishes them must be outside the essence, i.e., attributes.
This is crucial for the doctrine of divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Ask: Why does Razi need to first prove equality? What would happen if essences were inherently distinct?
This argument supports the Ash'ari view that attributes are additional to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك وجب أن يكون امتياز بعضها عن البعض بسبب الصفات والأحوال. وأما بيان المقدمة الأولى فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أنه يصح تقسيم الذوات إلى الواجب وإلى الممكن ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين. والثاني: أنا إذا اعتقدنا ذاتا فسواء اعتقدنا قديمًا أو محدثًا أو واجبًا أو ممكنا ، فإن اعتقاد كونه ذاتا لا يزول ولا يتبدل وهذا يدل على أن المفهوم من الذوات واحد في كل المواضع. والثالث : أنا نقول : المعلوم إما ذات وإما صفة وصريح العقل يشهد بأن هذا التقسيم منحصر ، ولولا أن المفهوم من كون الذوات ذاتا أمر واحد، وإلا لم يكن هذا التقسيم منحصراً، فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي أنه لما كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون امتياز بعض الذوات عن بعض بسبب الصفات - فذلك لأنه لا شك أن الله تعالى متميز عن سائر الموجودات وثبت أن ذاته مساوية لسائر الذوات وما به الامتیاز غیر ما به المشاركة، فوجب أن يكون امتياز ذاته عن سائر الذوات بأمور زائدة على الذات، يكون بالصفة.
Similarly, it is necessary that distinction of some of them from others be due to attributes and states. As for the demonstration of the first premise, there are proofs for it: First: that it is valid to divide essences into the Necessary and the possible, and the locus of division is common between the two divisions. Second: that if we conceive an essence, whether we conceive it as eternal or originated, or necessary or possible, the conception of its being an essence does not cease nor change, and this indicates that the concept of essences is one in all places. Third: that we say: the known is either an essence or an attribute, and clear reason testifies that this division is exhaustive; and were it not that the concept of essences being an essence is one thing, then this division would not be exhaustive. So by these proofs it is established that essences are equal in being essences. As for the second premise—that since this is the case, it is necessary that distinction of some essences from others be due to attributes—that is because there is no doubt that God Most High is distinct from all other existents, and it has been established that His essence is equal to all other essences, and that by which distinction occurs is other than that by which sharing occurs, so it is necessary that His essence's distinction from all other essences be by matters additional to the essence, which is by the attribute.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by Razi for two premises. First, he argues that essences (dhawat) are equal in their being essences. He gives three proofs: (1) The division into necessary and possible essences shows a common denominator. (2) The concept of 'essence' remains constant whether we think of something eternal or originated, necessary or possible. (3) The exhaustive division of the known into essence and attribute would not be exhaustive unless 'essence' had a single meaning. From these, he concludes that essences are equal in their essencehood. Then, he states the second premise: since essences are equal, distinction must come from attributes. He argues that God is distinct from other beings, but His essence is equal, so distinction must be through something additional—attributes. The proof is by elimination: what is shared cannot be what distinguishes, so distinguishing features must be extra.

Key Terms
ذوات
صفات
واجب
ممكن
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Razi intends essences to be identical in every respect, including reality.
Correction: Razi only argues for equality in the concept of being an essence (كونها ذاتا), not numerical identity. The essences are conceptually similar but distinct in reality; the distinction comes from attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the three proofs given by Razi, what is established about essences (dhawat)?
A) They are all identical in every respect.
B) They are equal in being essences.
C) They are distinct due to themselves.
D) They are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
All essences share a single concept of 'essence'; hence, what makes them distinct must be additional attributes, not the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence is equal in concept to Allah's, what does that reveal about your utter dependence on His attributes for your very existence? How are you currently seeking His mercy and power in your life?
?
You share the concept of 'essence' with every created being. Yet you are not a rock or a tree. What attributes given by Allah distinguish you? Are you using them to know Him or to forget Him?
?
This lesson proves that distinction comes from attributes, not essence. If Allah did not have attributes of mercy and justice, would you still worship Him? How does knowing His attributes change your prayer today?
