Opponent's First Proof: Allah Must Be in a Direction
The opponent presents the first station with two proofs for Allah being in a direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments the opponent uses to prove that Allah must necessarily be in a direction.

Core Idea
The opponent claims that any two existents must either interpenetrate or be separated by direction.

The opponent begins by arguing from reason that Allah must occupy a direction. They present two proofs: one based on the necessary relationship between any two existents, and the other based on the shared attribute of self-subsistence with bodies. This lesson examines these arguments critically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Note the structure: first station with two proofs.
The passage is part of the opponent's case, not the author's own view.
2
Identify the two proofs
The first proof is based on interpenetration and separation; the second is based on self-subsistence. Locate each one in the text.
3
Understand the logic
For the first proof, consider why this disjunction (interpenetration or separation by direction) is claimed to be necessary. For the second, note the analogy between a body and Allah.
The opponent treats self-subsistence as implying occupancy of direction.
4
Reflect on the context
Remember that these are the opponent's arguments that Razi will later refute. Think about what assumptions they make.
This prepares you for the refutation in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بالعقل والنقل : أما أما العقل : فهو أنه تعالى لا بد وأن يكون في حيز وجهة، فإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن يكون في جهة فوق. المقام الأول، وهو أنه تعالى في الحيز والجهة فاحتجوا عليه بوجهين : الأول : أن كل موجودين لا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريًا في الآخر، كالعرض الساري في الجوهر، أو يكون مباينا عنه بالجهة كالجسمين والعلم بذلك ضروري. والثاني : أن الجسم مختص بالحيز والجهة وإنما كان كذلك لأنه قائم بالنفس، والله تعالى يشاركه في كونه قائمًا بالنفس، فوجب أن يكون مشاركًا له له في الحصول في الجهة. وأما المقام الثاني : وهو أنه تعالى لما ثبت أنه يجب أن يكون في الجهة ونقول : يجب أن تكون تلك الجهة هي جهة فوق فيدل عليه وجهان الأول : أن أشرف الجهات جهة فوق وتخصيص أشرف الجهات بأشرف الموجودات هو المناسب المعقول. والثاني : أن الخلق بمجرد طباعهم وقلوبهم السليمة يرفعون الأيدي إلى جهة العلو عند الدعاء والتضرع، وذلك يدل على أن فطرتهم تشهد بأن معبودهم في جهة العلو.
The opponent argued by reason and transmitted text. As for reason: it is that He (exalted) must necessarily be in a place and direction; if this is established, then He must be in the direction above. The first station, that He (exalted) is in place and direction, they argued by two aspects: First, that every two existents must necessarily be either one interpenetrating the other, like an accident in a substance, or separated from it by direction, like two bodies, and knowledge of that is necessary. Second, that a body is specified to a place and direction, and it is only so because it is self-subsistent, and Allah (exalted) shares with it in being self-subsistent, so He must share with it in being in a direction. As for the second station, that when it is established that He (exalted) must be in a direction, we say: that direction must be the direction above. Two aspects indicate that: First, the noblest of directions is the direction above, and specifying the noblest of directions for the noblest of existents is rationally appropriate. Second, creation, by their very nature and sound hearts, raise their hands towards the direction of height when supplicating and beseeching, and that indicates that their innate disposition testifies that their object of worship is in the direction of height.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the adversary's (khaṣm) rational arguments for Allah being in a direction. It is divided into two stations (maqām): first, proving that Allah must be in a direction; second, proving that direction is upwards. For the first station, they give two proofs: (1) Any two existents are either interpenetrating (like accident in substance) or separated by direction (like two bodies)—this is known necessarily; since Allah and the world are not interpenetrating, they must be separated by direction. (2) A body is specified to a direction because it is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-l-nafs); Allah also is self-subsistent, so He must likewise occupy a direction. For the second station, they give two proofs that the direction must be above: (1) The noblest direction is above, and the noblest being deserves the noblest direction. (2) Human fitra (innate disposition) leads people to raise their hands upwards in supplication, indicating that their innate nature witnesses that their object of worship is in the direction of above.

Key Terms
ساري
مباين
قائم بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are presented by the author as true.
Correction: These are the opponent's arguments, which Razi will later refute.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's first proof, what must be true of any two existents?
A) They are always separate.
B) They must either interpenetrate or be separated by direction.
C) They are always interpenetrating.
D) They are never in a direction.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that Allah must be in a direction because any two existents are either interpenetrating or separated by direction, and because Allah shares self-subsistence with bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah as 'above' in a physical sense, as if He occupies a place? How does this lesson's presentation of the opponent's argument make you examine your own mental image of Allah? What will you do to ensure your belief remains free from spatial concepts?
?
The opponent uses the fact that we cannot conceive of two things without either interpenetration or separation. Have you ever found yourself limiting Allah by your own 'necessary' reasoning? In what areas of your faith might you be unconsciously applying human logic to the Divine? Ask Allah to purify your tawheed.
?
If the opponent's argument were true, Allah would be confined by direction. How would that affect your sense of His omnipresence and your intimacy with Him in worship? Is your heart satisfied with a God who is not bound by space, or do you long for a physical nearness? Commit to learning more about Allah's transcendence.

Opponent's Second Proof: The Direction Must Be Above
The opponent presents the second station with two proofs that the direction is above.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's two arguments for why the direction of Allah must be specifically above.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that the noblest direction (above) belongs to the noblest being, and human nature testifies to this.

Having established that Allah must be in a direction, the opponent now argues that this direction is above. They present two proofs: one based on the appropriateness of associating the noblest direction with the noblest being, and the other based on the universal human practice of raising hands upward in supplication. This lesson explores these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second station

The opponent assumes the first station is proven.

3
Understand the nobility argument
The opponent claims the noblest direction (above) should belong to the noblest being (Allah). Reflect on the assumption that direction has degrees of nobility.
4
Understand the fitra argument
The opponent uses the universal practice of raising hands in supplication as evidence that human nature testifies that Allah is above. Consider the strength of this argument.
Fitra is often used in Islamic theology.
5
Reflect on context
Remember these are the opponent's arguments. Think about potential counterarguments.

The Mutakallimun's Answer and the Necessary Distinction
Razi presents the mutakallimun's answer and proof from necessary distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's distinction between the need for a preponderator in temporal origination versus in voluntary agency, and their proof from the necessary distinction between a voluntary agent and a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
A voluntary agent's act does not need a preponderator, unlike temporal origination.

In this passage, al-Razi presents the mutakallimun's response to the objection that voluntary action also needs a preponderator. They clarify that the need for a preponderator applies only to temporal origination, not to the preponderance of action in a capable agent. Then they offer a powerful proof from the necessary, intuitive distinction between voluntary and natural causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the flow from answer to proof.
The passage has two clear parts: an answer and a proof.
2
Identify the two parts
Locate where the answer ends and the proof begins. The proof starts with 'والذي يدل عليه'.
3
Understand the key distinction
Focus on what requires a preponderator (temporal origination) and what does not (voluntary agency). This is the core claim.
4
Analyze the proof
Follow the logic: the distinction between voluntary and natural agency is necessary; if voluntary acts needed a preponderator, the distinction would vanish; therefore, the necessity of a preponderator is false.
Think about why this is a powerful argument.
5
Reflect on divine attributes
Consider how this argument supports the view that Allah is a free agent (qadir mukhtar) and not a necessitating cause.
This connects to the broader theme of divine volition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب المتكلمون عن السؤال الأول بأنا لا نقول : إن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا يحوج إلى المرجح في جميع المواضع بل نقول : الشيء إذا وجد بعد عدمه فهذا الحدوث وهذا الإمكان هو المحوج إلى المقتضي، فأما ترجيح الفعل على الترك في حق القادر فذلك لا يحوج إلى المؤثر. والذي يدل عليه أن الفرق بين القدر المختار وبين العلة الموجبة أمر معلوم بالضرورة، فإن كل أحد يفرق بالضرورة بين كون الإنسان مختارًا في فعله وقوله و قیامه وقعوده وبين كون الحجر هابطا بالطبع والنار صاعدة بالطبع، وتوقف صدور الفعل عن القادر على المرجح يقتضي بأن يبقى بين الموجب و بين المختار فرق البتة، وكل نظري أفضى إلى فساد الضروري كان باطلا، فعلمنا أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على المرجح فهذا منتهى البحث في هذه الباب.
The theologians (mutakallimun) answered the first question by saying: We do not say that the preponderance of one side of a possible [thing] over the other does not need a preponderator in all cases. Rather, we say: When a thing comes into existence after non-existence, it is this temporal origination (huduth) and this possibility that necessitate an efficient cause (muqtadi). As for the preponderance of the act over the omission in the case of a capable agent (qadir), that does not necessitate an effecter (mu'aththir). And what indicates this is that the distinction between a voluntary capacity (al-qadr al-mukhtar) and a necessitating cause (al-illa al-mujiba) is known by necessity. For everyone necessarily distinguishes between a human being being free in his action, speech, standing, and sitting, and a stone falling by nature and fire rising by nature. And the dependence of the act issuing from a capable agent upon a preponderator would entail that no distinction remains between the necessitator (mujib) and the free agent (mukhtar) at all. And every theoretical [argument] that leads to the corruption of a necessary [truth] is false. So we know that it is necessary to acknowledge that the act issuing from a capable agent does not depend upon a preponderator. This is the end of the discussion in this chapter.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the response of the mutakallimun to the earlier objection about the need for a preponderator. They clarify that the need for a preponderator applies to temporal origination (huduth) and possibility (imkan), not to the preponderance of action over omission in a capable agent. They then offer a proof from the necessary distinction between voluntary agency (al-qadr al-mukhtar) and necessitating causality (al-illa al-mujiba). Razi argues that everyone intuitively knows the difference between a human choosing freely (e.g., standing or sitting) and a stone falling or fire rising by natural necessity. If the act of a capable agent required a preponderator, then the agent would be no different from a necessitating cause. Since such a conclusion would contradict a necessary truth, the premise must be false. Therefore, the act of a capable agent does not require a preponderator.

Key Terms
قادر (qadir)
موجب (mujib)
مرجح (murajjih)
علة موجبة (illa mujiba)
ضروري (daruri)
مختار (mukhtar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mutakallimun deny any cause for voluntary action.
Correction: They only deny the need for a preponderator in the specific sense of a necessitating external factor, not causation entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what requires a preponderator?
A) The act of a capable agent
B) Temporal origination and possibility
C) The distinction between voluntary and natural acts
D) The celestial causes

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun establish that a voluntary agent's act does not require a preponderator, based on the necessary intuitive distinction between voluntary and natural agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Empirical Argument for the Need of a Preponderator
Razi presents an empirical argument that will requires a preponderator, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's empirical argument that every voluntary choice requires a preponderator (murajjih), and how he defends it against the objection of unknown cause.

Core Idea
Choice without an inner inclination is impossible; that inclination is the preponderator.

Razi begins his second argument by grounding theology in everyday human experience. When faced with two equal options, we notice that we only choose one when a specific inclination arises in the heart. This simple observation leads to a profound conclusion: every voluntary act presupposes a preponderator. In this passage, Razi presents the argument, anticipates a skeptical objection, and closes with a vivid example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let Razi's reasoning flow naturally.
Notice the structure: argument, then objection, then answer.
2
Locate the empirical premise
Identify the key empirical claim: when we experiment with two equal options, we find that we do not choose without an inner inclination.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection says we don't know why the inclination occurs. Razi's answer avoids infinite regress and points to God or celestial causes.
This defends the argument without needing to explain every detail.
4
Analyze the example of the thirsty person
Razi uses a concrete scenario to reinforce the argument. See how the example mirrors the abstract reasoning.
This is an inductive verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنا إذا جربنا من أنفسنا في القدحين والرغيفين والطريقين علمنا أنه ما لم يحدث في قلبنا ميل وداعية إلى اختيار أحدهما دون الآخر، فإنا لا نختار ذلك المعين دون الآخر، أو إذا علمنا أنه لا بد في الترجيح من حصول الميل إلى أحدهما في القلب على التعيين فذلك الميل مرجح خاص فثبت أن في هذه الصورة لم يحصل الرجحان إلا مع المرجح. أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : لا ندري لم حدث الميل إلى هذا الرغيف ولم يحدث إلى الرغيف الآخر ؟ لأنا نقول : سبب حدوث الميل في قلوبنا ليس لميل آخر في القلب، وإلا لزم التسلسل، بل الميول والإرادات تنتهي إلى ميل وإرادة تحدث في القلب إما بخلق الله تعالى أو بسبب من الأسباب السماوية، وحينئذ يكون هذا الإشكال زائلا. والذي يحقق هذا الكلام أن العطشان إذا خير بين القدحين فإنه ما لم يخص أحد القدحين بمد اليد لأخذه فإنه لا يمكنه أن يشرب ذلك الماء، وما لم يمل قلبه إلى أخذ ذلك القدح فإنه لا يمد يده إليه فذلك الميل الخاص والإرادة الخاصة مرجحة لأحد الطرفين على الآخر، فثبت أن في هذه الصورة لم يحصل الرجحان إلا مع المرجح، وأما أنه لم حدث الميل إلى هذا ولم يحدث إلى ذلك ؟ فذلك مستند إلى الأسباب الفلكية.
The second [argument]: If we experiment with ourselves concerning two cups, two loaves, and two paths, we know that as long as an inclination (mayl) and a motive (da'iya) to choose one of them over the other have not occurred in our hearts, we do not choose that specific one over the other. Or if we know that for preponderance (tarjih) it is necessary for inclination toward one of them to occur in the heart specifically, then that inclination is a specific preponderator (murajjih khaas). So it is established that in this case preponderance does not occur except with a preponderator. The utmost that can be said in this matter is: We do not know why the inclination occurred toward this loaf and did not occur toward the other loaf? We say: The cause of the occurrence of inclination in our hearts is not another inclination in the heart, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. Rather, inclinations and volitions terminate at an inclination and volition that occur in the heart either by the creation of God or by one of the celestial causes. Then this problem disappears. And what verifies this statement is that the thirsty person, when given the choice between two cups, as long as he does not specify one of the cups by extending his hand to take it, he cannot drink that water. And as long as his heart does not incline to take that cup, he does not extend his hand to it. So that specific inclination and specific volition are a preponderator of one of the two sides over the other. Thus it is established that in this case preponderance does not occur except with a preponderator. As for why the inclination occurred to this [one] and did not occur to that [one], that is attributed to celestial causes.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-thani) in a series of proofs for the necessity of a preponderator (murajjih) for voluntary action. Razi begins with an empirical observation: when we experience being torn between two equal alternatives (two cups of water, two loaves of bread, two paths), we find that we do not choose one over the other unless a specific inclination (mayl) or motive (da'iya) arises in the heart. This inclination is a specific preponderator that tilts the balance. The author then anticipates an objection: one might say that we do not know why this inclination occurs toward one option and not the other. Razi's answer is that the inclination itself is not caused by another inclination (to avoid infinite regress) but ultimately terminates at an inclination created directly by God or by celestial causes. He then reinforces the argument with the concrete example of a thirsty person choosing between two cups: without a specific inclination toward one cup, the hand does not move. The conclusion is that preponderance always requires a preponderator, and the specific cause of the inclination is attributed to celestial factors.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih)
ميل (mayl)
ترجيح (tarjih)
إرادة (irada)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument proves that all choices are determined and we have no free will.
Correction: Razi only shows that choice requires an internal inclination, not that the inclination is coercive or that we are not agents.

Checkpoint
According to the empirical argument, what is necessary for a person to choose one of two equal alternatives?
A) External compulsion
B) A specific inclination in the heart
C) Infinite regress of volitions
D) Mere possibility

Key Takeaway
Every voluntary action requires an internal specific inclination, which serves as the preponderator, and this inclination ultimately traces back to God or celestial causes.

Two Proofs Against the Identity of Future and Present Knowledge
Razi states the claim and gives two proofs against identity of future and present knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two arguments showing that knowledge of a future event cannot be identical to knowledge of its present occurrence.

Core Idea
Future knowledge and present knowledge are not interchangeable; they differ in conditions and epistemic status.

This passage addresses a subtle theological question: is knowing that something will happen the same as knowing it is happening now? Razi argues they are distinct. The first proof uses the criterion of substitutability; the second uses the difference in conditions. Both lead to the conclusion that the two knowledges are different in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and the two proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the claim is stated, then 'The First Proof' and 'The Second Proof' follow.
The claim is what Razi is arguing against; his proofs aim to refute it.
2
Understand the first proof: interchangeability
The first proof says that if two things are identical, they must be able to replace each other. Show that before the event, one belief is knowledge and the other ignorance; at the time of the event, the reverse is true. Thus they are not interchangeable, so not identical.
Note the time-sensitive nature of the argument.
3
Understand the second proof: conditions
The second proof notes that knowledge of future occurrence is not conditioned on the event happening now, while knowledge of present occurrence is. Since one is conditioned and the other is not, they cannot be the same thing.
This is a simpler logical argument based on necessary and sufficient conditions.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why this distinction matters in Islamic theology. It touches on divine knowledge: does Allah's foreknowledge differ from His knowledge of the present? Razi's reasoning shows a conceptual difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية عشرة: العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد هو نفس العلم بوجوده حال ما يصير موجوداً، وله أن يحتج على ذلك بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: أن من شرط المثلين أن يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر، والعلم بأن الشيء سيوجد لا يقوم مقام العلم بأنه موجود الآن، فإن قبل وقوع المعلوم لو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك، كان علماً، ولو اعتقدنا أنه واقع الآن كان جهلاً، وأما حال وقوعه فإنه ينقلب الأمر، فلو اعتقدنا بأنه سيقع بعد ذلك وأنه الآن غير واقع كان جهلا، ولو اعتقدنا أنه الآن واقع كان علمًا، فثبت أن كل واحد منهما لا يقوم مقام الآخر، وذلك يقتضي كون هذين الاعتقادين مختلفين في الحقيقة، ومع هذا الاختلاف في الماهية والحقيقة كيف يمكن دعوى الاتحاد؟ الحجة الثانية: أن كونه عالمًا بأنه سيقع غير مشروط بكونه واقعا في الحال، وكونه عالمًا بوقوعه مشروط بوقوعه في الحال والشيئان اللذان يكون أحدهما مشروطا بشيء والآخر لا يكون مشروطًا بذلك الشيء يمتنع أن يكون أحدهما نفس الآخر.
The Twelfth Question: The knowledge that a thing will exist is the same as the knowledge of its existence at the time it becomes existent. He can argue against that in several ways: The First Proof: From the condition of two similar things is that each one takes the place of the other, and knowledge that a thing will exist does not take the place of knowledge that it exists now. For before the occurrence of the known, if we believe that it will occur later, that is knowledge, and if we believe that it is occurring now, that is ignorance. But at the time of its occurrence, the matter reverses: if we believe that it will occur later and that it is not occurring now, that is ignorance, and if we believe that it is occurring now, that is knowledge. So it is established that each one does not take the place of the other, and that necessitates that these two beliefs are different in reality. With this difference in essence and reality, how can one claim identity? The Second Proof: That his being knowledgeable that it will occur is not conditioned by its being actual in the present, while his being knowledgeable of its occurrence is conditioned by its occurring in the present. Two things, one of which is conditioned by something and the other is not conditioned by that thing, it is impossible that one of them is the same as the other.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage sets out the twelfth question of the work: the claim that knowledge that something will exist is identical to knowledge of its existence when it actually occurs. The author then presents arguments against this claim. The first proof relies on the notion of substitution: for two things to be identical, they must be interchangeable. But belief about future occurrence and belief about present occurrence are not interchangeable; before the event, one is knowledge and the other ignorance, and at the time of the event, the situation reverses. Therefore they are different in essence. The second proof focuses on conditions: knowledge of future occurrence is unconditional, whereas knowledge of present occurrence requires the event to have actually happened. Since one is conditioned and the other is not, they cannot be the same thing.

Key Terms
العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد
العلم بوجوده حال ما يصير موجوداً
المثلين
مشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because both knowledges refer to the same event, they must be identical.
Correction: Razi shows that identity requires more than same referent; the epistemic conditions and roles differ, proving they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why cannot the two knowledges be identical?
A) Because before the event, believing it will occur is knowledge, while believing it is occurring now is ignorance.
B) Because knowledge of future is conditioned by the event's occurrence.
C) Because the two knowledges are both about the same thing.
D) Because the event cannot occur twice.

Key Takeaway
Razi's two proofs establish that knowledge of future existence and knowledge of present existence are distinct in essence due to differences in substitutability and conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been absolutely certain that something would happen in the future, only to realize later that your certainty was misplaced? How does Razi's proof that future knowledge and present knowledge are distinct help you guard against overconfidence in your own plans? Write down one area of your life where you are treating your anticipation as if it were already reality, and commit to acknowledging the difference.
?
Is there a promise you have made to yourself or to Allah that you assume will automatically be fulfilled? Razi shows that knowledge of future events is not conditioned by their actual occurrence, but knowledge of present events is. How does this distinction affect your sense of responsibility? Reflect on a current situation where you are relying on future knowledge (e.g., 'I will pray later') and ask yourself: are you treating that future knowledge as if it were present, and thereby neglecting the conditionality of action?
?
The proof about substitution shows that two beliefs cannot be identical if they are not interchangeable in all contexts. Think of a belief you hold about a future good deed (like giving charity next month) versus the actual act of giving. In your heart, do you treat the intention as equal to the deed? How can you use this lesson to make your good intentions more concrete, knowing that knowledge of future action and the action itself are distinct?

Abu al-Husayn's Proof: Future Knowledge vs. Present Knowledge
Razi presents Abu al-Husayn's scenario-based proof that future and present knowledge are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's scenario-based proof that knowledge of a future event is distinct from knowledge of the event when it occurs.

Core Idea
Future knowledge does not automatically become present knowledge.

The third proof in Razi's text relies on a simple but powerful thought experiment. Abu al-Husayn asks us to imagine someone who knows about a future event but is unable to perceive the passage of time. This scenario shows that the two states of knowledge are not the same. Let's examine how the argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
2
Understand the scenario
Imagine someone knows Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, but then sits in a dark room where he cannot perceive day or night. He retains that knowledge until Zayd actually enters.
The key detail is his inability to know when 'tomorrow' arrives.
3
Identify why he lacks present knowledge
Even though he knows the future event, he does not know that Zayd has entered because he doesn't know that 'tomorrow' has come. This shows the two knowledges are distinct.
4
Notice the refinement about a third knowledge
The passage adds that if he also knows that tomorrow has arrived, then a third knowledge (that Zayd is now entering) is generated from the two prior knowledges.
This reinforces that future knowledge alone is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: وهي التي عول عليها أبو الحسين فقال: مجرد العلم بأن الشيء سيقع، لا يكون علمًا بوقوعه إذا وقع، فإن من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم إنه جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار وبقي مستديما لذلك العلم حتى جاء الغد ودخل زيد البلد فههنا هذا الشخص بمجرد علمه بأن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا لا عالمًا بأنه دخل الآن في البلد، فثبت بهذا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد غدًا لا يكون نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. بلى من علم أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا ثم علم حصول الغد فحينئذ يتولد من هذين العلمين علم ثالث وهو العلم بأن زيدا دخل البلد الآن.
The Third Proof: It is the one on which Abu al-Husayn relied, and he said: Mere knowledge that a thing will occur is not knowledge of its occurrence when it occurs. For if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, then he sits in a dark room where he cannot distinguish between night and day, and he continues to hold that knowledge until tomorrow comes and Zayd enters the city, then this person, merely by his knowledge that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, is not knowledgeable that he has now entered the city. So it is established by this that the knowledge that a thing will exist tomorrow is not the same as the knowledge of its existence when it exists. Rather, if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and then knows the arrival of tomorrow, then from these two knowledges a third knowledge is generated, which is the knowledge that Zayd has now entered the city.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. The proof is a thought experiment: a person knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, but then he sits in a dark room where he cannot perceive the passage of time. He retains that knowledge until Zayd actually enters. Despite having the initial future knowledge, he does not know that Zayd has now entered because he is unaware that the time has arrived. This shows that the two knowledges are distinct. The author then clarifies that if the person also knows that tomorrow has arrived (i.e., he has a second piece of knowledge), then a third knowledge is generated—the knowledge that Zayd is now entering. Thus future knowledge alone does not suffice for present knowledge.

Key Terms
مُجَرَّدُ الْعِلْمِ
عِلْمٌ ثَالِثٌ
يَتَوَلَّدُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Future knowledge automatically becomes present knowledge over time without any additional awareness.
Correction: The scenario shows that without awareness that the time has arrived, future knowledge does not translate into present knowledge. Additional knowledge of the present time is needed.

Checkpoint
In Abu al-Husayn's example, why does the person not know that Zayd has entered the city?
A) Because he is in a dark room and cannot see Zayd.
B) Because he only has the knowledge that Zayd will enter, but lacks knowledge that the time has arrived.
C) Because he forgot his original knowledge.
D) Because Zayd never entered the city.

Key Takeaway
Future knowledge and present knowledge are two different types of knowledge; one does not imply the other without additional awareness of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so certain that something would happen that you stopped paying attention to the moment it actually did? What did you miss because of that assumption?
?
Is there an area of your Islamic practice where you rely on past knowledge or habits instead of actively renewing your awareness? Where do you need to 'know the arrival of tomorrow'—to seek fresh signs or reminders?
?
The proof shows that future knowledge alone does not make you present to what is happening now. In your relationship with Allah, are you living on future hopes (like promises of Paradise) without the present knowledge of His closeness and your own state? What would it take for you to 'generate' that third knowledge?

Fourth Proof: Correspondence of Knowledge and Object
Razi's fourth proof from correspondence theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the correspondence theory of knowledge leads to the conclusion that knowledges of different temporal modalities are distinct.

Core Idea
Knowledge corresponds to its object; different objects require different knowledges.

Imam Razi presents the fourth proof for the distinctness of knowledges. He begins with a foundational epistemological principle: knowledge is a mental form that matches its object. From this, he argues that since the object 'something will occur in the future' is different from the object 'something is occurring now', the knowledges corresponding to them must also be different. This proof is a direct application of the correspondence theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the principle
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the opening statement that defines knowledge as a corresponding form.
This is the key premise.
2
Understand the two objects
Razi contrasts two objects: 'it will occur in the future' and 'it is occurring now'. Recognize that they are different in reality.
3
Follow the conclusion
Since the objects differ, the knowledges must differ. Ask yourself why this conclusion follows from the principle.
This is a deductive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: أن العلم بالشيء صورة مطابقة لذلك الشيء ولا شك أن حقيقة الآن غير واقع - مغايرة لحقيقة أنه واقع في الحال وحاصل، وهو أنه سيقع بعد ذلك - وإذا اختلفت المعلومات وجب اختلاف العلمين. الحجة الخامسة: وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعلم كونه عالما بأن الشيء الفلاني سيقع غدا حال ما نجهل كونه عالما بوقوعه حال وقوعه ولما حصل العلم بأحد هذين العلمين حال ما حصل الشك في حصول العلم الآخر علمنا تغاير العلم.
The Fourth Proof: Knowledge of a thing is a form that corresponds to that thing, and there is no doubt that the reality of 'it will occur in the future' is different from the reality of 'it is occurring in the present and actual', and it is that it will occur later. And when the known objects differ, the two knowledges must differ. The Fifth Proof: It is that we can know that someone is knowledgeable that a certain thing will occur tomorrow while we are ignorant of his being knowledgeable of its occurrence at the time of its occurrence. And when knowledge of one of these two knowledges occurs while doubt occurs about the occurrence of the other knowledge, we know the distinctness of the two knowledges.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth proofs. The fourth proof is based on the correspondence theory of knowledge: knowledge is a mental form that matches its object. The object 'something will occur in the future' is different in reality from 'something is occurring now'. Therefore the knowledges corresponding to these different objects must also be different. The fifth proof is epistemic: we can know that someone knows that something will happen tomorrow while at the same time we are uncertain whether he knows that it is happening now. Since we can have knowledge of one while doubting the other, they cannot be identical.

Key Terms
صورة مطابقة
المعلومات
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why must the knowledge of a future event be different from the knowledge of a present event?
A) Because the future event does not exist yet.
B) Because knowledge is a form that matches its object, and the objects are different.
C) Because we cannot have knowledge of the future.
D) Because the mind cannot hold both knowledges at once.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof uses the correspondence theory of knowledge to show that different temporal modalities of objects necessitate distinct knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you anticipate a future event, you act as if your knowledge of it is certain. But after it occurs, do you recognize that your earlier 'knowledge' was actually different? Is it honest to call it knowledge at all? Will you stop pretending to know what you cannot yet know?
?
Your certainty about your own future actions—like a plan to pray consistently or quit a sin—rests on assuming the future is like the present. This proof shows the object of knowledge changes. Do you rely on your own plans more than on Allah's decree? How can you humble your heart before this reality today?
?
Think of a past moment when you insisted you 'knew' something would happen, yet it didn't. Did that experience reveal a gap between your claim and true knowledge? Will you now let this proof teach you to speak only of what corresponds to the actual object? What is one area where you need to stop claiming knowledge you do not have?

Fifth Proof: Epistemic Asymmetry
Razi's fifth proof from epistemic asymmetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how our ability to know that someone knows a future event while doubting their knowledge of its present occurrence demonstrates that the two knowledges are distinct.

Core Idea
We can know one knowledge while doubting another, proving they are distinct.

Imam Razi's fifth proof takes an epistemic approach. He points out that we can be certain that someone knows that something will happen tomorrow, while at the same time we are unsure whether that same person knows that it is happening now. This asymmetry in our own knowledge about those knowledges shows that the two knowledges are not identical. The proof relies on the fact that if they were the same, we could not have knowledge of one while doubting the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the scenario: someone knows about a future event, but we doubt their knowledge about the present occurrence.
2
Identify the asymmetry
We have definite knowledge of one (future knowledge) and doubt about the other (present knowledge). Ask: what does this tell us?
This is the key evidence.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since we can have knowledge of one while doubting the other, the two knowledges cannot be the same. They must be distinct.
This is a straightforward inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: أن العلم بالشيء صورة مطابقة لذلك الشيء ولا شك أن حقيقة الآن غير واقع - مغايرة لحقيقة أنه واقع في الحال وحاصل، وهو أنه سيقع بعد ذلك - وإذا اختلفت المعلومات وجب اختلاف العلمين. الحجة الخامسة: وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعلم كونه عالما بأن الشيء الفلاني سيقع غدا حال ما نجهل كونه عالما بوقوعه حال وقوعه ولما حصل العلم بأحد هذين العلمين حال ما حصل الشك في حصول العلم الآخر علمنا تغاير العلم.
The Fourth Proof: Knowledge of a thing is a form that corresponds to that thing, and there is no doubt that the reality of 'it will occur in the future' is different from the reality of 'it is occurring in the present and actual', and it is that it will occur later. And when the known objects differ, the two knowledges must differ. The Fifth Proof: It is that we can know that someone is knowledgeable that a certain thing will occur tomorrow while we are ignorant of his being knowledgeable of its occurrence at the time of its occurrence. And when knowledge of one of these two knowledges occurs while doubt occurs about the occurrence of the other knowledge, we know the distinctness of the two knowledges.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth proofs. The fourth proof is based on the correspondence theory of knowledge: knowledge is a mental form that matches its object. The object 'something will occur in the future' is different in reality from 'something is occurring now'. Therefore the knowledges corresponding to these different objects must also be different. The fifth proof is epistemic: we can know that someone knows that something will happen tomorrow while at the same time we are uncertain whether he knows that it is happening now. Since we can have knowledge of one while doubting the other, they cannot be identical.

Key Takeaway

Answering Objections with Divine Transcendence
Razi dismisses a second objection as mere astonishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi dismisses certain objections about divine attributes as mere astonishment and affirms that divine perfection surpasses human intellect.

Core Idea
Divine majesty transcends human intellect; astonishment is not a valid argument.

Razi continues his defense of divine attributes by addressing a second objection. Rather than engaging in complex logical refutation, he points out that the objection stems from human astonishment at infinity, which carries no weight against Allah's attributes. This lesson shows how Razi upholds divine transcendence by reminding us of the limits of human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for Razi's concise style. Notice the key terms like 'نسب' and 'إضافات'.
2
Understand the objection being answered
The objection apparently concerns the possibility of infinite relations in God's attributes. Razi dismisses it as mere astonishment (محض التعجب).
The fuller context of the objection is not given, but Razi's answer is clear.
3
Analyze Razi's argument strategy
Razi does not disprove the objection logically; instead, he invokes the transcendence of Allah and the limitation of human intellect. This is a theological move, not a philosophical one.
This reflects the Ash'ari emphasis on divine incomprehensibility.
4
Reflect on the closing doxology
The passage ends with a statement of humility: 'This is what the weak intellect has reached...' Reflect on how this frames the entire discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير متناهية والتعلقات من باب النسب والإضافات، ودخول ما لا نهاية له فيها غير كما ضربنا في المثال من الوحدة المشتملة على النسب والإضافات التي لا نهاية لها. والجواب عن الوجه الثاني : أنها محض التعجب ولا عبرة بذلك في صفات الله تعالى؛ فإن كمالها وجلالها أعظم من أن يحيط به عقول البشر. وهذا ما انتهى إليه العقل الضعيف وجلال الله تعالى منزه عن غايات عقول العقلاء، ونهايات علوم العلماء، وبالله التوفيق.
...non-finite and the relations are of the category of relative matters and additions, and the inclusion of what is infinite in them is not impossible, as we gave in the example of the unit that includes relations and additions that have no end. And the answer to the second aspect: It is mere astonishment and there is no consideration of that in the attributes of Allah the Most High; for His perfection and majesty are greater than to be encompassed by the intellects of humans. This is what the weak intellect has reached, and the majesty of Allah the Most High is exalted above the goals of the intellects of the intelligent and the ends of the knowledge of the learned. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the second objection in the twelfth issue. The objection itself appears to argue that infinite relations are impossible, but Razi dismisses it as mere astonishment that has no weight against Allah's attributes. He emphasizes that divine perfection and majesty transcend human intellectual capacity. Therefore, such objections are invalid. The passage concludes with a statement that human intellect is weak and Allah's glory is beyond the limits of rational minds and sciences.

Key Terms

إضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an attribute seems impossible to human reason, it must be impossible for God.
Correction: Razi teaches that human intellect is limited; divine perfection surpasses what we can conceive. Astonishment at infinity is not a valid argument against God's attributes.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's answer to the second objection about divine attributes?
A) It is a valid objection.
B) It is mere astonishment with no weight.
C) It requires further proof.
D) It applies only to created beings.

Key Takeaway
Razi dismisses objections based on human astonishment by affirming that Allah's majesty transcends the limits of human intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine attribute that baffles your mind, do you react with astonishment and doubt, or with humility and submission? What is one attribute you personally struggle with, and will you today accept it as true despite not fully comprehending it?
?
Is there a part of your faith that you have rejected or questioned simply because it seemed incomprehensible? How does Razi's response challenge you to change that stance?
?
In your daily life, do you allow the limitations of your intellect to dictate what you believe about Allah? Will you commit to trusting His majesty over your own reasoning, starting now?

Mental Judgment Is Distinct from Knowledge and Belief
Razi proves that mental judgment is distinct from knowledge and belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that mental judgment is a distinct cognitive act from knowledge and belief, and why this distinction is foundational for the concept of inner speech.

Core Idea
You can mentally judge something false while knowing it's true—so judgment is not knowledge.

In Islamic theology, the concept of 'inner speech' (al-khabar al-qa'im bi al-nafs) is crucial for understanding how God speaks. But before we can discuss that, Razi clarifies a fundamental point: the mental act of judging a proposition (e.g., 'the world is eternal') is not the same as knowing or believing it. In this passage, he offers a simple but powerful proof that these are distinct categories, using an example anyone can relate to.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument without overanalyzing yet.
The Arabic is philosophical but clear.
2
Identify the claim and the proof
Find where Razi states his claim that mental judgment is distinct, and then locate the core proof using the world-eternal example.
3
Understand the false proposition example
Razi says when you know the world is not eternal, you can still mentally judge 'the world is eternal'. This false judgment occurs without knowledge or belief of that falsehood.
This is the key insight.
4
Consider the objection and Razi's response
Someone might object that this is just assumption or estimation. Razi replies that even if so, assumption is still not knowledge, belief, will, or power, so it remains distinct.
Notice how Razi handles the objection concisely.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this distinction matters for theology, especially for understanding God's speech as a mental act.
This prepares for later lessons on inner speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الذهني هو الاعتقاد أو العلم، فإذا بينا بالبرهان أنه ليس الأمر كذلك ظهر أن الخبر القائم بالنفس معنى مغاير للعلوم والاعتقادات ومغاير للقدر والإرادات وذلك هو المراد من الخبر القائم بالنفس.
وإنما قلنا: إن هذا الحكم الذهني ليس من جنس العلوم والاعتقادات، وذلك لأني حال ما أكون عالمًا بأن العالم ليس بقديم يمكنني أن أقول في الذهن : العالم قديم، وذلك لأن الذهن كما يمكنه تركيب القضايا الصادقة فكذلك يمكنه تركيب القضايا الكاذبة، والقضايا الكاذبة الذهنية يكون ذلك الحكم الكاذب فيها حاصلا في الذهن، والعلم بها والاعتقاد فيها غير حاصل فههنا الكلام في القضايا الكاذبة التي يكون كذبها معلوما، حصل الحكم الذهني، ولم يحصل العلم والاعتقاد، وهذا يدل قطعًا على أن الحكم الذهني مغاير للعلم والاعتقاد.
فإن قلنا : هذا الحكم الذهني عبارة عن فرض يفرضه الذهن، وتقدير بقدره. قلنا : هب أن الأمر على ما قلتم إلا أن هذا الفرض وهذا التقدير ليس من باب العلوم والاعتقادات ولا من باب القدر والإرادات فكان معنى مغايرا لها وهو المطلوب، وذلك لا يختلف بأن سميتموه فرضًا وتقديرًا، أو لا تسمونه بذلك، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الطلب الذهني مغاير للإرادة وأن الحكم الذهني مغاير للعلم والاعتقاد.
ومن أنصف علم أن هذا التقرير والتلخيص لم يتيسر ممن تقدمنا.
The mental judgment is belief or knowledge. If we prove by demonstration that it is not the case, then it becomes clear that the speech subsisting in the self (al-khabar al-qa'im bi al-nafs) is a meaning distinct from the sciences and beliefs and distinct from power and wills, and that is what is intended by the speech subsisting in the self.
We say: this mental judgment is not from the genus of sciences and beliefs, because when I am knowing that the world is not eternal, I can say in the mind: the world is eternal. This is because the mind can combine true propositions just as it can combine false propositions, and in false mental propositions, that false judgment occurs in the mind, while knowledge of them and belief in them are not present. So here the speech concerns false propositions whose falsity is known. The mental judgment occurred, but knowledge and belief did not occur. This indicates decisively that the mental judgment is different from knowledge and belief.
If it is said: This mental judgment is an assumption (farḍ) that the mind assumes, and an estimation (taqdīr) that it estimates. We say: Granted that the matter is as you say, but this assumption and this estimation are not from the category of sciences and beliefs nor from the category of power and wills, so it is a meaning different from them, and that is what is sought. This does not differ whether you call it assumption and estimation or you do not call it that. Thus it is established by what we mentioned that the mental request (al-ṭalab al-dhihnī) is different from will, and that the mental judgment is different from knowledge and belief.
Whoever is fair knows that this exposition and summary was not achieved by those before us.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a proof that mental judgment (al-ḥukm al-dhihnī) is distinct from knowledge and belief. Razi begins by stating the claim: mental judgment is not the same as belief or knowledge; rather, the speech subsisting in the self (al-khabar al-qa'im bi al-nafs) is a meaning different from sciences, beliefs, power, and wills. He then provides a proof: when I know that the world is not eternal, I can still mentally form the judgment 'the world is eternal'—a false proposition. Since the mind can combine false propositions, the false judgment occurs in the mind, but knowledge and belief of that false proposition are absent (because knowledge requires truth). Thus mental judgment is clearly distinct from knowledge and belief. Then Razi addresses an objection: someone might say this mental judgment is merely an assumption (farḍ) or estimation (taqdīr). Razi replies that even if it were, it still does not belong to the categories of sciences, beliefs, power, or wills, so it remains a distinct meaning. The conclusion is that mental request is distinct from will, and mental judgment is distinct from knowledge and belief. Razi ends by noting that this precise exposition was not achieved by earlier scholars, emphasizing the novelty of his analysis.

Key Terms
حكم ذهني
خبر قائم بالنفس
فرض
تقدير
طلب ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental judgment is the same as belief, but Razi shows it can be false while belief tends toward truth.
Correction: Mental judgment is a cognitive act that can occur even when the proposition is known to be false, whereas belief requires at least some degree of assent to truth. Thus they are distinct.

Checkpoint
How does Razi prove that mental judgment is different from knowledge?
A) By showing that one can judge falsely while knowing better.
B) By citing scripture.
C) By defining terms.
D) By consensus.

Key Takeaway
Mental judgment is a distinct cognitive act that can be false even when knowledge and belief are absent, proving it is not reducible to them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally entertain a sinful thought that you know is wrong, do you treat that thought as merely a mental judgment distinct from your actual beliefs, or do you feel guilty as if you believed it? How does Razi's distinction between mental judgment and belief change how you should view your own inner thoughts?
?
In your daily life, you often mentally imagine scenarios that you know are false (e.g., worst-case fears). According to Razi, these mental judgments are not knowledge or belief. So why do they still affect your emotions? What does this reveal about the power of mental judgments beyond truth?
?
Razi says that mental judgment is a distinct meaning not reducible to knowledge, belief, power, or will. Consider a time you mentally judged something false while knowing it was false (like a doubt about your faith). Did you consider that judgment to be part of your faith or separate? How does Razi's analysis help you address such doubts without feeling they contradict your knowledge?

Introduction to the Proof from Existence
Razi introduces the proof from existence and states its initial premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial structure of the Ash'ari proof from existence (dalīl al-wujūd) for the beatific vision, as presented and critiqued by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Visibility of created things points to a shared cause—either existence or temporality.

In this chapter, Razi turns to the rational proofs that Ash'ari theologians have used to argue that God can be seen. He identifies the 'proof from existence' as the most common, though he himself finds it difficult to defend. We will examine the opening moves of this proof: the observation that substances and colors share the property of visibility, which must have a common cause.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's opening remarks
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice Razi's personal stance: he says the majority rely on this proof but he is unable to sustain it.
Razi distances himself from the proof.
2
Identify the shared property
The proof begins with substances and colors both being visible. Identify that this shared property (visibility) is a contingent ruling (ḥukm ḥādith) that requires a cause.
3
Understand the causal principle
Recognize the principle: a shared ruling must have a shared cause. Why? Because equal effects cannot come from different causes. This is a logical principle in kalam.
This principle is crucial for the proof.
4
Determine the possible shared causes
The shared cause is either temporality (ḥudūth) or existence (wujūd). This sets up the next step of the argument, which Razi will later critique.
The passage ends here, so the lesson concludes with this identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في حكاية ما قيل في هذه المسألة من الدلائل العقلية وذكر المباحث فيها. اعلم أن جمهور الأصحاب عولوا في إثبات أنه تعالى يصح أن يرى على دليل الوجود، وأما نحن فعاجزون عن تمشيه ونحن نذكر ذلك الدليل ثم نوجه عليه ما عندنا من الاعتراضات. قالوا: ثبت أن الجوهر يصح أن يرى، واللون يصح أن يرى، والجواهر والألوان تشتركان في صحة الرؤية، وهذه الصحة حكم حادث، فلا بد لها من علة، والحكم المشترك يجب تعليله بعلة مشتركة لامتناع الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة، والمشترك بين الجواهر والأعراض إما الحدوث وإما الوجود،
The second chapter: Concerning the narration of what has been said in this issue of rational proofs and mentioning the investigations therein. Know that the majority of our companions (al-aṣḥāb) relied in establishing that He, exalted, can be seen upon the proof from existence (dalīl al-wujūd). As for us, we are unable to sustain it. We will mention that proof and then direct against it what we have of objections. They said: It is established that a substance (jawhar) can be seen, and a color (lawn) can be seen, and substances and colors share in the validity of vision. This validity is a contingent ruling (ḥukm ḥādith), so it must have a cause (ʿilla). A shared ruling must be attributed to a shared cause, because equal rulings cannot be attributed to different causes. What is shared between substances and accidents is either temporality (ḥudūth) or existence (wujūd).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (chapter two) where Razi will present and critique rational proofs for the visibility of God. He states that the majority of Ash'ari scholars (al-aṣḥāb) rely on the 'proof from existence' (dalīl al-wujūd). However, Razi himself says he is unable to sustain (i.e., fully defend) that proof. Nevertheless, he will narrate it and then present his objections. The proof begins: substances (jawāhir) and colors (alwān) are seen. They share the property of being visible. This shared property is a contingent (ḥādith) ruling requiring a cause. Since a shared ruling must have a shared cause (to avoid equal effects from different causes), the common factor between substances and accidents must be either temporality (ḥudūth) or existence (wujūd). The passage cuts off at this point, but the argument is clearly directed toward establishing that existence (or temporality) is the cause of visibility, and then applying it to God. Razi's commentary will later critique this.

جوهر
عرض
حكم حادث
علة
حدوث
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi endorses the proof from existence.
Correction: Razi actually distances himself from it, saying he cannot sustain it ('Ammā naḥnu fa-ʿājizūn ʿan tamshīhi).

Checkpoint
What is the 'proof from existence' (dalīl al-wujūd) according to the Ash'ari majority as described by Razi?
A) It proves that God exists from the existence of creation.
B) It argues that visibility is a property shared by substances and colors, and the shared cause is existence or temporality.
C) It relies on the impossibility of infinite regress.
D) It is a proof based on divine names.

Key Takeaway
Razi introduces the proof from existence, which argues that the shared visibility of substances and colors must be caused by either existence or temporality, but he does not endorse it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi admits he cannot sustain the proof from existence, yet he narrates it honestly. When have you held onto a belief that you could not rationally defend? Did you suppress your doubts or admit them? How does Razi's intellectual honesty challenge your own approach to faith and reason?
?
The proof assumes that shared visibility between substances and colors must have a shared cause. Look at your own life: what shared experiences do you have with others that point to a common source? What does that reveal about your dependence on a single Creator? Are you willing to follow that conclusion wherever it leads, even if it unsettles you?

The Third Objection on the Shared Judgment of Visibility
Razi presents and refutes the third objection about the shared judgment of visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the shared judgment of visibility and Razi's response that generic unity does not necessitate a single cause.

Core Idea
Sharing a genus does not force sharing a cause.

Razi now addresses the third objection: the claim that the validity of vision is not truly shared between substance and accident. The objection points out that the validity of black being seen differs from white being seen, so it's not a single judgment. Razi's response uses the example of attributes like motion and knowledge to show that judgments differing in species can have different causes despite a shared genus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. The objector concedes that the validity of vision is a fixed judgment but denies it is truly shared because the validity of black differs from that of white. Notice the proof given: each cannot replace the other.
The objector argues that if they were identical, they would be interchangeable.
2
Analyze Razi's response
Read Razi's answer. He rejects the idea that generic unity forces a single cause. He uses the example of motion/rest and knowledge/power: these differ in species but are all attributes, yet each has a different cause in quiddity.
This shows that different species can have different causes despite sharing a genus.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument defends the possibility that the visibility of substance and accident could have different causes, even if they share the generic judgment of being visible. This supports the overall discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون علة، ولا أن يكون جزء علة، فثبت أن هذا الحكم المشترك فيه بين القدرة غير معلل بشيء أصلا. السؤال الثالث : هذه سلمنا أن صحة الرؤية حكم ثابت وإن كان حكمًا عدميًا، لكن الحكم العدمي يجب تعليله، لكن لا نسلم أن صحة الرؤية حكم مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض، وذلك لأن صحة كون السواد [مرتبًا؟] مخالفة لصحة كون البياض مرئياً، والدليل عليه أنه يمتنع قيام كل واحدة من هاتين الصحتين مقام الأخرى، بدليل أن الجوهر يمتنع أن يرى سوادا، والسواد يمتنع أن يرى جوهرًا، ولو تساوت الصحتان لقامت كل واحدة منهما مقام الأخرى. لا يقال : هب ان هاتين الصحتين نوعان مختلفان في النوعية، لكنهما مشتركان في جنس واحد وهو كونه صحة الرؤية وهذا الحكم واحد بحسب الوحدة والجنسية، وحينئذ يعود التقريب. لأنا نقول: إنه لا نزاع في أن الأحكام المختلفة بحسب النوعية وإن كانت متحدة في الجنس فإنه يجوز تعليلها بعلل مختلفة في الماهية والدليل عليه أن المتحركية والساكنية والعالمية والقادرية وإن كانت مختلفة بحسب الوحدة النوعية لكنها متحدة بحسب الوحدة الجنسية، فإنها بأسرها صفات وأحوال، ثم إنها مع تعلل بعلل مختلفة في الماهية والحقيقة، فثبت أن هذا غير ممتنع.
That it is a cause, nor that it is part of a cause, so it is established that this judgment shared between the power is not caused by anything at all. The third question: This: we concede that the validity of vision is a fixed judgment, even if it is a privative judgment, but the privative judgment must be caused, but we do not concede that the validity of vision is a judgment shared between substance and accident, because the validity of black being [perceived?] differs from the validity of white being seen, and the proof of that is that it is impossible for each of these two validities to take the place of the other, as evidenced by the fact that substance cannot be seen as black, and black cannot be seen as substance, and had the two validities been equal, each one of them would have taken the place of the other. It cannot be said: Suppose these two validities are different in species, but they share a common genus, namely being the validity of vision, and this judgment is one in terms of generic unity, and then the argument returns. We say: There is no dispute that judgments that differ in species, even if they are united in genus, may be caused by causes that differ in quiddity, and the proof of that is that moving and being stationary, being knowing and being powerful, even if they differ in terms of specific unity, they are united in terms of generic unity, for they are all attributes and states, yet they are caused by causes that differ in quiddity and reality, so it is established that this is not impossible.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (السؤال الثالث) and its answer within Razi's discussion on whether the shared judgment of visibility requires a single cause. The objection begins by conceding that the validity of vision (صحة الرؤية) is a fixed judgment, even if privative, but denies that it is truly shared between substance (جوهر) and accident (عرض). The objector argues that the validity of black being perceived is different from the validity of white being seen, as evidenced by the fact that each cannot replace the other: substance cannot be seen as black, and black cannot be seen as substance. If the two validities were identical, they would be interchangeable.
A counter-claim is anticipated: that although they differ in species, they share a genus (جنس واحد) — namely, being the validity of vision — and thus the genus judgment is one, reviving the argument for a shared cause. Razi's answer (لأنا نقول) rejects this by pointing out that it is universally accepted that judgments differing in species can be caused by causes differing in quiddity (ماهية), even if they share a genus. He provides the example of attributes like motion/rest and knowledge/power: they differ in species but are all attributes (صفات) and states (أحوال), yet each is caused by distinct quiddities. Therefore, the fact that two visibility validities share a genus does not force them to have a single cause; having different causes is not impossible.

Key Terms

حكم مشترك
تعليل
علة
جوهر
عرض
ماهية
نوعية
جنسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two judgments share a genus, they must have a single cause.
Correction: Razi shows that judgments differing in species can have different causes despite generic unity, as exemplified by attributes like motion/rest and knowledge/power.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the third objection regarding the validity of vision?
A) The validity of vision is a shared judgment between substance and accident.
B) The validity of vision is not a fixed judgment.
C) The validity of black and white are not identical, so the judgment is not truly shared.
D) All judgments must be caused by a single cause.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that generic unity does not entail a single cause; judgments differing in species can have distinct causes in quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that two things that share a label (e.g., 'good deed') must have the same root cause or motivation, and thus judged someone unfairly? How does Razi's argument challenge you to examine your own assumptions about causes and categories?
?
When you reflect on your understanding of God's attributes, do you realize that you might have been oversimplifying by assuming that because two attributes share a genus (e.g., 'knowledge' and 'power' both being attributes), they must have the same type of effect? How will you correct this assumption in your study of theology?
?
This lesson confronts you with the subtlety of logical reasoning in matters of faith. Are you willing to engage with such detailed arguments rather than relying on comfortable generalizations? What concrete step will you take to deepen your understanding of a specific theological issue?
