Introduction and First Proof: Shared Concomitants
Razi states the objection and gives the first of four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that different things can share equal concomitants, showing that similar judgments do not require identical causes.

Core Idea
Different things can share the same property without sharing a cause.

Razi opens the fourth objection by conceding that the two visibility judgments are similar, but he questions why their causes must be the same. He then presents his first proof: two different things, like a substance and an accident, share the property of being different from each other. This shows that equal concomitants can arise from distinct sources, laying the groundwork for the subsequent arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first proof
Read the Arabic passage for the objection and first proof. Notice how Razi frames the problem: the objector assumes similar judgments need similar causes, but Razi challenges that.
The first proof uses the example of 'difference' itself being a shared concept between two different things.
2
Understand the logic of sharing
Razi argues that two different things can share a 'concomitant' (lazim). Think of how two opposites both have the property of being opposite to each other. That property is shared even though the things themselves are different.
3
Connect to the main issue
Reflect: if difference itself can be shared, then the similarity of two judgments (like visibility) does not prove that their causes must be similar. The cause could be different.
This is the key point Razi is making against the objector's assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: هب ذلك أن صحة رؤية الجوهر وصحة رؤية العرض حكمان متماثلان لكن لم لا يجوز تعليل الحكمين المتماثلين بعلتين مختلفتين ؟ والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أن الأشياء المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لوازم متساوية، والدليل عليه أن المختلفين يشتركان في صحة الاختلاف فإنه كما أن هذا مخالف لذلك فذاك مخالف لهذا، فمسمى الاختلاف مشترك فيه وكذا القول في الضدين الثاني : وهو أن السواد بخصوص كونه سوادًا يصح أن يكون معلوما، وكذا البياض بخصوص كونه بياضًا يصح أن يكون معلوماً، وهذه أحكام متساوية معللة بخصوصيات هذه الماهيات، مختلفة. وهي الثالث : وهو أن الماهيتين إذا اشتركتا من وجه اختلفا من وجه آخر، ولا شك أن ما به التشابه مغاير لما به الامتياز. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن لا تكون بين الاعتبارين ملازمة أصلا، وإما أن يكون كل واحد منهما مستلزما للآخر، وإما أن يكون ما به المشاركة مستلزما لما به الممايزة، وإما أن يكون بالعكس. والقسم الأول باطل، وإلا لانفك كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين عن الآخر، وحينئذ لا يحصل من مجموعهما حقيقة واحدة هذا ،خلف والثاني والثالث أيضًا باطل، إذ لو كان ما به الاشتراك مستلزما لما به الامتياز لما كان ما به الامتياز موجبا للمايزة، وذلك محال، فلم يبق إلا القسم الرابع، وهو أن يكون ما به الممايزة مستلزما لما به المشاركة. وهذا يقتضي الجزم بتعليل الأشياء المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة. الرابع لو وجب تعليل الحكم المشترك بوصف مشترك لكان صحة كونه موصوفًا بذلك الوصف المشترك، معللة بوصف آخر مشترك فيه، والكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال، فعلمنا أنه لا بد من انتهاء تعليل الأحكام المشتركة إلى خصوصيات الماهيات وحينئذ يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالماهيات المختلفة. لا يقال : الدليل على أن تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة لا يجوز، وذلك لأن هذا الحكم لما كان معللا بهذه العلة لذاته، والحكم الثاني مساويا للحكم الأول في الماهية وللمتساويان في الماهية يجب استواؤهما في اللوازم، فيلزم أن يكون الحكم الثاني مستندا إلى ما يماثل تلك العلة فثبت أن الحكمين المتساويين يجب تعليلهما بعلتين متماثلتين. لأنا نقول : إن صح هذا الكلام لزم تعليل جميع الأحكام المتساوية بعلة واحدة بالشخص ؛ لأن كون هذا الحكم معللا بهذه العلة المعينة أمر ثيت له لذاته، والحكم المتماثلة له مساو له في تمام الماهية ، فيلزم في مثل ذلك الحكم أن يكون معللا بعين تلك العلة، وبالاتفاق هذا باطل، وكذا ما ذكرتموه. ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إسناد هذا الحكم إلى هذه العلة ليس متولدا من ذات الحكم، بل العلة المخصوصة لذاتها توجب ذلك الحكم ، ولما حصل ذلك الحكم بتلك العلة وجب إسناده إلى تلك العلة لا لأن ذاته يوجب الإسناد إليها ، بل لأجل أن تكون العلة مستلزمة له، فوجب إسناده إليها، وهذا كلام دقيق لا بد من التأمل فيه.
The fourth question: Suppose that the validity of seeing substance and the validity of seeing accident are two similar judgments, but why is it not permissible to cause the two similar judgments by two different causes? That which indicates this are aspects: The first: That different things are not prevented from sharing equal concomitants, and the evidence for that is that two different things share in the validity of difference, for just as this one is contrary to that, so that one is contrary to this, so the meaning of difference is shared, and likewise for two opposites. The second: That black, by virtue of being black, is valid to be known, and likewise white, by virtue of being white, is valid to be known, and these are equal judgments caused by the particularities of these different quiddities. The third: That when two quiddities share in one respect, they differ in another respect, and there is no doubt that that by which they are similar is other than that by which they are distinguished. Once this is established, we say: Either there is no entailment between the two considerations at all, or each one entails the other, or that by which they share entails that by which they differ, or the opposite. The first division is false, for then each of the two considerations would be separate from the other, and then from their combination no single reality would result – this is a contradiction. The second and third are also false, because if that by which they share entailed that by which they differ, then that by which they differ would not be a cause of differentiation, which is impossible. So nothing remains except the fourth division, which is that that by which they differ entails that by which they share. This necessitates the certainty of causing equal things by different causes. The fourth: If the shared judgment must be caused by a shared attribute, then the validity of its being described by that shared attribute would be caused by another shared attribute, and the argument for it is the same as the first, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. So we know that the causation of shared judgments must terminate at the particularities of quiddities, and then it follows that equal judgments are caused by different quiddities. It cannot be said: The evidence that causing equal judgments by different causes is not permissible is that when this judgment is caused by this cause by its essence, and the second judgment is equal to the first in quiddity, and two things equal in quiddity must be equal in their concomitants, so it follows that the second judgment is based on something similar to that cause, so it is established that two equal judgments must be caused by two similar causes. We say: If this statement is sound, it would necessitate causing all equal judgments by one identical cause, because this judgment being caused by this particular cause is a fixed attribute of it by its essence, and the judgment similar to it is equal to it in the entirety of quiddity, so it would follow that such a judgment must be caused by that same cause, and by agreement this is false, and likewise what you have mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: The attribution of this judgment to this cause does not arise from the essence of the judgment, but rather the particular cause by its essence necessitates that judgment, and when that judgment occurs by that cause, its attribution to that cause becomes necessary, not because its essence necessitates attribution to it, but because the cause is entailing of it, so its attribution to it becomes necessary. This is a subtle point that must be contemplated.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (السؤال الرابع) and its detailed answer. The objector concedes that the two visibility judgments are similar (متماثلان) but asks why they cannot be caused by two different causes. Razi then provides four proofs (وجوه) that similar judgments can indeed have different causes.

The first proof: Different things can share equal concomitants (لوازم متساوية). For example, two different things share the property of being different from each other, so 'difference' is a shared concept, and similarly for opposites. Thus, different quiddities can produce the same type of effect.

The second proof: Black and white each, by their specific nature, are valid to be known (معلوم). These are equal judgments (أحكام متساوية) caused by the particularities (خصوصيات) of these different quiddities. So different quiddities cause the same type of judgment (knowability).

The third proof: When two quiddities share in one respect and differ in another, the aspect of similarity is distinct from the aspect of difference. Razi then sets up a logical division of entailment (ملازمة) between the two aspects: either no entailment, mutual entailment, the shared entailing the distinguishing, or the distinguishing entailing the shared. He eliminates the first three: no entailment would lead to each consideration being separate, so no single reality arises from their combination (contradiction); the shared entailing the distinguishing would mean the distinguishing is not really causing distinction (impossible). This leaves only the fourth: the distinguishing entails the shared. Therefore, equal judgments (like visibility) can be caused by different quiddities, because the differing essence entails the shared judgment.

The fourth proof: If a shared judgment required a shared attribute, that shared attribute's own validity would require another shared attribute, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. So the causation of shared judgments must ultimately rest on the particularities of quiddities, not on a series of shared attributes.

After these proofs, Razi addresses a counter-objection (لا يقال): if two equal judgments are caused by different causes, but equality in quiddity requires equality in concomitants, then the second judgment would need a cause similar to the first's cause. Razi replies that if that argument were sound, it would force all equal judgments to have exactly the same cause (بعين العلة), which is absurd by agreement. So the counter-objection fails.

Finally, Razi offers a deeper point (ثم نقول): the attribution of a judgment to a cause does not arise from the judgment's essence; rather, the specific cause by its own nature necessitates the judgment. Therefore, the judgment is attributed to that cause because the cause entails it, not because the judgment's essence demands that specific cause. This is a subtle (دقيق) point requiring reflection.

Key Terms
حكم متماثل
علة
لوازم متساوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two judgments are similar (e.g., both are valid instances of seeing), then their causes must be similar.
Correction: Razi shows that different causes can produce similar effects, just as two different things can share the property of being different.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's first proof?
A) Different things cannot share any property.
B) Different things can share equal concomitants, so similar judgments need not have similar causes.
C) The objector's argument is valid but incomplete.
D) Visibility of substance and accident must have the same cause.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof breaks the assumed link between similar effects and similar causes by showing that different things can share equal concomitants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed that two similar outcomes in your life—like two successes or two failures—must have had the same cause. How does Razi's argument that similar judgments can have different causes challenge that assumption? What would it mean for your gratitude or repentance if you admitted that each outcome could have its own distinct cause? Are you willing to reexamine a specific past judgment in light of this?
?
The passage shows that even opposites share the property of opposition. Reflect on a conflict you are currently in. Do you and the other person share the property of 'being opposed'? What does that shared property reveal about your common humanity, and how might that change the way you view the conflict? What step can you take this week to acknowledge that shared ground?
?
If different things can share a good quality without a common cause, what does that say about your tendency to compare yourself to others? Do you ever envy others for a virtue they have, assuming that your lack of it means you are fundamentally different? Can you accept that you might share that quality in a different way or from a different source? What would it look like to thank Allah for what He has given you without comparison?

Second Proof: Black and White Knowability
Razi provides the second proof using black and white.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that different quiddities (like black and white) can cause equal judgments (knowability) precisely because of their unique particularities.

Core Idea
Different essences can produce the same type of judgment.

Razi's second proof uses a concrete example from epistemology: blackness, by its specific nature, is knowable, and whiteness, by its specific nature, is also knowable. Both are 'knowable' (معلوم), a similar judgment, yet the cause is the particularity of each quiddity. This illustrates that equal judgments can be caused by different causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic for the second proof carefully. Note how Razi identifies black and white as two different quiddities, each producing the same judgment: being knowable.
The term 'خصوصيات الماهيات' (particularities of quiddities) is key here.
2
Analyze the equality of judgments
The judgment 'knowable' is the same for both black and white. Focus on the fact that the cause is the specific essence (blackness or whiteness), not some shared abstract property.
This directly challenges the idea that similarity of judgment requires similarity of cause.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider: if blackness and whiteness can both grant knowability without sharing a cause, then the visibility of substance and accident can also be caused by different causes.
This proof is straightforward but powerful.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (السؤال الرابع) and its detailed answer. The objector concedes that the two visibility judgments are similar (متماثلان) but asks why they cannot be caused by two different causes. Razi then provides four proofs (وجوه) that similar judgments can indeed have different causes.

The first proof: Different things can share equal concomitants (لوازم متساوية). For example, two different things share the property of being different from each other, so 'difference' is a shared concept, and similarly for opposites. Thus, different quiddities can produce the same type of effect.

The second proof: Black and white each, by their specific nature, are valid to be known (معلوم). These are equal judgments (أحكام متساوية) caused by the particularities (خصوصيات) of these different quiddities. So different quiddities cause the same type of judgment (knowability).

The third proof: When two quiddities share in one respect and differ in another, the aspect of similarity is distinct from the aspect of difference. Razi then sets up a logical division of entailment (ملازمة) between the two aspects: either no entailment, mutual entailment, the shared entailing the distinguishing, or the distinguishing entailing the shared. He eliminates the first three: no entailment would lead to each consideration being separate, so no single reality arises from their combination (contradiction); the shared entailing the distinguishing would mean the distinguishing is not really causing distinction (impossible). This leaves only the fourth: the distinguishing entails the shared. Therefore, equal judgments (like visibility) can be caused by different quiddities, because the differing essence entails the shared judgment.

The fourth proof: If a shared judgment required a shared attribute, that shared attribute's own validity would require another shared attribute, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. So the causation of shared judgments must ultimately rest on the particularities of quiddities, not on a series of shared attributes.

After these proofs, Razi addresses a counter-objection (لا يقال): if two equal judgments are caused by different causes, but equality in quiddity requires equality in concomitants, then the second judgment would need a cause similar to the first's cause. Razi replies that if that argument were sound, it would force all equal judgments to have exactly the same cause (بعين العلة), which is absurd by agreement. So the counter-objection fails.

Finally, Razi offers a deeper point (ثم نقول): the attribution of a judgment to a cause does not arise from the judgment's essence; rather, the specific cause by its own nature necessitates the judgment. Therefore, the judgment is attributed to that cause because the cause entails it, not because the judgment's essence demands that specific cause. This is a subtle (دقيق) point requiring reflection.

Third Proof: Entailment Between Similarity and Distinction
Razi presents the third proof with a logical division of entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof, which uses a logical division of entailment to show that the distinguishing aspect of quiddities entails the shared aspect, thereby allowing different causes for equal judgments.

Core Idea
What makes things different can entail what makes them similar.

Razi's third proof is a sophisticated logical argument. He observes that any two quiddities share one aspect and differ in another. He then examines the possible relations of entailment (ملازمة) between these two aspects. By eliminating the impossible cases, he concludes that the distinguishing aspect must entail the shared one. This means that the very thing that makes two quiddities different also brings about the similarity in their effects—allowing different causes for equal judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof segment
Read the Arabic passage for the third proof. Notice the four-fold division of entailment between the shared and distinguishing aspects.
This is a deductive argument with a division into four cases (اقسام).
2
Understand the four cases
The four cases are: (1) no entailment, (2) mutual entailment, (3) shared entails distinguishing, (4) distinguishing entails shared. Follow Razi's elimination of the first three.
Case 1 would mean no single reality results; case 2 and 3 would make the distinguishing aspect ineffective.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Only case 4 remains: the distinguishing aspect entails the shared aspect. This means that different quiddities (their distinguishing features) cause the shared judgment (like visibility).
Thus, equal judgments can be caused by different causes—exactly what Razi set out to prove.

لوازم متساوية، والدليل عليه أن المختلفين يشتركان في صحة الاختلاف فإنه كما أن هذا مخالف لذلك فذاك مخالف لهذا، فمسمى الاختلاف مشترك فيه وكذا القول في الضدين الثاني : وهو أن السواد بخصوص كونه سوادًا يصح أن يكون معلوما، وكذا البياض بخصوص كونه بياضًا يصح أن يكون معلوماً، وهذه أحكام متساوية معللة بخصوصيات هذه الماهيات، مختلفة. وهي الثالث : وهو أن الماهيتين إذا اشتركتا من وجه اختلفا من وجه آخر، ولا شك أن ما به التشابه مغاير لما به الامتياز. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن لا تكون بين الاعتبارين ملازمة أصلا، وإما أن يكون كل واحد منهما مستلزما للآخر، وإما أن يكون ما به المشاركة مستلزما لما به الممايزة، وإما أن يكون بالعكس. والقسم الأول باطل، وإلا لانفك كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين عن الآخر، وحينئذ لا يحصل من مجموعهما حقيقة واحدة هذا ،خلف والثاني والثالث أيضًا باطل، إذ لو كان ما به الاشتراك مستلزما لما به الامتياز لما كان ما به الامتياز موجبا للمايزة، وذلك محال، فلم يبق إلا القسم الرابع، وهو أن يكون ما به الممايزة مستلزما لما به المشاركة. وهذا يقتضي الجزم بتعليل الأشياء المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة. الرابع لو وجب تعليل الحكم المشترك بوصف مشترك لكان صحة كونه موصوفًا بذلك الوصف المشترك، معللة بوصف آخر مشترك فيه، والكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال، فعلمنا أنه لا بد من انتهاء تعليل الأحكام المشتركة إلى خصوصيات الماهيات وحينئذ يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالماهيات المختلفة. لا يقال : الدليل على أن تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة لا يجوز، وذلك لأن هذا الحكم لما كان معللا بهذه العلة لذاته، والحكم الثاني مساويا للحكم الأول في الماهية وللمتساويان في الماهية يجب استواؤهما في اللوازم، فيلزم أن يكون الحكم الثاني مستندا إلى ما يماثل تلك العلة فثبت أن الحكمين المتساويين يجب تعليلهما بعلتين متماثلتين. لأنا نقول : إن صح هذا الكلام لزم تعليل جميع الأحكام المتساوية بعلة واحدة بالشخص ؛ لأن كون هذا الحكم معللا بهذه العلة المعينة أمر ثيت له لذاته، والحكم المتماثلة له مساو له في تمام الماهية ، فيلزم في مثل ذلك الحكم أن يكون معللا بعين تلك العلة، وبالاتفاق هذا باطل، وكذا ما ذكرتموه. ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إسناد هذا الحكم إلى هذه العلة ليس متولدا من ذات الحكم، بل العلة المخصوصة لذاتها توجب ذلك الحكم ، ولما حصل ذلك الحكم بتلك العلة وجب إسناده إلى تلك العلة لا لأن ذاته يوجب الإسناد إليها ، بل لأجل أن تكون العلة مستلزمة له، فوجب إسناده إليها، وهذا كلام دقيق لا بد من التأمل فيه.
The fourth question: Suppose that the validity of seeing substance and the validity of seeing accident are two similar judgments, but why is it not permissible to cause the two similar judgments by two different causes? That which indicates this are aspects: The first: That different things are not prevented from sharing equal concomitants, and the evidence for that is that two different things share in the validity of difference, for just as this one is contrary to that, so that one is contrary to this, so the meaning of difference is shared, and likewise for two opposites. The second: That black, by virtue of being black, is valid to be known, and likewise white, by virtue of being white, is valid to be known, and these are equal judgments caused by the particularities of these different quiddities. The third: That when two quiddities share in one respect, they differ in another respect, and there is no doubt that that by which they are similar is other than that by which they are distinguished. Once this is established, we say: Either there is no entailment between the two considerations at all, or each one entails the other, or that by which they share entails that by which they differ, or the opposite. The first division is false, for then each of the two considerations would be separate from the other, and then from their combination no single reality would result – this is a contradiction. The second and third are also false, because if that by which they share entailed that by which they differ, then that by which they differ would not be a cause of differentiation, which is impossible. So nothing remains except the fourth division, which is that that by which they differ entails that by which they share. This necessitates the certainty of causing equal things by different causes. The fourth: If the shared judgment must be caused by a shared attribute, then the validity of its being described by that shared attribute would be caused by another shared attribute, and the argument for it is the same as the first, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. So we know that the causation of shared judgments must terminate at the particularities of quiddities, and then it follows that equal judgments are caused by different quiddities. It cannot be said: The evidence that causing equal judgments by different causes is not permissible is that when this judgment is caused by this cause by its essence, and the second judgment is equal to the first in quiddity, and two things equal in quiddity must be equal in their concomitants, so it follows that the second judgment is based on something similar to that cause, so it is established that two equal judgments must be caused by two similar causes. We say: If this statement is sound, it would necessitate causing all equal judgments by one identical cause, because this judgment being caused by this particular cause is a fixed attribute of it by its essence, and the judgment similar to it is equal to it in the entirety of quiddity, so it would follow that such a judgment must be caused by that same cause, and by agreement this is false, and likewise what you have mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: The attribution of this judgment to this cause does not arise from the essence of the judgment, but rather the particular cause by its essence necessitates that judgment, and when that judgment occurs by that cause, its attribution to that cause becomes necessary, not because its essence necessitates attribution to it, but because the cause is entailing of it, so its attribution to it becomes necessary. This is a subtle point that must be contemplated.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (السؤال الرابع) and its detailed answer. The objector concedes that the two visibility judgments are similar (متماثلان) but asks why they cannot be caused by two different causes. Razi then provides four proofs (وجوه) that similar judgments can indeed have different causes.

The first proof: Different things can share equal concomitants (لوازم متساوية). For example, two different things share the property of being different from each other, so 'difference' is a shared concept, and similarly for opposites. Thus, different quiddities can produce the same type of effect.

The second proof: Black and white each, by their specific nature, are valid to be known (معلوم). These are equal judgments (أحكام متساوية) caused by the particularities (خصوصيات) of these different quiddities. So different quiddities cause the same type of judgment (knowability).

The third proof: When two quiddities share in one respect and differ in another, the aspect of similarity is distinct from the aspect of difference. Razi then sets up a logical division of entailment (ملازمة) between the two aspects: either no entailment, mutual entailment, the shared entailing the distinguishing, or the distinguishing entailing the shared. He eliminates the first three: no entailment would lead to each consideration being separate, so no single reality arises from their combination (contradiction); the shared entailing the distinguishing would mean the distinguishing is not really causing distinction (impossible). This leaves only the fourth: the distinguishing entails the shared. Therefore, equal judgments (like visibility) can be caused by different quiddities, because the differing essence entails the shared judgment.

The fourth proof: If a shared judgment required a shared attribute, that shared attribute's own validity would require another shared attribute, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. So the causation of shared judgments must ultimately rest on the particularities of quiddities, not on a series of shared attributes.

After these proofs, Razi addresses a counter-objection (لا يقال): if two equal judgments are caused by different causes, but equality in quiddity requires equality in concomitants, then the second judgment would need a cause similar to the first's cause. Razi replies that if that argument were sound, it would force all equal judgments to have exactly the same cause (بعين العلة), which is absurd by agreement. So the counter-objection fails.

Finally, Razi offers a deeper point (ثم نقول): the attribution of a judgment to a cause does not arise from the judgment's essence; rather, the specific cause by its own nature necessitates the judgment. Therefore, the judgment is attributed to that cause because the cause entails it, not because the judgment's essence demands that specific cause. This is a subtle (دقيق) point requiring reflection.

Key Terms
مشاركة
ممايزة
ملازمة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of Razi's third proof?
A) The shared aspect always entails the distinguishing aspect.
B) The distinguishing aspect entails the shared aspect.
C) There is no relationship between the two aspects.
D) Equal judgments must have identical causes.

Key Takeaway
The distinguishing aspects of quiddities entail the shared concomitant, so different causes can produce equal judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that what makes you distinct entails what you share with others. Is there a unique talent or quality you have been neglecting, thinking it doesn't affect those around you? How will you use it this week to serve the community, starting with one concrete action?
?
He eliminates the possibility that the shared aspect causes the distinct: you cannot blame your 'common human nature' for your failures. Which sin have you been excusing by saying 'everyone does it'? Will you instead take full responsibility for your distinct choice and repent specifically today?
?
The proof shows different causes can produce equal judgments. In your worship, have you assumed that the same outward action (prayer, charity) brings the same reward regardless of intention? Which prayer will you offer tomorrow with a distinct, sincere intention that you have never tried before, seeking a deeper connection?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
10 min
Fourth Proof: Infinite Regress Argument
Razi gives the fourth proof using infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth proof that if a shared judgment required a shared attribute, it would lead to an infinite regress, which is impossible; therefore, shared judgments must be grounded in the particularities of quiddities.

Core Idea
Shared judgments cannot be infinitely traced back through shared attributes.

Razi's fourth proof is a classic reduction to absurdity: if we assume that every shared judgment needs a shared attribute as its cause, then that shared attribute itself must be a shared judgment that needs another shared attribute, and so on indefinitely. Since infinite regress (تسلسل) is impossible, the chain must terminate in the particularities of quiddities—the very essences that distinguish things. Thus, shared judgments can be caused by different quiddities.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the Arabic for the fourth proof. Notice the conditional: 'If the shared judgment must be caused by a shared attribute...'
Razi uses the term 'تسلسل' (infinite regress) as the key problem.
2
Understand the regress
If a shared judgment J requires a shared attribute A as its cause, then A itself must be a shared judgment (since it is predicated of multiple things), requiring another shared attribute B, and so on. This goes on forever.
This is unsustainable because an infinite chain of causes is impossible in this context.
3
Grasp the termination
Razi concludes that the regress must stop at the particularities of quiddities (خصوصيات الماهيات). That means different quiddities serve as the ultimate causes for shared judgments.
Thus, different causes for equal judgments are not only possible but necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: هب ذلك أن صحة رؤية الجوهر وصحة رؤية العرض حكمان متماثلان لكن لم لا يجوز تعليل الحكمين المتماثلين بعلتين مختلفتين ؟ والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أن الأشياء المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لوازم متساوية، والدليل عليه أن المختلفين يشتركان في صحة الاختلاف فإنه كما أن هذا مخالف لذلك فذاك مخالف لهذا، فمسمى الاختلاف مشترك فيه وكذا القول في الضدين الثاني : وهو أن السواد بخصوص كونه سوادًا يصح الأحكام المشتركة إلى خصوصيات الماهيات وحينئذ يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالماهيات المختلفة. لا يقال : الدليل على أن تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة لا يجوز، وذلك لأن هذا الحكم لما كان معللا بهذه العلة لذاته، والحكم الثاني مساويا للحكم الأول في الماهية وللمتساويان في الماهية يجب استواؤهما في اللوازم، فيلزم أن يكون الحكم الثاني مستندا إلى ما يماثل تلك العلة فثبت أن الحكمين المتساويين يجب تعليلهما بعلتين متماثلتين. لأنا نقول : إن صح هذا الكلام لزم تعليل جميع الأحكام المتساوية بعلة واحدة بالشخص ؛ لأن كون هذا الحكم معللا بهذه العلة المعينة أمر ثيت له لذاته، والحكم المتماثلة له مساو له في تمام الماهية ، فيلزم في مثل ذلك الحكم أن يكون معللا بعين تلك العلة، وبالاتفاق هذا باطل، وكذا ما ذكرتموه. ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إسناد هذا الحكم إلى هذه العلة ليس متولدا من ذات الحكم، بل العلة المخصوصة لذاتها توجب ذلك الحكم ، ولما حصل ذلك الحكم بتلك العلة وجب إسناده إلى تلك العلة لا لأن ذاته يوجب الإسناد إليها ، بل لأجل أن تكون العلة مستلزمة له، فوجب إسناده إليها، وهذا كلام دقيق لا بد من التأمل فيه.
The fourth question: Suppose that the validity of seeing substance and the validity of seeing accident are two similar judgments, but why is it not permissible to cause the two similar judgments by two different causes? That which indicates this are aspects: The first: That different things are not prevented from sharing equal concomitants, and the evidence for that is that two different things share in the validity of difference, for just as this one is contrary to that, so that one is contrary to this, so the meaning of difference is shared, and likewise for two opposites. The second: That black, by virtue of being black, is valid to be known, and likewise white, by virtue of being white, is valid to be known, and these are equal judgments caused by the particularities of these different quiddities. The third: That when two quiddities share in one respect, they differ in another respect, and there is no doubt that that by which they are similar is other than that by which they are distinguished. Once this is established, we say: Either there is no entailment between the two considerations at all, or each one entails the other, or that by which they share entails that by which they differ, or the opposite. The first division is false, for then each of the two considerations would be separate from the other, and then from their combination no single reality would result – this is a contradiction. The second and third are also false, because if that by which they share entailed that by which they differ, then that by which they differ would not be a cause of differentiation, which is impossible. So nothing remains except the fourth division, which is that that by which they differ entails that by which they share. This necessitates the certainty of causing equal things by different causes. The fourth: If the shared judgment must be caused by a shared attribute, then the validity of its being described by that shared attribute would be caused by another shared attribute, and the argument for it is the same as the first, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. So we know that the causation of shared judgments must terminate at the particularities of quiddities, and then it follows that equal judgments are caused by different quiddities. It cannot be said: The evidence that causing equal judgments by different causes is not permissible is that when this judgment is caused by this cause by its essence, and the second judgment is equal to the first in quiddity, and two things equal in quiddity must be equal in their concomitants, so it follows that the second judgment is based on something similar to that cause, so it is established that two equal judgments must be caused by two similar causes. We say: If this statement is sound, it would necessitate causing all equal judgments by one identical cause, because this judgment being caused by this particular cause is a fixed attribute of it by its essence, and the judgment similar to it is equal to it in the entirety of quiddity, so it would follow that such a judgment must be caused by that same cause, and by agreement this is false, and likewise what you have mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: The attribution of this judgment to this cause does not arise from the essence of the judgment, but rather the particular cause by its essence necessitates that judgment, and when that judgment occurs by that cause, its attribution to that cause becomes necessary, not because its essence necessitates attribution to it, but because the cause is entailing of it, so its attribution to it becomes necessary. This is a subtle point that must be contemplated.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (السؤال الرابع) and its detailed answer. The objector concedes that the two visibility judgments are similar (متماثلان) but asks why they cannot be caused by two different causes. Razi then provides four proofs (وجوه) that similar judgments can indeed have different causes.

The first proof: Different things can share equal concomitants (لوازم متساوية). For example, two different things share the property of being different from each other, so 'difference' is a shared concept, and similarly for opposites. Thus, different quiddities can produce the same type of effect.

The second proof: Black and white each, by their specific nature, are valid to be known (معلوم). These are equal judgments (أحكام متساوية) caused by the particularities (خصوصيات) of these different quiddities. So different quiddities cause the same type of judgment (knowability).

The third proof: When two quiddities share in one respect and differ in another, the aspect of similarity is distinct from the aspect of difference. Razi then sets up a logical division of entailment (ملازمة) between the two aspects: either no entailment, mutual entailment, the shared entailing the distinguishing, or the distinguishing entailing the shared. He eliminates the first three: no entailment would lead to each consideration being separate, so no single reality arises from their combination (contradiction); the shared entailing the distinguishing would mean the distinguishing is not really causing distinction (impossible). This leaves only the fourth: the distinguishing entails the shared. Therefore, equal judgments (like visibility) can be caused by different quiddities, because the differing essence entails the shared judgment.

The fourth proof: If a shared judgment required a shared attribute, that shared attribute's own validity would require another shared attribute, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. So the causation of shared judgments must ultimately rest on the particularities of quiddities, not on a series of shared attributes.

After these proofs, Razi addresses a counter-objection (لا يقال): if two equal judgments are caused by different causes, but equality in quiddity requires equality in concomitants, then the second judgment would need a cause similar to the first's cause. Razi replies that if that argument were sound, it would force all equal judgments to have exactly the same cause (بعين العلة), which is absurd by agreement. So the counter-objection fails.

Finally, Razi offers a deeper point (ثم نقول): the attribution of a judgment to a cause does not arise from the judgment's essence; rather, the specific cause by its own nature necessitates the judgment. Therefore, the judgment is attributed to that cause because the cause entails it, not because the judgment's essence demands that specific cause. This is a subtle (دقيق) point requiring reflection.

Key Terms
تسلسل
خصوصيات الماهيات
علة مشتركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shared judgments require a chain of shared attributes as causes, but this chain can go on indefinitely without problem.
Correction: Razi shows that such a chain leads to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible in causal explanations. The chain must terminate in the particularities of quiddities.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if we assume that a shared judgment requires a shared attribute as its cause, according to Razi's fourth proof?
A) The chain of causes terminates quickly.
B) It leads to an infinite regress.
C) It proves that all quiddities are identical.
D) It shows that shared judgments are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress forces us to ground shared judgments in the unique particularities of quiddities, allowing different causes for equal judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that an infinite regress of causes is impossible and that each judgment must be grounded in a unique particularity. Where in your spiritual life have you been seeking a 'shared attribute' to explain multiple failings, when you need to face the unique root cause of each one? Will you stop the regress and bring each one before Allah individually?
?
The proof shows that equal effects can have different causes. In your own life, do you assume that others' sins or struggles are caused by the same things as yours? How does this lesson call you to avoid judging others by your own 'shared attribute' and instead consider their unique essence?
?
If infinite regress is impossible in metaphysics, it is also impossible in your excuses for delaying repentance. Are you stringing together an infinite chain of 'I will repent tomorrow'? What is the particularity of your heart that makes you procrastinate, and will you cut the chain today?

Rational Judgments
advanced

Refutation of Opposing Argument and Final Insight
Razi refutes a counter-objection and provides a final subtle insight on causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the counter-argument that equal quiddities require similar causes, and his final subtle point that the attribution of a judgment to a cause arises from the cause's own nature, not from the judgment's essence.

Core Idea
The link between judgment and cause is not inherent in the judgment's essence but stems from the cause's necessitation.

After presenting his four proofs, Razi addresses a potential counter-objection: if two judgments are equal in quiddity, they should have similar causes because equal quiddities have equal concomitants. Razi refutes this by showing it would lead to the absurd conclusion that all equal judgments must have exactly the same cause (بعين العلة), which is false by agreement. Then he offers a profound insight: the attribution of a judgment to a specific cause is not based on the judgment's own essence, but on the fact that that particular cause necessitates the judgment. This careful distinction resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection and Razi's reply
Read the Arabic from 'لا يقال' to the end. First, understand the opponent's argument: equal quiddities require similar causes because they share the same essence.
The opponent uses the principle that equal quiddities have equal concomitants.
2
Analyze Razi's refutation
Razi shows that the opponent's logic would force all equal judgments to have one identical cause, which is absurd. Therefore, the premise that equal quiddities require similar causes is false.
Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: if A causes judgment J by its essence, and another judgment J' is equal in quiddity to J, then J' would need the same cause A, not just a similar one.
3
Grasp the final subtle point
Razi then offers a deeper explanation: judgment J is attributed to cause C not because J's essence demands it, but because C by its own nature necessitates J. So the link is from the cause, not from the judgment.
This is a nuanced causation theory: the cause's essence is the active factor, not the effect's essence.

السؤال الرابع: هب ذلك أن صحة رؤية الجوهر وصحة رؤية العرض حكمان متماثلان لكن لم لا يجوز تعليل الحكمين المتماثلين بعلتين مختلفتين ؟ والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أن الأشياء المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لوازم متساوية، والدليل عليه أن المختلفين يشتركان في صحة الاختلاف فإنه كما أن هذا مخالف لذلك فذاك مخالف لهذا، فمسمى الاختلاف مشترك فيه وكذا القول في الضدين الثاني : وهو أن السواد بخصوص كونه سوادًا يصح أن يكون معلوما، وكذا البياض بخصوص كونه بياضًا يصح أن يكون معلوماً، وهذه أحكام متساوية معللة بخصوصيات هذه الماهيات، مختلفة. وهي الثالث : وهو أن الماهيتين إذا اشتركتا من وجه اختلفا من وجه آخر، ولا شك أن ما به التشابه مغاير لما به الامتياز. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن لا تكون بين الاعتبارين ملازمة أصلا، وإما أن يكون كل واحد منهما مستلزما للآخر، وإما أن يكون ما به المشاركة مستلزما لما به الممايزة، وإما أن يكون بالعكس. والقسم الأول باطل، وإلا لانفك كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين عن الآخر، وحينئذ لا يحصل من مجموعهما حقيقة واحدة هذا ،خلف والثاني والثالث أيضًا باطل، إذ لو كان ما به الاشتراك مستلزما لما به الامتياز لما كان ما به الامتياز موجبا للمايزة، وذلك محال، فلم يبق إلا القسم الرابع، وهو أن يكون ما به الممايزة مستلزما لما به المشاركة. وهذا يقتضي الجزم بتعليل الأشياء المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة. الرابع لو وجب تعليل الحكم المشترك بوصف مشترك لكان صحة كونه موصوفًا بذلك الوصف المشترك، معللة بوصف آخر مشترك فيه، والكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال، فعلمنا أنه لا بد من انتهاء تعليل الأحكام المشتركة إلى خصوصيات الماهيات وحينئذ يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالماهيات المختلفة. لا يقال : الدليل على أن تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة لا يجوز، وذلك لأن هذا الحكم لما كان معللا بهذه العلة لذاته، والحكم الثاني مساويا للحكم الأول في الماهية وللمتساويان في الماهية يجب استواؤهما في اللوازم، فيلزم أن يكون الحكم الثاني مستندا إلى ما يماثل تلك العلة فثبت أن الحكمين المتساويين يجب تعليلهما بعلتين متماثلتين. لأنا نقول : إن صح هذا الكلام لزم تعليل جميع الأحكام المتساوية بعلة واحدة بالشخص ؛ لأن كون هذا الحكم معللا بهذه العلة المعينة أمر ثيت له لذاته، والحكم المتماثلة له مساو له في تمام الماهية ، فيلزم في مثل ذلك الحكم أن يكون معللا بعين تلك العلة، وبالاتفاق هذا باطل، وكذا ما ذكرتموه. ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إسناد هذا الحكم إلى هذه العلة ليس متولدا من ذات الحكم، بل العلة المخصوصة لذاتها توجب ذلك الحكم ، ولما حصل ذلك الحكم بتلك العلة وجب إسناده إلى تلك العلة لا لأن ذاته يوجب الإسناد إليها ، بل لأجل أن تكون العلة مستلزمة له، فوجب إسناده إليها، وهذا كلام دقيق لا بد من التأمل فيه.
The fourth question: Suppose that the validity of seeing substance and the validity of seeing accident are two similar judgments, but why is it not permissible to cause the two similar judgments by two different causes? That which indicates this are aspects: The first: That different things are not prevented from sharing equal concomitants, and the evidence for that is that two different things share in the validity of difference, for just as this one is contrary to that, so that one is contrary to this, so the meaning of difference is shared, and likewise for two opposites. The second: That black, by virtue of being black, is valid to be known, and likewise white, by virtue of being white, is valid to be known, and these are equal judgments caused by the particularities of these different quiddities. The third: That when two quiddities share in one respect, they differ in another respect, and there is no doubt that that by which they are similar is other than that by which they are distinguished. Once this is established, we say: Either there is no entailment between the two considerations at all, or each one entails the other, or that by which they share entails that by which they differ, or the opposite. The first division is false, for then each of the two considerations would be separate from the other, and then from their combination no single reality would result – this is a contradiction. The second and third are also false, because if that by which they share entailed that by which they differ, then that by which they differ would not be a cause of differentiation, which is impossible. So nothing remains except the fourth division, which is that that by which they differ entails that by which they share. This necessitates the certainty of causing equal things by different causes. The fourth: If the shared judgment must be caused by a shared attribute, then the validity of its being described by that shared attribute would be caused by another shared attribute, and the argument for it is the same as the first, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. So we know that the causation of shared judgments must terminate at the particularities of quiddities, and then it follows that equal judgments are caused by different quiddities. It cannot be said: The evidence that causing equal judgments by different causes is not permissible is that when this judgment is caused by this cause by its essence, and the second judgment is equal to the first in quiddity, and two things equal in quiddity must be equal in their concomitants, so it follows that the second judgment is based on something similar to that cause, so it is established that two equal judgments must be caused by two similar causes. We say: If this statement is sound, it would necessitate causing all equal judgments by one identical cause, because this judgment being caused by this particular cause is a fixed attribute of it by its essence, and the judgment similar to it is equal to it in the entirety of quiddity, so it would follow that such a judgment must be caused by that same cause, and by agreement this is false, and likewise what you have mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say: The attribution of this judgment to this cause does not arise from the essence of the judgment, but rather the particular cause by its essence necessitates that judgment, and when that judgment occurs by that cause, its attribution to that cause becomes necessary, not because its essence necessitates attribution to it, but because the cause is entailing of it, so its attribution to it becomes necessary. This is a subtle point that must be contemplated.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (السؤال الرابع) and its detailed answer. The objector concedes that the two visibility judgments are similar (متماثلان) but asks why they cannot be caused by two different causes. Razi then provides four proofs (وجوه) that similar judgments can indeed have different causes.

The first proof: Different things can share equal concomitants (لوازم متساوية). For example, two different things share the property of being different from each other, so 'difference' is a shared concept, and similarly for opposites. Thus, different quiddities can produce the same type of effect.

The second proof: Black and white each, by their specific nature, are valid to be known (معلوم). These are equal judgments (أحكام متساوية) caused by the particularities (خصوصيات) of these different quiddities. So different quiddities cause the same type of judgment (knowability).

The third proof: When two quiddities share in one respect and differ in another, the aspect of similarity is distinct from the aspect of difference. Razi then sets up a logical division of entailment (ملازمة) between the two aspects: either no entailment, mutual entailment, the shared entailing the distinguishing, or the distinguishing entailing the shared. He eliminates the first three: no entailment would lead to each consideration being separate, so no single reality arises from their combination (contradiction); the shared entailing the distinguishing would mean the distinguishing is not really causing distinction (impossible). This leaves only the fourth: the distinguishing entails the shared. Therefore, equal judgments (like visibility) can be caused by different quiddities, because the differing essence entails the shared judgment.

The fourth proof: If a shared judgment required a shared attribute, that shared attribute's own validity would require another shared attribute, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. So the causation of shared judgments must ultimately rest on the particularities of quiddities, not on a series of shared attributes.

After these proofs, Razi addresses a counter-objection (لا يقال): if two equal judgments are caused by different causes, but equality in quiddity requires equality in concomitants, then the second judgment would need a cause similar to the first's cause. Razi replies that if that argument were sound, it would force all equal judgments to have exactly the same cause (بعين العلة), which is absurd by agreement. So the counter-objection fails.

Finally, Razi offers a deeper point (ثم نقول): the attribution of a judgment to a cause does not arise from the judgment's essence; rather, the specific cause by its own nature necessitates the judgment. Therefore, the judgment is attributed to that cause because the cause entails it, not because the judgment's essence demands that specific cause. This is a subtle (دقيق) point requiring reflection.

Key Terms
لا يقال
تعليل
ذات العلة
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two judgments are similar in quiddity, they must have similar causes because the similarity of the judgment is inherent in its essence.
Correction: Razi shows that the similarity of judgment does not entail similarity of cause; rather, the cause's own nature necessitates the judgment independently of the judgment's essence.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Razi's final subtle point?
A) The judgment's essence determines its cause.
B) The cause's essence necessitates the judgment, so attribution is from the cause.
C) Infinite regress is acceptable in some cases.
D) Equal judgments must have identical causes.

Key Takeaway
The cause's own nature, not the judgment's essence, is the basis for attributing a judgment to its cause, allowing different causes for similar judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring struggle in your life where you tend to blame your 'essence' (e.g., 'I'm just not patient'). Razi says the judgment (your impatience) does not arise from your essence but from a cause. What is that cause? Are you willing to examine it honestly and change it at its root?
?
When you fail in worship, do you attribute it to a weakness in yourself or to the true cause (e.g., heedlessness, lack of sincerity)? Razi says the cause necessitates the effect. What cause have you been ignoring? Commit to addressing that cause today.
?
Contemplate: If the attribution of your actions to their causes is not built into the act itself but derives from the cause's necessity, how does that reorient your responsibility before Allah? Write down one concrete way you will align your understanding of cause and effect in your life with this subtle insight.

Twelfth Argument: Taqlib al-Hadaqa is Under Human Control
Razi argues that nazar literally means turning the pupil based on human control and linguistic preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the word 'nazar' literally refers to the physical act of turning the pupil, not to vision, based on human capability and the principle that literal interpretation is preferable.

Core Idea
'Nazar' is literally turning the pupil because we control it, unlike vision.

In this twelfth argument, Razi continues his systematic refutation of the claim that nazar is synonymous with ru'ya. He introduces a linguistic principle: if a word can be interpreted literally (haqiqa), that is preferable to metaphorical interpretation (majaz). He then applies this principle to the word 'nazar' by analyzing what we can actually control in the act of seeing. This argument is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari position on the nature of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage uses conditional reasoning: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the key distinction
Razi distinguishes between what a human can control (turning the pupil) and what occurs without control (vision). This distinction is the foundation of the argument.
3
Examine the two interpretations
Consider the two possible meanings of 'nazar': literal turning of the pupil vs. vision. See how each affects the interpretation of commands like 'look' and 'do not look'.
4
Apply the principle of preference
Understand the principle that keeping a word literal is preferable to making it metaphorical. Then see how this forces the conclusion that 'nazar' is literally for turning the pupil.
This step ties the reasoning together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشرة : أن الذي يقدر عليه الإنسان في باب الرؤية، إنما هو تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي، فأما حصول الرؤية فليس ذلك في قدرة الإنسان، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو حملنا لفظ النظر على تقليب الحدقة، كان قولنا في الأم والنهي : انظر ولا تنظر محمولاً على الحقيقة، أما لو حملنا النظر على الرؤية لوجب حمل قولنا : انظر ولا تنظر على مقدمات الرؤية، لا على نفس الرؤية، فيصير هذا اللفظ مجازا، ومعلوم أن حمل الكلام على معنى يبقى اللفظ معه حقيقة أولى من حمله على معنى يبقى اللفظ معه مجازاً ، وهذا يقتضي كون لفظ النظر حقيقة في تقليب الحدقة لا في الرؤية.
The twelfth argument: That which a human is capable of in the domain of vision is only turning the pupil towards the direction of the visible object. As for the occurrence of vision, that is not within human capability. If this is established, we say: If we interpret the word 'nazar' (looking) as turning the pupil, then our statement in command and prohibition: 'Look' and 'Do not look' would be interpreted literally. But if we interpret 'nazar' as vision, then it would be necessary to interpret our statement 'Look' and 'Do not look' as referring to the preliminaries of vision, not to vision itself, thus this word would become metaphorical. And it is known that interpreting speech in a way that keeps the word literal is preferable to interpreting it in a way that makes it metaphorical. This necessitates that the word 'nazar' is literally (haqiqa) for turning the pupil, not for vision (ru'ya).
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is the twelfth argument in the series proving that 'nazar' is not synonymous with 'ru'ya'. Razi begins by noting that what a human can control in the process of seeing is only the physical act of turning the pupil toward the object. The actual occurrence of vision is beyond human power, as it involves light reception and formation of the image. Given this, if we take 'nazar' to mean the physical turning of the pupil, then commands like 'look' and 'do not look' (referring to nazar) are literal, because the action is under our control. But if we interpret 'nazar' as vision (ru'ya), then commands must be interpreted metaphorically, referring to the preliminaries that lead to vision, not the vision itself. Razi argues that literal interpretation is always preferable to metaphorical. Therefore, 'nazar' properly means the voluntary turning of the pupil, not the involuntary occurrence of vision.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlib al-hadaqa)
حقيقة (haqiqa)
مجاز (majaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'nazar' is equivalent to 'ru'ya' (vision) because both relate to sight.
Correction: This argument shows that 'nazar' is literally the voluntary act of turning the pupil, which is under human control, while 'ru'ya' is the involuntary occurrence of vision. Commands to 'look' would be meaningless if 'nazar' meant vision, since we cannot command vision directly.

Checkpoint
According to the twelfth argument, what does the word 'nazar' literally (haqiqa) refer to?
A) Vision (ru'ya)
B) The preliminaries of vision
C) Turning the pupil toward the visible object
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The word 'nazar' literally means 'turning the pupil' because that action is under human control, and literal interpretation is preferable to metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I see', do you truly mean you have control over the vision? Or do you realize that the actual seeing is not by your power? How does this affect your gratitude to Allah for granting vision?
?
In your daily life, are you careful to use words literally when you mean what is under your control? Reflect on a time when you used a word metaphorically and it caused misunderstanding. How can this argument remind you to be precise?
?
If the command to 'look' only controls the physical act, what does that imply about your responsibility to choose what you look at? Are you ignoring that you can turn your gaze away from sin, yet you often use the excuse that you couldn't help seeing?

Sixteenth Argument: Looking as a Voluntary Act
Razi cites a poem to show looking is a voluntary act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a poetic contrast to argue that looking (nazar) is a deliberate physical act, distinct from involuntary seeing (ru'ya).

Core Idea
The contrast between looking and heedlessness proves looking is a deliberate act, not automatic.

Imam Razi presents a crisp argument drawn from Arabic poetry. By placing 'looking' opposite 'heedlessness,' the verse reveals that looking is something one can be mindful of or forget — a hallmark of voluntary actions. This distinction reinforces the Ash'ari position that nazar is a physical, intentional act.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic verse and its analysis
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the poet contrasts 'looked' with 'were heedless'.
The contrast is the key logical move.
2
Understand the logical implication
Razi argues that one is only heedless of an action one intends to do. Therefore, looking is intentional.
Heedlessness implies prior intention.
3
Identify the physical action defined
Razi specifies that the action is 'turning of the pupil' — a deliberate physical movement of the eye.
This distinguishes looking from mere seeing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشرة : أنشد بعضهم : إذا نظر الواشون صدت وأعرضت وإن غفلوا قلت ألست على العهد جعل قوله : وإن غفلوا في مقابلة قوله : نظروا، وإنما يغفل الإنسان عن أمر يفعله، ويشتغل بتحصيله وما ذلك إلا تقليب الحدقة.
The sixteenth argument: Some of them recited: 'When the slanderers look (naẓarū), she turns away and averts; but when they are heedless (ghafalū), I say: Are you not still faithful?' He made his statement 'when they are heedless' in opposition to his statement 'they looked'. And one is only heedless of an action that he does and busies himself with its accomplishment, and that is nothing but the turning of the pupil (taqlib al-hadaqa).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is the sixteenth argument, based on a poetic verse. The poet says that when the slanderers look (naẓarū) at the beloved, she turns away, but when they are heedless (ghafalū), he asks if she remains faithful. Razi points out that 'heedlessness' is placed in opposition to 'looking', meaning that looking is an action one can be heedless of. One is only heedless of something that one does intentionally. Therefore, looking (nazar) is a deliberate physical action, specifically the turning of the pupil. It is not an involuntary event like seeing. This again demonstrates that nazar is distinct from ru'ya.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
الغفلة (al-ghafla)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlib al-hadaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Looking (nazar) is an involuntary reaction like seeing.
Correction: The contrast with heedlessness shows looking is an intentional act one can forget to do.

Checkpoint

A) Seeing
B) Heedlessness (ghafla)
C) Turning away
D) Faithfulness

Key Takeaway
The contrast between looking and heedlessness in the poem demonstrates that looking is a voluntary physical action, specifically the turning of the pupil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at something forbidden, do you recognize that you are deliberately turning your pupil? How does knowing this affect your responsibility before Allah?
?
The poet contrasts looking and heedlessness: are you 'looking' away from your duties to Allah due to heedlessness? What would it take to turn your gaze back to Him today?
?
Your eyes are a voluntary tool: have you ever consciously used your gaze as an act of worship, like lowering your eyes for Allah? If not, what prevents you from doing so right now?

The First Two Rational Doubts of the Mu'tazila Against Ru'ya
Razi presents the first two rational doubts of the Mu'tazila against ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two rational arguments used by the Mu'tazila to deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that if God were visible, we would see Him now; since we don't, He cannot be seen.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents two rational (ʿaqlī) doubts raised by the Muʿtazila against the doctrine of ruʾyat Allāh (the vision of God). The first doubt, called the doubt of obstacles, analyzes the necessary conditions for sight in the empirical world and applies them to God. The second doubt, the doubt of facing, argues that visibility requires spatial opposition, which is impossible for a non‑spatial being. These arguments set the stage for Razi’s subsequent refutations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the preamble of eight conditions
The Mu'tazila list eight conditions that, when all present, necessitate sight in the empirical world. This sets up their argument.
These conditions include soundness of sense, distance, facing, etc.
2
Follow the argument from necessity
If all conditions are met, sight must occur; otherwise absurd consequences follow (e.g., not seeing large objects). This step shows the logical force of their claim.
3
Apply the argument to God
Only two conditions apply to God (sound sense and being visible); since they are currently satisfied, we would see God now if ru'ya were possible. The absence of vision proves impossibility.
4
Examine the doubt of facing
The second argument requires the seen object to be spatially facing the seer, which cannot apply to Allah. The passage includes a brief exception for accidents.
This argument is incomplete in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبه العقلية : فهي أيضا أربع :
الشبهة الأولى - وهى شبهة الموانع - وقبل تقريرها لا بد من مقدمة . وهي أن الأشياء التي يجب حصول الإبصار في الشاهد عند حصولها، ثمانية : أحدها : سلامة الحاسة وثانيها : كون الشيء بحيث أن يكون جائز الرؤية. وثالثها: أن لا يكون في غاية البعد والرابع أن لا يكون في غاية القرب. والخامس : أن يكون مقابلا للرائي أو في حكم المقابل والسادس: أن لا يكون في غاية اللطافة والسابع : أن لا يكون بين الرائي والمرئي حجاب. والثامن: أن لا يكون في غاية الصغر.
قالوا : عند حصول هذه الأمور الثمانية، يجب حصول الإبصار . إذ لو لم يجب لجاز أن يحصل بحضرتنا جبال عالية وشموس مضيئة وأصوات هائلة، ونحن لا نراها ولا نسمعها وذلك يقتضي دخول الإنسان في الجهالات.
إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فلنرجع إلى تقرير الشبهة، ونقول: أما الشرائط الستة الأخيرة فلا يمكن اعتبارها إلا في رؤية الأجسام . والله تعالى ليس بجسم، فلا يمكن اعتبار هذه الشرائط في رؤيته . فعلى هذا لو صحت رؤيته لوجب أن لا يصير في حصول رؤيته إلا أمران سلامة الحاسة وكونه بحيث يصح أن يرى . وهذان الأمران حاصلان الآن. فثبت أنه لو صحت رؤيته، لوجب أن نراه الآن ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك وجب أن يقال : إنما لا نراه الآن لأنه لا تصح رؤيته.
الشبهة الثانية : - وهي شبهة المقابلة - وهي أن كل ما كان مرئيا، وجب أن يكون مقابلا للرائي أو في حكم المقابل له. وذلك لا يصح إلا في الشيء الذي يكون حاصلا في الحيز والمكان والله تعالى ليس في المكان والحيز، فامتنع كونه مقابلا للرائي أو في حكم المقابل له فامتنع كونه مرئيا، وإنما قلنا : إن المرئي يجب أن يكون مقابلا أو في حكم المقابل احترازا عن صور ثلاث : أحدها: أنا نرى الأعراض مقابلة للجسم، إلا أنها حالة في الأجسام المقابلة
As for the rational doubts, they are also four: The first doubt—and it is the doubt of obstacles—and before its formulation, a preamble is necessary. And that is that the things due to which sight (ibsar) necessarily occurs in the witnessed world when they are present are eight: First: soundness of the sense; second: the thing being such that it is possible to be seen; third: that it not be at extreme distance; fourth: that it not be at extreme proximity; fifth: that it be facing the seer or in the ruling of facing; sixth: that it not be at extreme subtlety; seventh: that there be no veil between the seer and the seen; eighth: that it not be at extreme smallness. They said: When these eight matters are present, the occurrence of sight is necessary. For if it were not necessary, it would be possible that high mountains, bright suns, and loud sounds exist in our presence and we neither see them nor hear them, and that would lead to an person entering into ignorances. When you have known this preamble, let us return to the formulation of the doubt, and we say: As for the last six conditions, they can only be considered in the vision of bodies. And Allah the Most High is not a body, so it is not possible to consider these conditions in seeing Him. Thus, if His vision were possible, then for its occurrence only two matters would be necessary: soundness of the sense and His being such that it is valid to see Him. And these two matters are present now. So it is established that if His vision were possible, it would be necessary that we see Him now. And since it is not so, it must be said: He is not seen now only because His vision is not valid. The second doubt—and it is the doubt of facing—and it is that everything that is visible must be facing the seer or in the ruling of facing. And that is only valid for something that is located in space and place. And Allah the Most High is not in place or space, so it is impossible for Him to be facing the seer or in the ruling of facing, so it is impossible for Him to be visible. And we only said that the visible must be facing or in the ruling of facing as an exception for three images: First: we see accidents facing the body, except that they inhere in facing bodies.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents the first two rational (aqli) doubts of the Mu'tazila against the vision of Allah. The author structures them with a preamble first. The doubt of obstacles begins by listing eight necessary conditions for sight in the empirical world: sound sense organ, the object being visible, not too far, not too close, facing or in the ruling of facing, not too subtle, no veil, not too small. They argue that when all eight are present, sight must occur; otherwise, absurdities would follow (e.g., not seeing large objects). Then they apply this to God: the last six conditions apply only to bodies, but God is not a body, so only the first two conditions (sound sense and the object being visible) remain. Since these two are present now (people have sound eyes and God is theoretically visible if possible), we should see God now if ru'ya were possible. But we do not, so ru'ya is impossible. The second doubt is the doubt of facing (muqabala). It argues that any visible object must face the seer or be in the ruling of facing, which requires being located in space. God is not spatial, so He cannot face us, hence cannot be seen. The author adds an exception: accidents are seen as facing because they inhere in facing bodies; the text cuts off here. This passage is purely expositional—the author is presenting the Mu'tazili arguments before refuting them.

Key Terms
شبهة عقلية
موانع
إبصار
مقابلة
حيز
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the conditions for sight are all physical; the Mu'tazila use them to argue that a non-physical being cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not claim that physical conditions directly apply to God; they argue that the last six conditions only apply to bodies, and since God is not a body, only the first two matter. Their conclusion is based on the absence of vision despite those two conditions being met.

Checkpoint
According to the doubt of obstacles, why must we see God now if ru'ya were possible?
A) Because all eight conditions are present.
B) Because the last six conditions are irrelevant for a non-body, and the first two are currently satisfied.
C) Because God has promised to be seen.
D) Because the Mu'tazila have a command to see God.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first two rational doubts against ru'ya rely on empirical conditions for sight and the necessity of spatial facing, both of which they claim cannot apply to a non‑corporeal God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that if God could be seen, you would see Him now. Do you ever secretly measure God's reality by what you can perceive or experience directly? What would it mean to trust that you will see Him on the Day of Judgment even though you do not see Him now? Are you living as if that meeting is certain?
?
You rely on your senses and logic every day. But when it comes to God, are you willing to admit that your empirical expectations are not the measure of His being? What would change in your prayer or worship if you truly believed that God sees you, even though you cannot see Him?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot be seen because He does not occupy space. Do you ever limit your understanding of God to what fits your mental categories? This lesson confronts you with the fact that God transcends your reason. Will you accept that, or will you, like the Mu'tazila, demand that God fit into your logical boxes?

The Doubt of Impediments: Eight Conditions for Sight
Razi presents the first rational doubt against ru'ya with eight conditions for sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational doubt against the vision of Allah (ru'ya) and how the argument from impediments seeks to prove its impossibility.

Core Idea
Eight worldly conditions for sight become an argument against seeing Allah.

Razi begins the rational doubts against ru'ya with the strongest one: the doubt of impediments. Before presenting it, he lists the eight conditions that make sight possible in this world. Understanding these conditions is key to seeing how the doubt works.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction carefully
Read the Arabic passage first, then the translation. Notice the list of eight conditions. These are the preconditions for sight in the physical world.
2
Understand the logical necessity
The doubt argues that if these eight conditions are met, sight must occur; otherwise absurdities follow. Reflect on why this is a strong analogy.
The doubt relies on the principle that if conditions are sufficient, the effect must follow.
3
Apply to Allah
Notice how the last six conditions only apply to bodies. Since Allah is not a body, only two remain. These two are present now, so if vision were possible, we would see Allah now. We don't, so vision is impossible.
This is the core of the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبه العقلية: فهي أيضا أربع: الشبهة الأولى - وهي شبهة الموانع - وقبل تقريرها لا بد من مقدمة. وهي أن الأشياء التي يجب حصول الإبصار في الشاهد عند حصولها، ثمانية: أحدها: سلامة الحاسة وثانيها: كون الشيء بحيث أن يكون جائز الرؤية. وثالثها: أن لا يكون في غاية البعد والرابع أن لا يكون في غاية القرب. والخامس: أن يكون مقابلا للرائي أو في حكم المقابل والسادس: أن لا يكون في غاية اللطافة والسابع: أن لا يكون بين الرائي والمرئي حجاب. والثامن: أن لا يكون في غاية الصغر. قالوا: عند حصول هذه الأمور الثمانية، يجب حصول الإبصار. إذ لو لم يجب لجاز أن يحصل بحضرتنا جبال عالية وشموس مضيئة وأصوات هائلة، ونحن لا نراها ولا نسمعها وذلك يقتضي دخول الإنسان في الجهالات. إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فلنرجع إلى تقرير الشبهة، ونقول: أما الشرائط الستة الأخيرة فلا يمكن اعتبارها إلا في رؤية الأجسام. والله تعالى ليس بجسم، فلا يمكن اعتبار هذه الشرائط في رؤيته. فعلى هذا لو صحت رؤيته لوجب أن لا يصير في حصول رؤيته إلا أمران: سلامة الحاسة وكونه بحيث يصح أن يرى. وهذان الأمران حاصلان الآن. فثبت أنه لو صحت رؤيته، لوجب أن نراه الآن ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك وجب أن يقال: إنما لا نراه الآن لأنه لا تصح رؤيته.
As for the rational doubts, they are also four: The first doubt - and it is the doubt of impediments - and before presenting it, an introduction is necessary. And it is that the things which necessitate the occurrence of sight in the witnessed (world) when they occur are eight: First: soundness of the sense faculty; Second: the thing being such that it is possible to see; Third: that it not be extremely distant; Fourth: that it not be extremely close; Fifth: that it be opposite the seer or in the ruling of being opposite; Sixth: that it not be extremely subtle; Seventh: that there be no veil between the seer and the seen; Eighth: that it not be extremely small. They said: When these eight matters are present, the occurrence of sight is necessary. For if it were not necessary, it would be possible that high mountains, bright suns, and tremendous sounds exist in our presence and we do not see or hear them, and that would necessitate man falling into ignorance. Once you know this introduction, let us return to presenting the doubt, and we say: As for the six latter conditions, they can only be considered with respect to seeing bodies. Allah the Exalted is not a body, so these conditions cannot be considered with respect to seeing Him. So, if His vision were valid, then only two matters would be required for its occurrence: soundness of the sense faculty and it being such that it is possible to see. And these two matters are present now. So it is established that if His vision were valid, it would be necessary that we see Him now. And since it is not the case, it must be said that we do not see Him now because His vision is not possible.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational doubt against ru'ya, called the doubt of impediments (shubha al-mawani'). It begins with a necessary introduction listing eight conditions for sight in the physical world: sound sense faculty, the object being visible, not too distant, not too close, being opposite or in the ruling of being opposite, not too subtle, no veil, not too small. The doubt argues that if these eight conditions do not necessitate sight, absurdities would follow (e.g., large objects present but unseen). Then, it applies this to Allah: the last six conditions only apply to bodies, so for a non-corporeal God, only the first two conditions are relevant. Since those two (a sound sense faculty and the possibility of being seen) are present now, if Allah were visible, we would see Him now. We do not, therefore ru'ya is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this doubt proves that God cannot be seen in the afterlife.
Correction: The doubt only argues against vision in this world; later answers will show that the conditions of worldly sight may not apply to the hereafter.

Key Takeaway
The doubt of impediments argues that since we don't see Allah now, and the necessary conditions for sight are present, vision of Allah in this world is impossible.
