The Impossible Timing of Preference
Razi argues that commanding future preference is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why commanding a preference in a future time leads to an obligation of the unbearable and how this argument refutes the claim of just obligation at the time of equalization.

Core Idea
A command conditioned on a future impossible moment is itself impossible.

Imam Razi presents a subtle logical argument against the idea that Allah can obligate a person to choose at a future moment while currently the person has no preference. The argument hinges on the impossibility of a condition that does not yet exist. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'rajhan', 'al-zaman al-thani', 'shart muhal'.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Trace the logical structure
Break down the argument: The command to cause preference in the second time is conditioned on the arrival of that second time. But at the time of command (first time), the second time's arrival is impossible. Therefore, the command depends on an impossible condition, making the command itself impossible.
3
Connect to the concept of 'taklif ma la yutaq'
Since the command is logically impossible, it would be an obligation of the unbearable. Understand why this is a problem for the opponent's position.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm your grasp of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية والعشرون
لكن على هذا التقدير يتوجه تكليف ما لا يطاق لأن إيقاع الرجحان في الزمان الثاني، مشروط بحصول الزمان الثاني. وحصول الزمان الثاني في الزمان الأول محال، فكان إيقاع الترجيح في الزمان الثاني عند حصول الزمان الأول، موقوفا على شرط محال والموقوف على المحال محال، فقول القائل إنه كلف حال الاستواء بأن يوقع الترجيح في الزمان الثاني يكون تكليفا بما لا يطاق.
The twenty-second question.
But on this estimation, the obligation of what is unbearable is directed, because the occurrence of preference in the second time is conditioned upon the arrival of the second time. And the arrival of the second time in the first time is impossible. So the occurrence of preference in the second time at the time of arrival of the first time is dependent on an impossible condition. And what is dependent on the impossible is impossible. So the statement of the one who says that he obligated at the time of equalization to cause preference in the second time is an obligation of what is unbearable.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is the fourth argument within the twenty-second question, refuting the claim that a just obligation can be made at a time of equal preference. The author argues that if a command is given to create a preference in a future moment, that command is conditioned upon that future moment arriving. But at the time of the command (the first moment), the future moment has not yet arrived; its arrival is impossible in that first moment. Therefore, the command is dependent on an impossible condition, and anything so dependent is itself impossible. Hence, such a command would be an obligation of what is unbearable (تكليف ما لا يطاق).

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
الرجحان
الزمان الثاني
شرط محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument questions God's ability to command.
Correction: The argument is about logical impossibility, not divine power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is commanding a preference in the second time an impossible obligation?
A) Because the second time never arrives.
B) Because the act requires divine intervention which is absent.
C) Because the condition for the command (the second time) is impossible in the first time.
D) Because the person cannot perform the act.

Key Takeaway
The command to cause a preference in a future time is logically impossible because the condition for that command (the future time) does not exist at the time of command, making it an obligation of the unbearable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you put off a difficult duty by promising yourself you will do it later, are you not implicitly assuming that the future moment's arrival will make it possible? Razi's argument shows that such a conditional promise is logically flawed. Where in your life are you using this flawed reasoning to avoid present accountability? What would change if you stopped deferring to a future that does not yet exist?
?
This lesson uncovers the impossibility of commanding a preference when the condition for that command cannot be met now. Consider a duty you feel you cannot fulfill because you lack the will. Are you rationalizing avoidance by claiming you will have the motivation later? How does Razi's logical critique expose the dishonesty in that stance, and what can you do today to actually fulfill that duty?
?
Razi says that what depends on the impossible is impossible. Are there any actions you are planning to take that depend on conditions that are not yet true—and may never be true? For example, waiting for the perfect time to repent, or for your heart to become pure before you change a habit. What would it mean for you to stop using 'the right time' as an excuse and act now?

Why Concepts Cannot Be Acquired
Razi argues that concepts are unacquired, using the paradox of seeking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that some concepts (tasawwurat) must be unacquired, based on the impossibility of seeking something already known or unknown.

Core Idea
Concepts cannot be acquired because acquisition requires prior awareness, creating a paradox.

Imam Razi opens his fifth argument by tackling the nature of concepts. He posits that if we try to 'acquire' a concept, we either already have it or cannot seek it. This foundational point underlies the entire argument about unacquired knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on concepts
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Razi argues that some concepts are unacquired. Note the two scenarios: having awareness or lacking it.
2
Analyze the objection and reply
Consider the hypothetical objection that something is known from one aspect but not another. See how Razi refutes it by distinguishing the aspects.
This strengthens the case against acquisition.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Understand that if concepts are unacquired, they form the basis for the next step about axiomatic judgments.
This lays the foundation for the entire fifth argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن نقول إن شيئا من التصورات غير مكتسب، فشيء من التصديقات البديهية غير مكتسب، فشيء من التصديقات غير مكتسب مع أنه ورد الأمر بتحصيل المعرفة فكان هذا تكليفا بما لا يطاق وإنما قلنا إن شيئا من التصورات غير مكتسب، لأن من طلب اكتسابه فإما أن يكون له شعور بتلك الماهية وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان له شعور بتلك الماهية كان تصورها حاصلا له، وطلب الحاصل محال، وإن لم يكن له شعور بتلك الماهية استحال طلبها؛ لأن الغافل عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون طالبا له.
فإن قلت : إنه مشعور به من وجه دون وجه :قلت الوجه المشعور به مغایر للوجه الغير مشعور به فالمشعور به امتنع طلبه، لكونه حاصلا، وغير المشعور به، امتنع طلبه، لكونه مغفولا عنه وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كانت التصورات غير مكتسبة، كانت التصديقات البديهية غير مكتسبة وذلك أن التصديقات البديهية هي التي يكون مجرد طرفي موضوعها ومحمولها كافيًا في جزم الذهن بإثبات أحدهما للآخر أو سلبه عنه.
وعلى هذا التقدير إن حضرت التصورات كان ذلك التصديق واجب الحصول وإن لم تحضر كان ذلك التصديق ممتنع الحصول. وإذا كان هذا التصديق واجب الحصول، لزم الدوران، نفيا وإثباتا عند حضور تلك التصورات نفيا وإثباتا. وثبت أن حضور تلك التصورات نفيا وإثباتا ليس باختيار الإنسان فلزم أن يكون حصول هذه التصديقات نفيا وإثباتا ليس باختيار الإنسان فثبت أن هذه التصديقات البديهية، ليس شيء منها مكتسبا.
وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع أن يكون شيء من التصديقات مكتسبا.
وذلك لأن التصديقات المكتسبة لا تتسلسل ولا تدور بل لابد من انتهائها إلى المكتسب الأول، فيكون التصور المكتسب الأول هو تلك البديهيات لا محالة، فتلك البديهيات إما أن تكون تامة في استلزام المكتسب الأول، وإما أن لا تكون فإن كانت البديهيات تامة، لزم من حصولها حصول المكتسب الأول، ويلزم من عدمها امتناع حصول المكتسب الأول، لأنه لا سبب للمكتسب الأول، إلا تلك البديهيات وحينئذ يكون المكتسب الأول واجب الدوران نفيا وإثباتا مع ما لا يكون باختياره، لا نفيا ولا إثباتا . وحينئذ يخرج المكتسب الأول عن أن يكون باختياره.
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : حال المكتسب الثاني بالنسبة إلى المكتسب الأول - كما ذكرناه - وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون شيء من العلوم مكتسبا. وأما إن قلنا بأن تلك البديهيات غير تامة في استلزام المكتسب فحينئذ لابد مع تلك البديهيات من شيء من المكتسبات فحينئذ يكون ذلك المكتسب مقدما على أول المكتسبات . وذلك محال فثبت ما ذكرنا أن جميع العلوم، إما بديهية، وإما أن تكون من لوازم البديهيات وشيء منها ليس بمكتسب، فإذن شيء من المعلوم غير مكتسب ولا شك في ورود الأمر بها، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] فثبت أن تكليف ما لا يطاق لازم على الكل.
The fifth argument: That we say: Indeed, some concepts are unacquired, so some axiomatic judgments are unacquired, so some judgments are unacquired despite the command to acquire knowledge, so this is an obligation of what is unbearable. And we only said that some concepts are unacquired because whoever seeks to acquire it, either he has awareness of that essence or he does not. If he has awareness of that essence, then its conception is already present for him, and seeking what is present is impossible. And if he has no awareness of that essence, then seeking it is impossible, because one who is heedless of something cannot be a seeker of it. If you say: It is known in one respect but not another. I say: The respect that is known is different from the respect that is unknown, so seeking the known is impossible because it is already present, and seeking the unknown is impossible because it is overlooked. And we only said: Since concepts are unacquired, axiomatic judgments are unacquired. That is because axioms are those judgments where the mere two terms of subject and predicate are sufficient for the mind to decisively affirm one for the other or negate it from the other. And on this estimation, if the concepts are present, that judgment is necessary of attainment, and if they are absent, that judgment is impossible of attainment. And when this judgment is necessary of attainment, it implies circularity, negatively and positively, with the presence of those concepts negatively and positively. And it is established that the presence of those concepts negatively and positively is not under human choice, so it follows that the attainment of these judgments negatively and positively is not under human choice. So it is established that none of these axioms are acquired. And we only said: Since it is like this, it is impossible that any judgment is acquired. That is because acquired judgments do not regress infinitely nor are circular, but must terminate at the first acquired [judgment]. That first acquired [judgment] would necessarily be those axioms. So those axioms either are sufficient in entailing the first acquired or are not. If the axioms are sufficient, then from their attainment follows the attainment of the first acquired, and from their absence follows the impossibility of the first acquired, because there is no cause for the first acquired except those axioms. Then the first acquired becomes necessarily correlated negatively and positively with something not under its choice, neither negatively nor positively. Then the first acquired ceases to be under its choice. And if you understand this, we say: The state of the second acquired relative to the first acquired is as we mentioned. On this estimation, none of the sciences are acquired. And if we say that those axioms are insufficient in entailing the acquired, then along with those axioms, something from the acquired is needed, and then that acquired would be prior to the first of the acquired, and that is impossible. So what we mentioned is established: that all sciences are either axioms or necessary consequences of axioms, and none of them is acquired. So indeed, some knowledge is unacquired, and there is no doubt that the command to acquire it came, by His statement: 'So know that there is no god but Allah' (Muhammad 47:19). So it is established that the obligation of what is unbearable is necessary for all.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the impossibility of obligatory knowledge acquisition. The author argues that some concepts must be unacquired to avoid infinite regress. He then extends this to axioms (تصديقات بديهية), which depend on those concepts, so they too are unacquired. Since commands like 'So know that there is no god but Allah' require acquiring knowledge that is not under our control, this constitutes an obligation of what is unbearable. The argument proceeds logically: First, concepts cannot be acquired because acquisition requires prior awareness; if one already has awareness, the concept is already present; if not, it cannot be sought. Then axioms are defined as judgments requiring only the concepts of subject and predicate; if those concepts are present, the judgment is necessary; if absent, impossible. Since the presence of concepts is not under human choice, the axioms are not chosen. Then acquired judgments must terminate in a first acquired, which would be these axioms. If axioms are sufficient, the first acquired is necessarily correlated with them, thus not chosen. If they are insufficient, another acquired judgment must precede the first, causing regress. Hence, all knowledge is either axiomatic or a consequence of axioms, none truly acquired. The inclusion of the Qur'anic command proves that such an obligation is indeed imposed, but it is impossible, thus affirming the Ash'ari stance on taklif ma la yutaq.

Key Terms
التصورات
مكتسب
شعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge, including concepts, can be acquired through demonstrative reasoning.
Correction: Razi shows that concepts cannot be acquired because acquisition requires prior awareness, leading to a logical dead end.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is it impossible to seek a concept when one has no awareness of it?
A) Because the concept is too complex.
B) Because one cannot seek what one is unaware of.
C) Because it is a divine mystery.
D) Because it requires prior knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Concepts must be unacquired because seeking them requires prior awareness, leading to a logical dead end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you once worked hard to 'acquire'—like justice, love, or knowledge itself. Did you realize, looking back, that you already had some awareness of it before your study? How does Razi's argument challenge the way you view your own learning journey?
?
In your pursuit of truth, have you ever found yourself seeking something you were completely unaware of? How did you even know to seek it? What does that say about the source of your inquiry?
?
If core concepts cannot be acquired, then your most fundamental beliefs must come from somewhere else. Are you willing to examine whether your deepest convictions rest on unacquired principles, or have you assumed they were learned? Spend time today identifying one unacquired concept that shapes your worldview.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Axiomatic Judgments Are Not Acquired
Razi argues that axioms are unacquired, dependent on concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi extends the unacquiredness of concepts to axiomatic judgments (tasdiqat badihiyya), based on the dependency of such judgments on concepts.

Core Idea
Axioms are not acquired because their attainment depends solely on concepts, which are beyond our choice.

Having established that concepts are unacquired, Razi now turns to axiomatic judgments. He defines axioms and shows that their truth is forced when the relevant concepts are present. Since the presence of concepts is not under our control, the judgments are not acquired either.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of axioms
Razi defines axioms as judgments where the mere concepts of subject and predicate are sufficient for the mind to affirm or deny. Understand this definition.
This is a standard definition in Islamic logic.
2
Trace the dependency
Note that if the concepts are present, the judgment is necessary; if absent, impossible. Since the presence of concepts is not under human choice, the judgment is not chosen either.
This connects to the earlier argument.
3
Consider the implication
Realize that this means axioms cannot be acquired by effort; they are either forced or absent.
This sets up the next step about acquired judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن نقول إن شيئا من التصورات غير مكتسب، فشيء من التصديقات البديهية غير مكتسب، فشيء من التصديقات غير مكتسب مع أنه ورد الأمر بتحصيل المعرفة فكان هذا تكليفا بما لا يطاق وإنما قلنا إن شيئا من التصورات غير مكتسب، لأن من طلب اكتسابه فإما أن يكون له شعور بتلك الماهية وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان له شعور بتلك الماهية كان تصورها حاصلا له، وطلب الحاصل محال، وإن لم يكن له شعور بتلك الماهية استحال طلبها؛ لأن الغافل عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون طالبا له.
فإن قلت : إنه مشعور به من وجه دون وجه :قلت الوجه المشعور به مغایر للوجه الغير مشعور به فالمشعور به امتنع طلبه، لكونه حاصلا، وغير المشعور به، امتنع طلبه، لكونه مغفولا عنه وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كانت التصورات غير مكتسبة، كانت التصديقات البديهية غير مكتسبة وذلك أن التصديقات البديهية هي التي يكون مجرد طرفي موضوعها ومحمولها كافيًا في جزم الذهن بإثبات أحدهما للآخر أو سلبه عنه.
وعلى هذا التقدير إن حضرت التصورات كان ذلك التصديق واجب الحصول وإن لم تحضر كان ذلك التصديق ممتنع الحصول. وإذا كان هذا التصديق واجب الحصول، لزم الدوران، نفيا وإثباتا عند حضور تلك التصورات نفيا وإثباتا. وثبت أن حضور تلك التصورات نفيا وإثباتا ليس باختيار الإنسان فلزم أن يكون حصول هذه التصديقات نفيا وإثباتا ليس باختيار الإنسان فثبت أن هذه التصديقات البديهية، ليس شيء منها مكتسبا.
وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع أن يكون شيء من التصديقات مكتسبا.
وذلك لأن التصديقات المكتسبة لا تتسلسل ولا تدور بل لابد من انتهائها إلى المكتسب الأول، فيكون التصور المكتسب الأول هو تلك البديهيات لا محالة، فتلك البديهيات إما أن تكون تامة في استلزام المكتسب الأول، وإما أن لا تكون فإن كانت البديهيات تامة، لزم من حصولها حصول المكتسب الأول، ويلزم من عدمها امتناع حصول المكتسب الأول، لأنه لا سبب للمكتسب الأول، إلا تلك البديهيات وحينئذ يكون المكتسب الأول واجب الدوران نفيا وإثباتا مع ما لا يكون باختياره، لا نفيا ولا إثباتا . وحينئذ يخرج المكتسب الأول عن أن يكون باختياره.
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : حال المكتسب الثاني بالنسبة إلى المكتسب الأول - كما ذكرناه - وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون شيء من العلوم مكتسبا. وأما إن قلنا بأن تلك البديهيات غير تامة في استلزام المكتسب فحينئذ لابد مع تلك البديهيات من شيء من المكتسبات فحينئذ يكون ذلك المكتسب مقدما على أول المكتسبات . وذلك محال فثبت ما ذكرنا أن جميع العلوم، إما بديهية، وإما أن تكون من لوازم البديهيات وشيء منها ليس بمكتسب، فإذن شيء من المعلوم غير مكتسب ولا شك في ورود الأمر بها، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] فثبت أن تكليف ما لا يطاق لازم على الكل.
The fifth argument: That we say: Indeed, some concepts are unacquired, so some axiomatic judgments are unacquired, so some judgments are unacquired despite the command to acquire knowledge, so this is an obligation of what is unbearable. And we only said that some concepts are unacquired because whoever seeks to acquire it, either he has awareness of that essence or he does not. If he has awareness of that essence, then its conception is already present for him, and seeking what is present is impossible. And if he has no awareness of that essence, then seeking it is impossible, because one who is heedless of something cannot be a seeker of it. If you say: It is known in one respect but not another. I say: The respect that is known is different from the respect that is unknown, so seeking the known is impossible because it is already present, and seeking the unknown is impossible because it is overlooked. And we only said: Since concepts are unacquired, axiomatic judgments are unacquired. That is because axioms are those judgments where the mere two terms of subject and predicate are sufficient for the mind to decisively affirm one for the other or negate it from the other. And on this estimation, if the concepts are present, that judgment is necessary of attainment, and if they are absent, that judgment is impossible of attainment. And when this judgment is necessary of attainment, it implies circularity, negatively and positively, with the presence of those concepts negatively and positively. And it is established that the presence of those concepts negatively and positively is not under human choice, so it follows that the attainment of these judgments negatively and positively is not under human choice. So it is established that none of these axioms are acquired. And we only said: Since it is like this, it is impossible that any judgment is acquired. That is because acquired judgments do not regress infinitely nor are circular, but must terminate at the first acquired [judgment]. That first acquired [judgment] would necessarily be those axioms. So those axioms either are sufficient in entailing the first acquired or are not. If the axioms are sufficient, then from their attainment follows the attainment of the first acquired, and from their absence follows the impossibility of the first acquired, because there is no cause for the first acquired except those axioms. Then the first acquired becomes necessarily correlated negatively and positively with something not under its choice, neither negatively nor positively. Then the first acquired ceases to be under its choice. And if you understand this, we say: The state of the second acquired relative to the first acquired is as we mentioned. On this estimation, none of the sciences are acquired. And if we say that those axioms are insufficient in entailing the acquired, then along with those axioms, something from the acquired is needed, and then that acquired would be prior to the first of the acquired, and that is impossible. So what we mentioned is established: that all sciences are either axioms or necessary consequences of axioms, and none of them is acquired. So indeed, some knowledge is unacquired, and there is no doubt that the command to acquire it came, by His statement: 'So know that there is no god but Allah' (Muhammad 47:19). So it is established that the obligation of what is unbearable is necessary for all.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the impossibility of obligatory knowledge acquisition. The author argues that some concepts must be unacquired to avoid infinite regress. He then extends this to axioms (تصديقات بديهية), which depend on those concepts, so they too are unacquired. Since commands like 'So know that there is no god but Allah' require acquiring knowledge that is not under our control, this constitutes an obligation of what is unbearable. The argument proceeds logically: First, concepts cannot be acquired because acquisition requires prior awareness; if one already has awareness, the concept is already present; if not, it cannot be sought. Then axioms are defined as judgments requiring only the concepts of subject and predicate; if those concepts are present, the judgment is necessary; if absent, impossible. Since the presence of concepts is not under human choice, the axioms are not chosen. Then acquired judgments must terminate in a first acquired, which would be these axioms. If axioms are sufficient, the first acquired is necessarily correlated with them, thus not chosen. If they are insufficient, another acquired judgment must precede the first, causing regress. Hence, all knowledge is either axiomatic or a consequence of axioms, none truly acquired. The inclusion of the Qur'anic command proves that such an obligation is indeed imposed, but it is impossible, thus affirming the Ash'ari stance on taklif ma la yutaq.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Axioms are acquired through learning and reasoning.
Correction: Razi shows that axioms are not acquired because they are forced by the mere presence of concepts, independent of choice.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say makes an axiomatic judgment necessary?
A) The involvement of reason.
B) The presence of the concepts of subject and predicate.
C) The command of the Quran.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Axiomatic judgments are unacquired because their truth is entailed by the mere presence of their conceptual terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often pride yourself on your intelligence or your ability to reason things out. But consider this: the very most basic certainties that your mind holds—like the impossibility of a thing being both true and false at the same time—are not earned by your effort. They are forced upon you by the mere presence of the concepts. Does this realization humble you? Do you see how your intellect operates within boundaries set not by you, but by the Creator? How should this change your attitude towards learning and seeking knowledge?
?
Razi states that the presence of these concepts is not under human choice, and therefore the axiomatic judgments that follow are also not under choice. When you feel certain of something obvious, do you ever thank Allah for granting you that certainty? Or do you attribute it to your own 'common sense'? Is there a danger in taking these innate judgments for granted, and forgetting their Divine origin?
?
If axiomatic judgments are unacquired and necessary, then they form the foundation of all your knowledge. What bedrock truths do you build your life upon? Are they based on these unavoidable axioms, or have you accepted some falsehoods as 'axiomatic' without realizing they are not truly self-evident? Examine your own fundamental beliefs: which ones are truly unacquired, and which have you absorbed from culture or desire? Will you now scrutinize them more carefully?

Rational Judgments
advanced
8 min
All Knowledge Is Ungained; The Command Is Unbearable
Razi concludes that all knowledge is unacquired, making divine commands impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi concludes that all knowledge is either axiomatic or a necessary consequence, none truly acquired, and how this makes divine commands to acquire knowledge an obligation of the unbearable (taklif ma la yutaq).

Core Idea
If all knowledge is unacquired, then the command 'So know' is impossible to fulfill by human effort.

In this concluding part of his argument, Razi tackles the nature of acquired judgments. He shows that the first acquired judgment must be an axiom or dependent on axioms, leading to the conclusion that nothing is truly acquired. The Qur'anic command to know thus becomes a command to do the impossible, affirming the Ash'ari position on taklif ma la yutaq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the regress argument
Razi argues that acquired judgments cannot regress infinitely or be circular; they must terminate in a first acquired, which is the axioms.
This is a foundational point in epistemology.
2
Examine the two scenarios
If axioms are sufficient, the first acquired is necessarily correlated with them and thus not chosen. If insufficient, another acquired must precede, causing a regress.
Both scenarios lead to the same conclusion: no knowledge is truly acquired.
3
Connect to the Qur'anic command
Razi cites the verse 'So know that there is no god but Allah' as a command that requires acquiring knowledge, but since knowledge is unacquired, this is taklif ma la yutaq.
This is the ultimate point of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن نقول إن شيئا من التصورات غير مكتسب، فشيء من التصديقات البديهية غير مكتسب، فشيء من التصديقات غير مكتسب مع أنه ورد الأمر بتحصيل المعرفة فكان هذا تكليفا بما لا يطاق وإنما قلنا إن شيئا من التصورات غير مكتسب، لأن من طلب اكتسابه فإما أن يكون له شعور بتلك الماهية وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان له شعور بتلك الماهية كان تصورها حاصلا له، وطلب الحاصل محال، وإن لم يكن له شعور بتلك الماهية استحال طلبها؛ لأن الغافل عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون طالبا له.
فإن قلت : إنه مشعور به من وجه دون وجه :قلت الوجه المشعور به مغایر للوجه الغير مشعور به فالمشعور به امتنع طلبه، لكونه حاصلا، وغير المشعور به، امتنع طلبه، لكونه مغفولا عنه وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كانت التصورات غير مكتسبة، كانت التصديقات البديهية غير مكتسبة وذلك أن التصديقات البديهية هي التي يكون مجرد طرفي موضوعها ومحمولها كافيًا في جزم الذهن بإثبات أحدهما للآخر أو سلبه عنه.
وعلى هذا التقدير إن حضرت التصورات كان ذلك التصديق واجب الحصول وإن لم تحضر كان ذلك التصديق ممتنع الحصول. وإذا كان هذا التصديق واجب الحصول، لزم الدوران، نفيا وإثباتا عند حضور تلك التصورات نفيا وإثباتا. وثبت أن حضور تلك التصورات نفيا وإثباتا ليس باختيار الإنسان فلزم أن يكون حصول هذه التصديقات نفيا وإثباتا ليس باختيار الإنسان فثبت أن هذه التصديقات البديهية، ليس شيء منها مكتسبا.
وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع أن يكون شيء من التصديقات مكتسبا.
وذلك لأن التصديقات المكتسبة لا تتسلسل ولا تدور بل لابد من انتهائها إلى المكتسب الأول، فيكون التصور المكتسب الأول هو تلك البديهيات لا محالة، فتلك البديهيات إما أن تكون تامة في استلزام المكتسب الأول، وإما أن لا تكون فإن كانت البديهيات تامة، لزم من حصولها حصول المكتسب الأول، ويلزم من عدمها امتناع حصول المكتسب الأول، لأنه لا سبب للمكتسب الأول، إلا تلك البديهيات وحينئذ يكون المكتسب الأول واجب الدوران نفيا وإثباتا مع ما لا يكون باختياره، لا نفيا ولا إثباتا . وحينئذ يخرج المكتسب الأول عن أن يكون باختياره.
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : حال المكتسب الثاني بالنسبة إلى المكتسب الأول - كما ذكرناه - وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون شيء من العلوم مكتسبا. وأما إن قلنا بأن تلك البديهيات غير تامة في استلزام المكتسب فحينئذ لابد مع تلك البديهيات من شيء من المكتسبات فحينئذ يكون ذلك المكتسب مقدما على أول المكتسبات . وذلك محال فثبت ما ذكرنا أن جميع العلوم، إما بديهية، وإما أن تكون من لوازم البديهيات وشيء منها ليس بمكتسب، فإذن شيء من المعلوم غير مكتسب ولا شك في ورود الأمر بها، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] فثبت أن تكليف ما لا يطاق لازم على الكل.
The fifth argument: That we say: Indeed, some concepts are unacquired, so some axiomatic judgments are unacquired, so some judgments are unacquired despite the command to acquire knowledge, so this is an obligation of what is unbearable. And we only said that some concepts are unacquired because whoever seeks to acquire it, either he has awareness of that essence or he does not. If he has awareness of that essence, then its conception is already present for him, and seeking what is present is impossible. And if he has no awareness of that essence, then seeking it is impossible, because one who is heedless of something cannot be a seeker of it. If you say: It is known in one respect but not another. I say: The respect that is known is different from the respect that is unknown, so seeking the known is impossible because it is already present, and seeking the unknown is impossible because it is overlooked. And we only said: Since concepts are unacquired, axiomatic judgments are unacquired. That is because axioms are those judgments where the mere two terms of subject and predicate are sufficient for the mind to decisively affirm one for the other or negate it from the other. And on this estimation, if the concepts are present, that judgment is necessary of attainment, and if they are absent, that judgment is impossible of attainment. And when this judgment is necessary of attainment, it implies circularity, negatively and positively, with the presence of those concepts negatively and positively. And it is established that the presence of those concepts negatively and positively is not under human choice, so it follows that the attainment of these judgments negatively and positively is not under human choice. So it is established that none of these axioms are acquired. And we only said: Since it is like this, it is impossible that any judgment is acquired. That is because acquired judgments do not regress infinitely nor are circular, but must terminate at the first acquired [judgment]. That first acquired [judgment] would necessarily be those axioms. So those axioms either are sufficient in entailing the first acquired or are not. If the axioms are sufficient, then from their attainment follows the attainment of the first acquired, and from their absence follows the impossibility of the first acquired, because there is no cause for the first acquired except those axioms. Then the first acquired becomes necessarily correlated negatively and positively with something not under its choice, neither negatively nor positively. Then the first acquired ceases to be under its choice. And if you understand this, we say: The state of the second acquired relative to the first acquired is as we mentioned. On this estimation, none of the sciences are acquired. And if we say that those axioms are insufficient in entailing the acquired, then along with those axioms, something from the acquired is needed, and then that acquired would be prior to the first of the acquired, and that is impossible. So what we mentioned is established: that all sciences are either axioms or necessary consequences of axioms, and none of them is acquired. So indeed, some knowledge is unacquired, and there is no doubt that the command to acquire it came, by His statement: 'So know that there is no god but Allah' (Muhammad 47:19). So it is established that the obligation of what is unbearable is necessary for all.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the impossibility of obligatory knowledge acquisition. The author argues that some concepts must be unacquired to avoid infinite regress. He then extends this to axioms (تصديقات بديهية), which depend on those concepts, so they too are unacquired. Since commands like 'So know that there is no god but Allah' require acquiring knowledge that is not under our control, this constitutes an obligation of what is unbearable. The argument proceeds logically: First, concepts cannot be acquired because acquisition requires prior awareness; if one already has awareness, the concept is already present; if not, it cannot be sought. Then axioms are defined as judgments requiring only the concepts of subject and predicate; if those concepts are present, the judgment is necessary; if absent, impossible. Since the presence of concepts is not under human choice, the axioms are not chosen. Then acquired judgments must terminate in a first acquired, which would be these axioms. If axioms are sufficient, the first acquired is necessarily correlated with them, thus not chosen. If they are insufficient, another acquired judgment must precede the first, causing regress. Hence, all knowledge is either axiomatic or a consequence of axioms, none truly acquired. The inclusion of the Qur'anic command proves that such an obligation is indeed imposed, but it is impossible, thus affirming the Ash'ari stance on taklif ma la yutaq.

التصديقات
الدوران
التسلسل
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some knowledge can be acquired through effort, and divine commands are easy to fulfill.
Correction: Razi argues that no knowledge is truly acquired; all is either axiomatic or necessary consequence, making the command to know an obligation of the unbearable.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude about the Qur'anic command 'So know that there is no god but Allah'?
A) It is easy to fulfill with effort.
B) It is an obligation of what is unbearable (taklif ma la yutaq).
C) It only applies to prophets.
D) It is metaphorical and not a real command.

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes that all knowledge is unacquired, so the divine command to acquire knowledge is inherently impossible, affirming taklif ma la yutaq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study or seek knowledge, do you ever feel a sense of pride in your own intellectual effort? How does Razi's argument—that all knowledge is ultimately unacquired and dependent on axioms not under your choice—challenge that feeling? Would you be willing to sincerely say, 'All my learning is solely by Allah's gift, and I have no claim to it'?
?
The verse 'So know that there is no god but Allah' is a divine command that Razi argues is impossible to fulfill by human effort. If knowledge of tawhid cannot be earned, then your own conviction in 'La ilaha illa Allah' is a pure gift. Does your heart truly acknowledge this with gratitude, or do you secretly believe that your faith is a product of your own reasoning and choice?
?
Razi's conclusion implies that some commands of Allah (like acquiring knowledge) are beyond human ability. If you have ever struggled to fulfill a religious duty—such as attaining certainty in faith—does this argument comfort you, or does it disturb your sense of moral responsibility? How will you reconcile the idea that God commands what you cannot do with your daily effort to obey Him?

The Second Doubt: One Thing Cannot Produce Two Different Effects
Imam al-Razi presents the second objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the principle of 'from one only one' and how Imam al-Razi refutes it by distinguishing between not producing A and producing not-A.

Core Idea
Not producing A is not the same as producing not-A—this distinction saves the principle.

Imam al-Razi continues his defense of the principle that from one thing only one thing can proceed. The second objection tries to show a contradiction in a single cause producing two effects. Al-Razi's answer hinges on a subtle but crucial logical distinction. Let's examine the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully.
Read the objection (the second doubt) in Arabic and translation. Notice how it sets up a paradox: if a single thing produces A, it cannot produce B, and vice versa.
The objection assumes that producing A and producing B are mutually exclusive in one respect.
2
Understand the distinction in the answer.
Al-Razi points out that the opposite of 'it produced A' is simply 'it did not produce A', not 'it produced what is not A'. This is a logical move that blocks the alleged contradiction.
Think of this as: the negation of an action is not the same as a positive opposite action.
3
Apply the analogy of the receiver.
Al-Razi uses the example of a single receiver that accepts both A and B. Just because it received A does not mean it cannot receive B. This shows the objection's reasoning is flawed when applied to the agent.
The receiver analogy mirrors the agent's case: receiving A does not prevent receiving B.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إذا صدر عن الشيء الواحد باعتبار ،واحد الألف والباء - والألف من ذلك لا شك أنه غير الباء - فمن حيث إنه صدر عنه الألف لم يصدر عنه الباء؛ لأنه الحيث صدر عنه الباء، والباء ليس بالألف. ومن حيث إنه صدر عنه الباء، لم يصدر عنه الألف. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن نقيض أنه صدر عنه الألف، هو أنه ما صدر عنه الألف لا أنه صدر عنه ما ليس بألف والفرق ظاهر بين قولك ما حصل بالألف وبين قولك حصل ما ليس بالألف . ولو صح ما ذكرتم لزم أن لا يقبل الواحد إلا الواحد، لأنه قبل الألف وقبل الباء والباء ليس بألف - فحين قبل الألف لم يقبل الباء، وذلك محال . ولما كان هذا الكلام باطلا في جانب القابل، فكذا في جانب الفاعل.
The second doubt: If two things, alif and ba', proceed from one thing in one respect - and alif is undoubtedly not ba' - then insofar as alif proceeds from it, ba' does not proceed from it, because it is insofar as ba' proceeds, and ba' is not alif. And insofar as ba' proceeds from it, alif does not proceed from it. And the answer to the second doubt: That the opposite of 'alif proceeded from it' is that alif did not proceed from it, not that what is not alif proceeded from it. And the difference between your saying 'alif did not obtain' and your saying 'what is not alif obtained' is clear. And if what you mentioned were correct, it would necessitate that the one cannot receive except the one, because it receives alif and receives ba', and ba' is not alif - so when it received alif, it did not receive ba', and that is impossible. And since this statement is invalid on the side of the receiver, so too on the side of the agent.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (shubah) against the principle that from one thing only one thing can proceed, followed by its refutation. The objector argues: if a single entity in one respect produces two distinct things, say A and B, then insofar as it produces A, it does not produce B, and vice versa. Thus the one would be producing and not producing simultaneously, which is contradictory. Al-Razi's answer distinguishes between 'not producing A' and 'producing not-A'. The opposite of 'it produced A' is simply 'it did not produce A', not 'it produced what is not A'. He illustrates this with the example of a receiver: if a single receiver receives A and then B, the fact that it receives A does not mean it cannot receive B, because receiving A does not imply rejecting B. The same applies to the agent: producing A does not preclude producing B, as the act of producing A is not the same as the act of producing not-B. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الشبهة
العلة
الفاعل
القابل
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that not producing A equals producing the opposite of A.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that the opposite of 'produced A' is simply 'did not produce A', not 'produced what is not A'. These are logically distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second doubt, what is the correct opposite of 'alif proceeded from it'?
A) 'What is not alif proceeded from it'
B) 'Alif did not proceed from it'
C) 'Both A and B are the same'
D) 'Nothing proceeded from it'

Key Takeaway
The second doubt fails because the opposite of an action is its mere absence, not a contrary action, and this applies equally to agents and receivers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you fail to perform an obligatory act, do you ever feel you have instead performed its opposite (e.g., not praying is equivalent to sinning)? How does al-Razi's distinction between 'did not do' and 'did the opposite' challenge that feeling, and what responsibility does it actually place on you?

?
If you know that God is the sole agent (al-fā‘il) and that His not creating a thing is simply its absence, not an act of creating a contrary evil, how does this affect your trust in His wisdom when you face hardship or lack?

The Point of Contention: Rational vs. Divine Moral Values
Imam Razi defines the point of contention on moral values.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise point of disagreement between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila regarding the basis of good and evil.

Core Idea
The dispute is not about knowing good and evil but about their source.

This lesson clarifies the exact issue in the debate over moral values. Both sides agree on basic moral intuitions—like knowledge being perfection—but disagree on what makes actions good or bad: intrinsic qualities or divine command. Imam Razi sets the stage by defining the terms of the debate before presenting his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the agreed point
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through the statement that knowledge is perfection. Notice what both sides accept.
This shared ground is important for understanding the real point of contention.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where Razi states the Mu'tazila position (attributes of actions) and the Ash'ari position (pure divine ruling). Mark these clearly.
The phrase 'مذهبنا : أنه مجرد حكم الشرع' is the Ash'ari stance.
3
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Consider the theological and ethical implications of each view. How does the source of moral value affect our understanding of divine justice?
This will prepare you for the proof that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والعشرون في أن الحسن والقبح يثبتان بالشرع وأيضا نعلم بعقولنا : أن العلم صفة كمال والجهل صفة نقص، وإنما النزاع في أن كون بعض الأفعال متعلق الذم في الدنيا والعقاب في الآخرة، وكون البعض الآخر متعلق المدح في الدنيا والثواب في الآخرة. هل هو لأجل صفة عائدة إلى الفعل، أو ليس الأمر كذلك، بل هو محض حكم أهل المعرفة به؟ قالت المعتزلة : المؤثر في هذه الأحكام صفات عائدة إلى الأفعال. ومذهبنا : أنه مجرد حكم الشرع. وإذ تنقح محل النزاع . فنقول : الذي يدل على قولنا وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية. وعلى التقديرين فالقول بالحسن والقبح العقليين باطل بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن صدور الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي . إما أن يكون واجبا أو لا. فإن كان واجبا كان فعل العبد اضطراريا، لأن حصول القدرة والداعي ليس بالعبد وإلا لزم التسلسل وإذا كان كذلك فعند حصولهما يكون الفعل واجبا، وعندما لا يكونان حاصلين كان الفعل ممتنعا فكان الاضطرار لازما لا محالة. وأما إن لم يكن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي واجبا، فأما أن يتوقف رجحان الفعل على الترك على مرجح أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف كان حصول ذلك الفعل عند حصول ذلك المرجح واجبا . وإلا عاد الكلام الأول ولزم التسلسل . وإذا كان واجبا عاد القول بأن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي اضطراري. وأما إن لم يتوقف رجحان الفعل على الترك على مرجح كان رجحان الفعل اتفاقيا، أنه اتفق حصول هذا الرجحان، لا لمؤثر أصلا، فلا يكون ذلك الرجحان من العبد . فثبت أن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن
The twenty-fifth issue: On that good and evil are established by divine law. And also we know by our intellects that knowledge is an attribute of perfection and ignorance an attribute of deficiency. But the dispute is: some actions are objects of blame in this world and punishment in the afterlife, and others are objects of praise in this world and reward in the afterlife. Is this due to an attribute belonging to the action, or is it not so, but rather a pure judgment of the one who knows it? The Mu'tazila said: The effective factor in these judgments is attributes belonging to actions. And our school: It is merely the ruling of divine law. When the point of contention is clarified, we say: That which indicates our position are [several] aspects. The first proof: Human actions are either necessary or accidental. On both suppositions, the claim of rational good and evil is false. Explanation of the first premise: The occurrence of an action when power and motivation are present — either it is necessary or not. If it is necessary, then the slave's action is necessary, because the presence of power and motivation is not from the slave, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. If that is so, then when they are present the action is necessary, and when they are not present the action is impossible, so necessity is unavoidable. And if the occurrence of the action when power and motivation are present is not necessary, then either the preference of the action over its omission depends on a preferred or not. If it depends, then the occurrence of that action when that preferred is present is necessary. Otherwise the first argument returns and an infinite regress follows. And if it is necessary, then the statement returns that the occurrence of the action when power and motivation are present is necessary. And if the preference of the action over its omission does not depend on a preferred, then the preference of the action is accidental: that this preference happened, without any influencing factor at all, so that preference is not from the slave. Thus it is established that human actions are either necessary or accidental. And if that is so, it follows that...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage opens the twenty-fifth issue, which argues that moral values of good and evil are established by divine law, not by reason independently. Imam Razi first clarifies the point of contention: everyone knows that knowledge is perfection and ignorance deficiency, but the real dispute concerns why certain actions are blameworthy and punishable, and others praiseworthy and rewardable. The Mu'tazila claim that these judgments stem from intrinsic attributes of the actions themselves. The Ash'ari position (our school) is that these judgments are purely the ruling of divine law. After delimiting the issue, Razi presents the first proof. The proof begins with a premise: human actions are either necessary (addirariyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). On either case, rational good and evil are false. The explanation of this premise is detailed: an action occurs when power (qudra) and motivation (da'i) are present. If the action's occurrence at that point is necessary, then the action is necessary because the presence of power and motivation is not under the slave's control (otherwise infinite regress). Thus, when they are present, the action must occur; when absent, it cannot occur — so the action is necessitated. If the occurrence is not necessary, then the preference of action over omission either depends on a preferred (murajjih) or not. If it depends, then when that preferred is present, the action becomes necessary again, reverting to necessity. If it does not depend on any preferred, then the preference is purely accidental, with no cause from the slave. Therefore, all human actions are either necessary or accidental. The argument aims to show that if actions are not freely chosen in a rational sense, then attributing intrinsic moral qualities to them is baseless; moral judgments must come from divine command.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
شرع
عقل

What do the Ash'aris hold about the basis of moral judgments (good and evil)?
A) They are based on rational attributes of actions.

Key Takeaway
The core disagreement is whether moral values are intrinsic to actions (Mu'tazila) or purely determined by divine command (Ash'aris).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If moral values are purely from divine command, how does that impact your daily decisions when divine commands are not explicit? Do you rely on your reason or on revelation? Examine a recent choice: was it based on your own judgment of good and evil, or on seeking God's command?
?
The Ash'ari position affirms that even though we know knowledge is good by reason, the ultimate moral judgment (praise/blame, reward/punishment) is from God. Does this make you feel more accountable to divine law, or do you find yourself judging actions by your own standards? Identify one area where you tend to follow your own sense of right and wrong instead of seeking God's ruling.
?
Imam Razi says the dispute is about why some actions are worthy of punishment and others reward. Reflect: Do you live as if your actions have intrinsic moral weight, or as if their value depends solely on God's command? How would your life change if you truly believed that every moral judgment is ultimately God's decree?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
8 min
The First Proof: Actions Are Necessary or Accidental
Imam Razi presents the first proof that human actions are either necessary or accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that human actions are either necessary or accidental, which undermines rational moral evaluation.

Core Idea
If all actions are either necessary or accidental, they cannot be morally judged by reason alone.

After clarifying the dispute, Razi launches his first argument. He aims to show that human actions are not freely chosen in a way that could support intrinsic moral qualities. Instead, actions are either necessitated by causes or happen by accident. This proof challenges the Mu'tazila claim that reason can independently determine good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's premise carefully
Read the Arabic from 'وإذ تنقح محل النزاع' to the end. Focus on the initial premise: 'إن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية'.
This is the foundation of the entire argument.
2
Follow the logic of necessity
Trace the reasoning: if an action occurs when power and motivation are present, it must be necessary; if not, it depends on a preferred, leading again to necessity. Understand the proof against free choice.
The key terms are 'قدرة' (power) and 'داعي' (motivation).
3
Understand the alternative: accidental
If no preferred is needed, the action is purely accidental—not from the slave. Conclude that all actions are either necessary or accidental, so no free action exists to bear rational moral attributes.
This directly refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والعشرون في أن الحسن والقبح يثبتان بالشرع وأيضا نعلم بعقولنا : أن العلم صفة كمال والجهل صفة نقص، وإنما النزاع في أن كون بعض الأفعال متعلق الذم في الدنيا والعقاب في الآخرة، وكون البعض الآخر متعلق المدح في الدنيا والثواب في الآخرة. هل هو لأجل صفة عائدة إلى الفعل، أو ليس الأمر كذلك، بل هو محض حكم أهل المعرفة به؟ قالت المعتزلة : المؤثر في هذه الأحكام صفات عائدة إلى الأفعال. ومذهبنا : أنه مجرد حكم الشرع. وإذ تنقح محل النزاع . فنقول : الذي يدل على قولنا وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية. وعلى التقديرين فالقول بالحسن والقبح العقليين باطل بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن صدور الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي . إما أن يكون واجبا أو لا. فإن كان واجبا كان فعل العبد اضطراريا، لأن حصول القدرة والداعي ليس بالعبد وإلا لزم التسلسل وإذا كان كذلك فعند حصولهما يكون الفعل واجبا، وعندما لا يكونان حاصلين كان الفعل ممتنعا فكان الاضطرار لازما لا محالة. وأما إن لم يكن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي واجبا، فأما أن يتوقف رجحان الفعل على الترك على مرجح أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف كان حصول ذلك الفعل عند حصول ذلك المرجح واجبا . وإلا عاد الكلام الأول ولزم التسلسل . وإذا كان واجبا عاد القول بأن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي اضطراري. وأما إن لم يتوقف رجحان الفعل على الترك على مرجح كان رجحان الفعل اتفاقيا، أنه اتفق حصول هذا الرجحان، لا لمؤثر أصلا، فلا يكون ذلك الرجحان من العبد . فثبت أن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن
The twenty-fifth issue: On that good and evil are established by divine law. And also we know by our intellects that knowledge is an attribute of perfection and ignorance an attribute of deficiency. But the dispute is: some actions are objects of blame in this world and punishment in the afterlife, and others are objects of praise in this world and reward in the afterlife. Is this due to an attribute belonging to the action, or is it not so, but rather a pure judgment of the one who knows it? The Mu'tazila said: The effective factor in these judgments is attributes belonging to actions. And our school: It is merely the ruling of divine law. When the point of contention is clarified, we say: That which indicates our position are [several] aspects. The first proof: Human actions are either necessary or accidental. On both suppositions, the claim of rational good and evil is false. Explanation of the first premise: The occurrence of an action when power and motivation are present — either it is necessary or not. If it is necessary, then the slave's action is necessary, because the presence of power and motivation is not from the slave, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. If that is so, then when they are present the action is necessary, and when they are not present the action is impossible, so necessity is unavoidable. And if the occurrence of the action when power and motivation are present is not necessary, then either the preference of the action over its omission depends on a preferred or not. If it depends, then the occurrence of that action when that preferred is present is necessary. Otherwise the first argument returns and an infinite regress follows. And if it is necessary, then the statement returns that the occurrence of the action when power and motivation are present is necessary. And if the preference of the action over its omission does not depend on a preferred, then the preference of the action is accidental: that this preference happened, without any influencing factor at all, so that preference is not from the slave. Thus it is established that human actions are either necessary or accidental. And if that is so, it follows that...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage opens the twenty-fifth issue, which argues that moral values of good and evil are established by divine law, not by reason independently. Imam Razi first clarifies the point of contention: everyone knows that knowledge is perfection and ignorance deficiency, but the real dispute concerns why certain actions are blameworthy and punishable, and others praiseworthy and rewardable. The Mu'tazila claim that these judgments stem from intrinsic attributes of the actions themselves. The Ash'ari position (our school) is that these judgments are purely the ruling of divine law. After delimiting the issue, Razi presents the first proof. The proof begins with a premise: human actions are either necessary (addirariyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). On either case, rational good and evil are false. The explanation of this premise is detailed: an action occurs when power (qudra) and motivation (da'i) are present. If the action's occurrence at that point is necessary, then the action is necessary because the presence of power and motivation is not under the slave's control (otherwise infinite regress). Thus, when they are present, the action must occur; when absent, it cannot occur — so the action is necessitated. If the occurrence is not necessary, then the preference of action over omission either depends on a preferred (murajjih) or not. If it depends, then when that preferred is present, the action becomes necessary again, reverting to necessity. If it does not depend on any preferred, then the preference is purely accidental, with no cause from the slave. Therefore, all human actions are either necessary or accidental. The argument aims to show that if actions are not freely chosen in a rational sense, then attributing intrinsic moral qualities to them is baseless; moral judgments must come from divine command.

Key Terms
اضطرار
اتفاق
قدرة
داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this proof denies all human agency, making God unjust for rewarding/punishing.
Correction: Razi's argument only denies that actions are independently free from divine power; moral responsibility comes from divine law, not from rational autonomy.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's proof, what are the two categories of human actions?
A) Voluntary and involuntary
B) Necessary and accidental
C) Good and evil

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof establishes that human actions are either necessitated or accidental, undermining the Mu'tazila claim that reason can independently identify good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you take is either compelled or accidental, where does that leave your guilt for sins you thought you chose? Are you ready to take full responsibility for them despite this proof?
?
Razi says reason cannot independently judge good and evil. When was the last time you trusted your own moral reasoning over clear revelation? What will you do differently now?
?
This proof removes any space for your own choice. Does that make you anxious about your accountability before Allah? How will you respond to that anxiety—by clinging harder to the Shari'ah, or by doubting the proof?
